p Block Elements Groups 15 to 18

    Group 15: dinitrogen, nitrogen compounds and nitric acid
  • Nitrogen gas liberation reactions
    • For nitrogen gas liberation, remember the standard reactions: \(\mathrm{NH_4^+ + NO_2^- \rightarrow N_2 + 2H_2O}\), \(\mathrm{CO(NH_2)_2 + 2HNO_2 \rightarrow CO_2 + 2N_2 + 3H_2O}\), and \(\mathrm{(NH_4)_2Cr_2O_7 \xrightarrow{\Delta} Cr_2O_3 + N_2 + 4H_2O}\).
    • Sodium nitrate on heating gives \(\mathrm{NaNO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{O_2}\), not \(\mathrm{N_2}\).
    • Metal azides are direct nitrogen-liberating compounds: \(\mathrm{Ba(N_3)_2 \xrightarrow{\Delta} Ba + 3N_2}\).
    • In contrast, do not assume every nitrogen-containing compound liberates nitrogen gas on heating.
  • Ammonium nitrate and magnetic-property shortcut
    • Ammonium nitrate decomposes as \(\mathrm{NH_4NO_3 \xrightarrow{\Delta} N_2O + 2H_2O}\).
    • In magnetic-property questions, \(\mathrm{N_2O}\) is treated as diamagnetic, while \(\mathrm{NO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{O_2}\) are paramagnetic.
  • Brown ring test for nitrate
    • In the brown ring test, nitrate ion is reduced by \(\mathrm{Fe^{2+}}\) in acidic medium to \(\mathrm{NO}\).
    • \(\mathrm{NO}\) forms the brown nitrosyl complex \(\mathrm{[Fe(H_2O)_5NO]^{2+}}\).
    • The nitrogen oxide formed in situ is \(\mathrm{NO}\), not \(\mathrm{NO_2}\).
  • Nitrogen and oxygen combination
    • Nitrogen and oxygen combine non-catalytically only at very high temperature, about \(2000\ \mathrm{K}\), to form nitric oxide.
    • The reason is the very high bond strength of \(\mathrm{N_2}\).
  • Nitric acid reactions with metals
    • Nitric acid gives different nitrogen oxides depending on the metal and acid concentration.
    • A high-yield pair is \(\mathrm{Cu + dil.\ HNO_3 \rightarrow NO}\), while \(\mathrm{Cu + conc.\ HNO_3 \rightarrow NO_2}\).
    • Zinc with nitric acid is another recurring pattern: \(\mathrm{Zn}\) with concentrated \(\mathrm{HNO_3}\) gives \(\mathrm{NO_2}\), while with dilute \(\mathrm{HNO_3}\) it can give \(\mathrm{N_2O}\).
    • Nitric acid usually gives nitrogen oxides with metals, not hydrogen gas.
  • Nitrite acidification and nitrogen oxides
    • Acidification of nitrite first gives unstable nitrous acid: \(\mathrm{NaNO_2 + HCl \rightarrow HNO_2 + NaCl}\).
    • The unstable \(\mathrm{HNO_2}\) can give \(\mathrm{NO}\) and \(\mathrm{NO_2}\), so sodium nitrite with hydrochloric acid gives these two nitrogen oxides.
  • Acidity of nitrogen oxides
    • The acidity of nitrogen oxides increases with increasing oxidation state of nitrogen: \(\mathrm{N_2O<NO<N_2O_3<N_2O_4<N_2O_5}\).
    • This is a direct trend question and should not be mixed with hydride or hydrogen-halide trends.
  • Nitric acid structure and bond angles
    • In nitric acid structure, the important bond angles are \(\angle\mathrm{H-O-N}\approx102^\circ\) and \(\angle\mathrm{O-N-O}\approx130^\circ\).
    • The angle at oxygen is smaller, while the angle around nitrogen is larger due to trigonal planar/resonance character.

    Group 15: phosphorus, phosphine and phosphorus halides
  • Phosphine preparation reactions
    • White phosphorus with alkali gives phosphine and sodium hypophosphite: \(\mathrm{P_4 + 3NaOH + 3H_2O \rightarrow PH_3 + 3NaH_2PO_2}\).
    • This reaction is repeatedly used to identify \(\mathrm{PH_3}\) or \(\mathrm{NaH_2PO_2}\) in reaction sequences.
    • Phosphine is also formed by hydrolysis of metal phosphides: \(\mathrm{Ca_3P_2 + 6H_2O \rightarrow 3Ca(OH)_2 + 2PH_3}\).
    • Phosphine is also formed by heating phosphorous acid: \(\mathrm{4H_3PO_3 \xrightarrow{\Delta} PH_3 + 3H_3PO_4}\).
    • Phosphonium salts with alkali also liberate \(\mathrm{PH_3}\).
  • Reaction that does not give phosphine
    • Hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) does not give phosphine; it gives phosphorous acid: \(\mathrm{PCl_3 + 3H_2O \rightarrow H_3PO_3 + 3HCl}\).
    • This is a common elimination point in “which reaction does not give \(\mathrm{PH_3}\)” questions.
  • Phosphine with copper sulphate
    • Phosphine reacts with copper sulphate to form copper phosphide: \(\mathrm{4PH_3 + 6CuSO_4 \rightarrow 2Cu_3P_2 + 6H_2SO_4}\).
    • Once \(X=\mathrm{PH_3}\) is identified, \(Y=\mathrm{Cu_3P_2}\) follows directly.
  • Phosphorus chlorides from white phosphorus
    • Thionyl chloride converts white phosphorus to \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\): \(\mathrm{P_4 + 8SOCl_2 \rightarrow 4PCl_3 + 4SO_2 + 2S_2Cl_2}\).
    • \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) is pyramidal because phosphorus has one lone pair.
    • Sulphuryl chloride acts as a chlorinating agent and converts phosphorus to \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\).
  • \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\): hydrolysis, solid state and gas-phase structure
    • Complete hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) gives orthophosphoric acid: \(\mathrm{PCl_5 + 4H_2O \rightarrow H_3PO_4 + 5HCl}\).
    • Partial hydrolysis may involve \(\mathrm{POCl_3}\), but complete hydrolysis ends at \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).
    • Solid \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) is ionic and exists as \(\mathrm{[PCl_4]^+[PCl_6]^-}\).
    • Gaseous \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) is trigonal bipyramidal with non-equivalent axial and equatorial bonds.
  • Nitrogen vs phosphorus chlorides
    • Nitrogen forms \(\mathrm{NCl_3}\) but not \(\mathrm{NCl_5}\), while phosphorus forms both \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) and \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\).
    • The deciding reason is that nitrogen is a second-period element and cannot expand its octet.
    • This is different from saying nitrogen cannot show \(+5\) oxidation state.

    Group 15: oxoacids of phosphorus
  • Basicity rule for phosphorus oxoacids
    • Basicity of phosphorus oxoacids is decided by the number of ionisable \(\mathrm{O-H}\) hydrogens, not by total hydrogen count.
    • \(\mathrm{P-H}\) hydrogens are not ionisable.
    • The high-yield basicity sequence is: \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) is monobasic, \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is dibasic, and \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) is tribasic.
    • Structurally, \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3=HP(=O)(OH)_2}\) and \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4=P(=O)(OH)_3}\).
  • Hypophosphorous acid and reducing character
    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) is hypophosphorous acid, not phosphonic acid.
    • It is monobasic, reducing, and has phosphorus in \(+1\) oxidation state.
    • Reducing character in phosphorus oxoacids depends on the presence of \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds.
    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\), with two \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds, is a strong reducing agent and can reduce \(\mathrm{AgNO_3}\) to metallic silver while being oxidised to \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).
  • Phosphorous acid: basicity and heating
    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is the dibasic phosphorus oxoacid.
    • It undergoes disproportionation on heating to give \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) and \(\mathrm{PH_3}\).
    • Identify it from \(\mathrm{O-H}\) count before applying the reaction.
  • Hydration of phosphorus oxides
    • Hydration of phosphorus oxides gives oxoacids with one terminal \(\mathrm{P=O}\) bond per molecule in the usual exam representation.
    • \(\mathrm{P_2O_3+3H_2O\rightarrow 2H_3PO_3}\).
    • \(\mathrm{P_4O_{10}+6H_2O\rightarrow 4H_3PO_4}\).
    • Do not count all oxygen atoms as \(\mathrm{P=O}\) bonds.
  • Pyro and condensed phosphorus oxoacids
    • Pyrophosphoric acid \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_7}\) contains \(4\) \(\mathrm{P-O-H}\) bonds, \(2\) \(\mathrm{P=O}\) bonds, and one \(\mathrm{P-O-P}\) linkage.
    • Pyro/condensed phosphorus oxoacids often test bridge-bond recognition rather than formula alone.
    • \(\mathrm{P-O-P}\) linkages occur in condensed phosphorus oxoacids such as \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_5}\), \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_7}\), and cyclic metaphosphoric acid \((\mathrm{HPO_3})_3\).
    • Hypophosphoric acid \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_6}\) has a direct \(\mathrm{P-P}\) bond instead.
  • Calgon memory point
    • Calgon is sodium hexametaphosphate and is used in water softening by complexing \(\mathrm{Ca^{2+}}\) and \(\mathrm{Mg^{2+}}\).
    • Do not confuse “hexametaphosphate” with “hexametaphosphite”.

    Group 15 hydrides: trends and properties
  • Bond angle, basicity and thermal stability trends
    • In group-15 hydrides, bond angle decreases down the group: \(\mathrm{NH_3>PH_3>AsH_3>SbH_3}\).
    • This is linked to decreasing effective hybridisation and bond angles approaching \(90^\circ\) down the group.
    • Basic character also decreases down the group: \(\mathrm{NH_3>PH_3>AsH_3>SbH_3}\).
    • The lone pair on nitrogen is most available for donation.
    • Thermal stability decreases down the group because the E-H bond strength decreases: \(\mathrm{NH_3>PH_3>AsH_3>SbH_3}\).
    • Any order placing \(\mathrm{NH_3}\) last is incorrect.
  • Boiling point of group-15 hydrides
    • For boiling points of group-15 hydrides, include both hydrogen bonding and molar mass.
    • \(\mathrm{NH_3}\) has hydrogen bonding and therefore boils higher than \(\mathrm{PH_3}\).
    • Very heavy \(\mathrm{BiH_3}\) can have the highest boiling point among \(\mathrm{NH_3}\), \(\mathrm{PH_3}\), and \(\mathrm{BiH_3}\).
    • In the common set \(\mathrm{NH_3}\), \(\mathrm{PH_3}\), \(\mathrm{AsH_3}\), \(\mathrm{SbH_3}\), the descending boiling point order used is \(\mathrm{SbH_3>NH_3>AsH_3>PH_3}\).
    • The hydrogen-bonding exception of \(\mathrm{NH_3}\) must be combined with the mass trend.
  • Phosphine smell
    • \(\mathrm{PH_3}\) is a colourless gas with rotten fish smell.
    • Do not confuse it with \(\mathrm{H_2S}\), which is associated with rotten egg smell.

    Group 16: oxygen, ozone and sulphur allotropes
  • Group 16 identity and magnetic shortcut
    • Group 16 elements are called chalcogens, meaning ore-forming elements.
    • Do not confuse them with pnictogens of group 15 or halogens of group 17.
    • \(\mathrm{O_2}\) is paramagnetic because it has unpaired electrons.
    • \(\mathrm{N_2}\), \(\mathrm{CO_2}\), and \(\mathrm{N_2O}\) are treated as diamagnetic in these questions.
  • Ozone: stability, use and shape
    • Ozone \(\mathrm{O_3}\) is thermodynamically less stable than \(\mathrm{O_2}\).
    • It is a strong oxidising agent and is used as a disinfectant/germicide.
    • Do not state that ozone is more stable than oxygen.
    • Ozone is bent, with bond angle about \(116.8^\circ\).
    • In comparison questions, \(\mathrm{O_3}\) has the largest bond angle among \(\mathrm{P_4}\), \(\mathrm{S_6}\), \(\mathrm{S_8}\), and \(\mathrm{O_3}\).
  • Sulphur allotropes and bond angles
    • Rhombic sulphur is the stable allotrope of sulphur at room temperature.
    • Both rhombic and monoclinic sulphur contain \(\mathrm{S_8}\) molecules.
    • The \(\mathrm{S_8}\) ring is crown-shaped/non-planar, not planar.
    • Important bond-angle values are \(\mathrm{P_4}=60^\circ\), \(\mathrm{S_6}\approx102.2^\circ\), \(\mathrm{S_8}\approx107^\circ\), and \(\mathrm{O_3}\approx116.8^\circ\).
    • Hence the increasing order is \(\mathrm{P_4<S_6<S_8<O_3}\).

    Group 16 hydrides, oxides and sulphur dioxide
  • Boiling point of group-16 hydrides
    • For group-16 hydrides, \(\mathrm{H_2O}\) has the highest boiling point due to strong hydrogen bonding.
    • Among the heavier hydrides, boiling point increases with molar mass: \(\mathrm{H_2S<H_2Se<H_2Te}\).
    • The full boiling point order is \(\mathrm{H_2O>H_2Te>H_2Se>H_2S}\).
    • If a question asks lowest and highest among group-16 hydrides, the answer is lowest \(\mathrm{H_2S}\) and highest \(\mathrm{H_2O}\).
  • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) as reducing agent
    • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) is a classic reducing agent because sulphur is in the \(+4\) oxidation state and can be oxidised to \(+6\).
    • \(\mathrm{SO_3}\) already has sulphur in \(+6\), so it is not a reducing agent in the same sense.
    • When \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) reacts with aqueous \(\mathrm{KMnO_4}\), manganese in permanganate is reduced from \(+7\) to \(\mathrm{Mn^{2+}}\), while sulphur is oxidised from \(+4\) to \(+6\).
    • The standard product-side manganese ion is \(\mathrm{Mn^{2+}}\), not \(\mathrm{Mn^{4+}}\) or \(\mathrm{Mn^{6+}}\).
  • Hot concentrated sulphuric acid with carbon
    • Hot concentrated sulphuric acid oxidises carbon: \(\mathrm{C+2H_2SO_4(conc.)\xrightarrow{\Delta}CO_2+2SO_2+2H_2O}\).
    • Carbon forms \(\mathrm{CO_2}\), and sulphuric acid is reduced to \(\mathrm{SO_2}\).
  • Group 16 oxides and selenium compounds
    • Both \(\mathrm{CO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) are acidic oxides.
    • Do not classify \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) as neutral or basic just because it is the reduction product of sulphuric acid.
    • \(\mathrm{SeO_2}\) is a colourless solid at room temperature, not a gas.
    • \(\mathrm{SeO_3}\) is acidic, and \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) is reducing.
  • \(\mathrm{Se_2Cl_2}\) disproportionation
    • \(\mathrm{Se_2Cl_2}\) disproportionates by selenium going from \(+1\) to both \(+4\) and \(0\): \(\mathrm{2Se_2Cl_2 \rightarrow SeCl_4 + 3Se}\).
    • For disproportionation questions, first identify oxidation state, then choose one higher and one lower product.

    Group 16: oxoacids of sulphur and sulphuric acid manufacture
  • Peroxydisulphuric acid
    • Peroxydisulphuric acid is \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_8}\) and contains an \(\mathrm{O-O}\) peroxy bond.
    • In peroxo compounds, the oxygens in the peroxide linkage are assigned oxidation state \(-1\).
    • In \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_8}\), both sulphur atoms are in \(+6\) oxidation state.
    • The structure has two \(\mathrm{S-OH}\) bonds.
    • Do not count four \(\mathrm{S-OH}\) bonds from the formula.
  • Pyrosulphuric acid and sulphur oxoacid linkages
    • Pyrosulphuric acid \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_7}\) contains an \(\mathrm{S-O-S}\) bridge, not a peroxy bond.
    • The word “pyro” is a useful clue for a bridge oxygen linkage.
    • In sulphur oxoacid structure questions, distinguish three linkages carefully:
      • \(\mathrm{O-O}\) for peroxo acids.
      • \(\mathrm{S-O-S}\) for pyro acids.
      • \(\mathrm{S-S}\) for some lower sulphur oxoacids.
  • Contact process catalyst poisoning
    • In the contact process for sulphuric acid, arsenic impurities poison the \(\mathrm{V_2O_5}\) catalyst.
    • The arsenic purifier contains hydrated ferric oxide, \(\mathrm{Fe_2O_3\cdot xH_2O}\).

    Group 17: halogens, occurrence and physical properties
  • Fluorine minerals and chloride trap
    • Important fluorine minerals are fluorspar \(\mathrm{CaF_2}\), cryolite \(\mathrm{Na_3AlF_6}\), and fluoroapatite.
    • Carnallite, \(\mathrm{KCl\cdot MgCl_2\cdot 6H_2O}\), is a chloride mineral and is not a mineral of fluorine.
  • Halogen colours and chlorine identification
    • Halogen colours are direct memory points:
      • \(\mathrm{F_2}\) is pale yellow.
      • \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) is greenish-yellow.
      • \(\mathrm{Br_2}\) is red-brown.
      • \(\mathrm{I_2}\) is violet.
    • Chlorine is a greenish-yellow gas with pungent smell; it is not colourless.
    • \(\mathrm{N_2}\) is colourless, while brown gas is usually \(\mathrm{NO_2}\).
  • Hydrogen halide acidity and boiling point
    • The acidity of hydrogen halides increases down the group as \(\mathrm{HF<HCl<HBr<HI}\).
    • This is different from oxyacid acidity, where electronegativity or oxygen count may dominate.
    • \(\mathrm{HF}\) has an anomalously high boiling point among hydrogen halides because of strong intermolecular hydrogen bonding.
    • This explains the assertion-reason pattern where both statements are true and the reason correctly explains the assertion.

    Group 17: chlorine, hydrogen chloride and interhalogens
  • Deacon’s process for chlorine preparation
    • Deacon’s process is the catalytic oxidation of hydrogen chloride to chlorine: \(\mathrm{4HCl+O_2\xrightarrow[723\,K]{CuCl_2}2Cl_2+2H_2O}\).
    • The clues are \(\mathrm{HCl}\), \(\mathrm{O_2}\), \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\), \(723\ \mathrm{K}\), and \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) formation.
    • Do not confuse Deacon’s process with electrolysis of brine, bleaching powder formation, Hall-Heroult process, van-Arkel process, or Serpeck’s process.
    • Deacon’s process is specifically chlorine preparation from \(\mathrm{HCl}\).
  • Chlorine reactions and uses
    • Chlorine oxidises ferrous salts to ferric salts and bleaches by oxidation.
    • However, the statement that chlorine directly oxidises iodine to periodic acid in water is incorrect in the tested statement set.
    • Chlorine is used in phosgene preparation: \(\mathrm{CO+Cl_2\rightarrow COCl_2}\).
    • This application is frequently paired with other p-block uses.
  • Chlorine with excess ammonia
    • Excess ammonia reacts with chlorine as \(\mathrm{8NH_3+3Cl_2\rightarrow N_2+6NH_4Cl}\).
    • Therefore \(1\) mole of chlorine oxidises \(\dfrac{8}{3}\) moles of ammonia.
  • Interhalogen reactivity and hydrolysis
    • Interhalogen compounds are usually more reactive than the corresponding halogens because the bond between two different halogens is more polar and relatively weaker.
    • They are treated as diamagnetic in the usual entrance-exam context.
    • Hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{ICl}\) gives \(\mathrm{HOI+HCl}\), not \(\mathrm{HI+HOCl}\).
    • Match iodine with hypoiodous acid and chlorine with hydrochloric acid.
  • Fluorinating interhalogens and shapes
    • Fluorine-containing interhalogens can act as fluorinating agents.
    • For shape memory, \(\mathrm{ClF_3}\) is T-shaped, \(\mathrm{BrF_5}\) is square pyramidal, and \(\mathrm{IF_7}\) is pentagonal bipyramidal.
    • Bromine with excess fluorine forms \(\mathrm{BrF_5}\), whose VSEPR type is \(AX_5E\), giving square pyramidal molecular shape.
    • Do not choose \(\mathrm{BrF_3}\) when excess fluorine is specified.

    Group 17: oxoacids and oxides of halogens
  • Chlorine oxoacids and oxidation states
    • Among chlorine oxoacids, chlorine reaches its highest oxidation state of \(+7\) in \(\mathrm{HClO_4}\).
    • The sequence is \(\mathrm{HClO}\) \(+1\), \(\mathrm{HClO_2}\) \(+3\), \(\mathrm{HClO_3}\) \(+5\), and \(\mathrm{HClO_4}\) \(+7\).
  • Hypohalous acid acidity trend
    • For oxyacids of the same type \(\mathrm{HOX}\), acidity increases with electronegativity of \(X\).
    • Hence \(\mathrm{HClO>HBrO>HIO}\).
    • Do not apply the hydrogen-halide acidity order to hypohalous acids.
    • \(\mathrm{HF<HCl<HBr<HI}\) is for hydrogen halides, while \(\mathrm{HClO>HBrO>HIO}\) is for same-oxygen-count oxyacids.
  • Halogen oxides: stability, uses and traps
    • \(\mathrm{OF_2}\) is stable at room temperature in the context of halogen-oxygen compounds.
    • \(\mathrm{I_2O_5}\) is used for estimation of carbon monoxide through \(\mathrm{5CO+I_2O_5\rightarrow I_2+5CO_2}\).
    • \(\mathrm{ClO_2}\) is used as a bleaching agent, especially for paper pulp and textiles.
    • When \(\mathrm{I_2O_5}\) and \(\mathrm{ClO_2}\) appear in statement questions, these two are usually reliable anchors.
    • The statement “stability order of oxides of halogens is \(\mathrm{I>Br>Cl}\)” was rejected in the tested statement set.
    • Do not use it blindly without checking the exact context of the question.

    Group 18: noble gases and xenon compounds
  • Noble gas physical properties and uses
    • Helium has the smallest atomic size among the noble gases and can diffuse through silica glass.
    • This small size also explains its high diffusibility and tendency to leak through tiny openings.
    • Helium is used as a diluent for oxygen in modern diving apparatus.
    • Argon is used to provide an inert atmosphere in high-temperature metallurgical processes.
    • Watch the order if the question asks \(Y,X\) instead of \(X,Y\).
    • Neon is colourless and odourless under ordinary conditions.
    • In discharge tubes, it gives a red-orange glow; it is not a fluorescent green gas and does not have rotten egg smell.
    • Enthalpy of vaporisation of noble gases increases down the group because atomic size, molar mass, and London dispersion forces increase.
    • The order is \(\mathrm{Xe>Kr>Ar>Ne>He}\).
  • Xenon fluoride shapes and lone-pair counts
    • Xenon fluorides follow a key shape sequence: \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\) is linear, \(\mathrm{XeF_4}\) is square planar, and \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) is distorted octahedral.
    • Lone-pair counts on xenon are high-yield:
      • \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\) has \(3\) lone pairs.
      • \(\mathrm{XeF_4}\) has \(2\) lone pairs.
      • \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) has \(1\) lone pair.
      • \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\) has \(1\) lone pair.
    • \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) does not have zero lone pairs.
    • \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\) is \(AX_2E_3\); the three lone pairs occupy equatorial positions in trigonal bipyramidal electron geometry, leaving the two bonds opposite and the molecule linear.
    • This also explains why \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\) has maximum lone pair-lone pair repulsion among common options like \(\mathrm{ClF_3}\), \(\mathrm{IF_5}\), and \(\mathrm{SF_4}\).
    • \(\mathrm{XeF_4}\) is square planar because xenon has four bond pairs and two lone pairs arranged opposite in an octahedral electron-pair arrangement.
  • \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\), \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\) and \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\)
    • \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) is formed under excess fluorine/high pressure conditions and can also be made by fluorination of \(\mathrm{XeF_4}\): \(\mathrm{XeF_4+O_2F_2\rightarrow XeF_6+O_2}\).
    • Its shape is distorted octahedral.
    • Partial hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) gives \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\): \(\mathrm{XeF_6+H_2O\rightarrow XeOF_4+2HF}\).
    • \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\) has five bond pairs and one lone pair on xenon, so its shape is square pyramidal.
    • Complete hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) gives \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\).
    • \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\) is represented with three \(\mathrm{Xe=O}\) bonds and one lone pair, giving \(3\sigma+3\pi=6\) total \(\sigma+\pi\) bonds and pyramidal molecular shape.
    • \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\), \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\), and \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) each have one lone pair on xenon.
    • Shape questions often test whether you remember the lone pair even when xenon has many bonds.
  • Xenon fluoride reactivity and hydrolysis/disproportionation
    • Xenon fluorides become stronger fluorinating and oxidising agents with higher oxidation state: \(\mathrm{XeF_6>XeF_4>XeF_2}\).
    • Do not reverse the order by assuming the lower fluoride is more reactive.
    • Hydrolysis/disproportionation of \(\mathrm{XeF_4}\) can be balanced as: \(\mathrm{6XeF_4+12H_2O\rightarrow 4Xe+2XeO_3+24HF+3O_2}\).
    • Here fluorine fixes the \(\mathrm{HF}\) count, while xenon is split between elemental xenon and \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\).

    Common molecular shapes and VSEPR shortcuts
  • High-frequency VSEPR shapes
    • For VSEPR matching, memorise these high-frequency examples:
      • \(\mathrm{XeF_4}\) square planar.
      • \(\mathrm{ClF_3}\) T-shaped.
      • \(\mathrm{BrF_5}\) square pyramidal.
      • \(\mathrm{IF_7}\) pentagonal bipyramidal.
    • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\), \(\mathrm{SnCl_2}\), and \(\mathrm{PbCl_2}\) are bent/angular due to lone-pair effects.
    • \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\) is the linear molecule in comparison sets containing these species.
  • Lone-pair counting and geometry vs shape
    • For lone-pair repulsion questions, count lone pairs on the central atom before worrying about the final shape.
    • \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\), with three central lone pairs, gives more lone pair-lone pair repulsion than \(\mathrm{ClF_3}\), \(\mathrm{IF_5}\), or \(\mathrm{SF_4}\).
    • Distinguish electron-pair geometry from molecular shape.
    • For example, \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\) has tetrahedral electron-pair arrangement but pyramidal molecular shape because one position is occupied by a lone pair.

    Redox, oxidation state and magnetic-property shortcuts
  • Reducing-agent and highest-oxidation-state shortcut
    • A reducing agent is oxidised during the reaction.
    • Species with a central atom already in its highest common oxidation state, such as sulphur in \(\mathrm{SO_3}\), usually do not act as reducing agents in these simple comparisons.
  • Disproportionation shortcut
    • For disproportionation, the same element must go both to a higher and to a lower oxidation state.
    • Do oxidation-state checking before balancing; it quickly identifies pairs such as \(\mathrm{SeCl_4}\) and \(\mathrm{Se}\) from \(\mathrm{Se_2Cl_2}\).
  • Magnetic-property shortcut
    • For paramagnetism, remember \(\mathrm{O_2}\) and \(\mathrm{NO_2}\) as paramagnetic gases.
    • Most closed-shell molecules in these questions, such as \(\mathrm{N_2O}\), are treated as diamagnetic.
  • Peroxo oxidation-state shortcut
    • In peroxo compounds, oxygen in the \(\mathrm{O-O}\) bond has oxidation state \(-1\).
    • This is essential for correct oxidation-state calculation in \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_8}\).

    Common names, uses and physical identification
  • Direct use-pairs
    • Calgon is sodium hexametaphosphate and is used as a water-softening agent by complexing hardness-causing \(\mathrm{Ca^{2+}}\) and \(\mathrm{Mg^{2+}}\) ions.
    • \(\mathrm{I_2O_5}\) is used for estimation of carbon monoxide: \(\mathrm{5CO+I_2O_5\rightarrow I_2+5CO_2}\).
    • This pair is a direct application-based memory point.
    • \(\mathrm{O_3}\) is used as a disinfectant and water sterilising agent because of its strong oxidising nature.
    • \(\mathrm{ClO_2}\) is also used as a bleaching agent.
  • Odour and colour memory
    • \(\mathrm{PH_3}\) has rotten fish smell.
    • \(\mathrm{H_2S}\) has rotten egg smell.
    • \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) is greenish-yellow and pungent.
    • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) is colourless with pungent/choking smell.
  • Aqua regia trap
    • Aqua regia dissolves both gold and platinum by forming chloro-complexes.
    • The statement “Au dissolves in aqua regia but Pt does not” is false.

    Must-know equations
  • Group 15 phosphorus and phosphine equations
    • \(\mathrm{P_4+3NaOH+3H_2O\rightarrow PH_3+3NaH_2PO_2}\)
    • \(\mathrm{PCl_3+3H_2O\rightarrow H_3PO_3+3HCl}\)
    • \(\mathrm{PCl_5+4H_2O\rightarrow H_3PO_4+5HCl}\)
    • \(\mathrm{4H_3PO_3\xrightarrow{\Delta}PH_3+3H_3PO_4}\)
    • \(\mathrm{4PH_3+6CuSO_4\rightarrow 2Cu_3P_2+6H_2SO_4}\)
  • Group 15 and 17 nitrogen/chlorine equations
    • \(\mathrm{4HCl+O_2\xrightarrow[723\,K]{CuCl_2}2Cl_2+2H_2O}\)
    • \(\mathrm{NaNO_2+HCl\rightarrow HNO_2+NaCl}\), followed by formation of \(\mathrm{NO}\) and \(\mathrm{NO_2}\).
    • \(\mathrm{3Cu+8HNO_3(dil.)\rightarrow 3Cu(NO_3)_2+2NO+4H_2O}\)
    • \(\mathrm{Cu+4HNO_3(conc.)\rightarrow Cu(NO_3)_2+2NO_2+2H_2O}\)
    • \(\mathrm{2Pb(NO_3)_2\xrightarrow{\Delta}2PbO+4NO_2+O_2}\)
    • \(\mathrm{8NH_3+3Cl_2\rightarrow N_2+6NH_4Cl}\)
  • Group 16 and halogen oxide equations
    • \(\mathrm{C+2H_2SO_4(conc.)\xrightarrow{\Delta}CO_2+2SO_2+2H_2O}\)
    • \(\mathrm{5CO+I_2O_5\rightarrow I_2+5CO_2}\)
  • Xenon compound equations
    • \(\mathrm{XeF_6+H_2O\rightarrow XeOF_4+2HF}\)
    • \(\mathrm{XeF_6+3H_2O\rightarrow XeO_3+6HF}\)
    • \(\mathrm{XeF_4+O_2F_2\rightarrow XeF_6+O_2}\)
    • \(\mathrm{6XeF_4+12H_2O\rightarrow 4Xe+2XeO_3+24HF+3O_2}\)

    General characteristics of p-block elements
  • The p-block contains groups 13 to 18, in which the differentiating electron enters a p-orbital. For the groups covered here, the outer electronic configurations are: group 15 \(\mathrm{ns^2np^3}\), group 16 \(\mathrm{ns^2np^4}\), group 17 \(\mathrm{ns^2np^5}\), and group 18 \(\mathrm{ns^2np^6}\), except helium, which is \(\mathrm{1s^2}\).
  • Across a period, atomic size generally decreases because electrons are added to the same shell while nuclear charge increases. Down a group, atomic size increases because a new shell is added; this explains many trends in ionisation enthalpy, electronegativity and metallic character.
  • Ionisation enthalpy generally increases across a period and decreases down a group. A useful exception is that group 16 elements have lower first ionisation enthalpy than group 15 elements in the same period, because removing an electron from the half-filled \(\mathrm{np^3}\) configuration of group 15 is less favourable.
  • Electron gain enthalpy generally becomes more negative across a period and less negative down a group. In halogens, chlorine has more negative electron gain enthalpy than fluorine because the very small size of fluorine causes higher inter-electronic repulsion when an extra electron is added.
  • Electronegativity increases across a period and decreases down a group. Fluorine is the most electronegative element; this explains why fluorine forms fluorides readily, why \(\mathrm{OF_2}\) is called an oxygen fluoride, and why xenon compounds are mainly formed with fluorine and oxygen.
  • Metallic character increases down a group and decreases across a period. Non-metallic character shows the opposite trend; hence nitrogen, oxygen and fluorine show strongly non-metallic behaviour, while heavier elements in the same groups show increasing metallic tendency.
  • The first element of a p-block group often behaves anomalously because of small size, high electronegativity, high ionisation enthalpy and absence of vacant d-orbitals. This is important for nitrogen, oxygen and fluorine.
  • The inert pair effect becomes more important down the heavier p-block groups. It means the \(\mathrm{ns^2}\) pair becomes less available for bonding, so lower oxidation states become more stable in heavier elements.

    Group 15: occurrence, configuration and general trends
  • Group 15 elements have outer configuration \(\mathrm{ns^2np^3}\). The half-filled p-subshell gives extra stability, which is why their ionisation enthalpy is relatively high compared with group 16 elements of the same period.
  • Important oxidation states are \(-3\), \(+3\), and \(+5\). Nitrogen can show \(+5\) oxidation state in compounds such as \(\mathrm{HNO_3}\), \(\mathrm{NO_3^-}\), and \(\mathrm{N_2O_5}\), but it cannot expand its octet to form pentahalides.
  • Nitrogen forms trihalides such as \(\mathrm{NCl_3}\), but not pentahalides such as \(\mathrm{NCl_5}\). Phosphorus forms both \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) and \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) because it can accommodate higher covalency.
  • Hydrides of group 15 are \(\mathrm{NH_3}\), \(\mathrm{PH_3}\), \(\mathrm{AsH_3}\), \(\mathrm{SbH_3}\), and \(\mathrm{BiH_3}\). Bond angle, basic character and thermal stability decrease down the group: \(\mathrm{NH_3>PH_3>AsH_3>SbH_3}\).
  • Boiling point of group 15 hydrides depends on both molecular mass and hydrogen bonding. \(\mathrm{NH_3}\) has hydrogen bonding, so it boils higher than expected, but very heavy hydrides can still show high boiling points due to strong dispersion forces.
  • \(\mathrm{PH_3}\) is a colourless poisonous gas with rotten fish smell. This odour is useful for direct identification questions and should not be confused with rotten egg smell of \(\mathrm{H_2S}\).

    Dinitrogen, ammonia and nitrogen oxides
  • Dinitrogen is very stable because of the strong \(\mathrm{N\equiv N}\) bond. Direct non-catalytic combination of \(\mathrm{N_2}\) and \(\mathrm{O_2}\) requires very high temperature, about \(2000\ \mathrm{K}\), to form \(\mathrm{NO}\).
  • Nitrogen gas is obtained in many standard reactions: \(\mathrm{NH_4^+ + NO_2^- \rightarrow N_2 + 2H_2O}\), \(\mathrm{CO(NH_2)_2 + 2HNO_2 \rightarrow CO_2 + 2N_2 + 3H_2O}\), and \(\mathrm{(NH_4)_2Cr_2O_7 \xrightarrow{\Delta} Cr_2O_3 + N_2 + 4H_2O}\).
  • Metal azides liberate nitrogen on heating, for example \(\mathrm{Ba(N_3)_2 \xrightarrow{\Delta} Ba + 3N_2}\). However, nitrate salts do not automatically liberate \(\mathrm{N_2}\); \(\mathrm{NaNO_3}\) on heating gives \(\mathrm{NaNO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{O_2}\).
  • Ammonium nitrate decomposes to nitrous oxide: \(\mathrm{NH_4NO_3 \xrightarrow{\Delta} N_2O + 2H_2O}\). In magnetic-property questions, \(\mathrm{N_2O}\) is treated as diamagnetic, while \(\mathrm{NO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{O_2}\) are paramagnetic.
  • Ammonia is oxidised by chlorine. With excess ammonia, the reaction is \(\mathrm{8NH_3 + 3Cl_2 \rightarrow N_2 + 6NH_4Cl}\), so \(1\) mole of \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) oxidises \(\dfrac{8}{3}\) moles of \(\mathrm{NH_3}\).
  • Nitrogen oxides show different oxidation states and acidic behaviour. In general, acidic character increases with oxidation state of nitrogen: \(\mathrm{N_2O<NO<N_2O_3<N_2O_4<N_2O_5}\).
  • Sodium nitrite with acid first forms unstable nitrous acid: \(\mathrm{NaNO_2 + HCl \rightarrow HNO_2 + NaCl}\). Nitrous acid can decompose to give \(\mathrm{NO}\) and \(\mathrm{NO_2}\), so nitrite-acid reactions are a common source of mixed nitrogen oxides.

    Nitric acid
  • Nitric acid is a strong oxidising acid. With metals, it generally gives nitrogen oxides instead of hydrogen gas, because nitrate ion is reduced while the metal is oxidised.
  • Copper with dilute nitric acid gives nitric oxide: \(\mathrm{3Cu+8HNO_3(dil.)\rightarrow 3Cu(NO_3)_2+2NO+4H_2O}\). Copper with concentrated nitric acid gives nitrogen dioxide: \(\mathrm{Cu+4HNO_3(conc.)\rightarrow Cu(NO_3)_2+2NO_2+2H_2O}\).
  • Zinc with concentrated nitric acid gives \(\mathrm{NO_2}\), while zinc with dilute nitric acid can give \(\mathrm{N_2O}\) in the standard entrance-exam reaction set. The product depends on the metal and the concentration of nitric acid.
  • In the brown ring test for nitrate ion, nitrate is reduced by \(\mathrm{Fe^{2+}}\) in acidic medium to \(\mathrm{NO}\), which then forms the brown nitrosyl complex \(\mathrm{[Fe(H_2O)_5NO]^{2+}}\). The oxide formed in situ is \(\mathrm{NO}\), not \(\mathrm{NO_2}\).
  • In nitric acid, the structure is planar around nitrogen due to resonance. Useful bond-angle values are \(\angle\mathrm{H-O-N}\approx102^\circ\) and \(\angle\mathrm{O-N-O}\approx130^\circ\).

    Phosphorus and phosphine
  • Phosphorus shows several allotropes; white phosphorus is especially reactive and gives phosphine with alkali. A key reaction is \(\mathrm{P_4+3NaOH+3H_2O\rightarrow PH_3+3NaH_2PO_2}\).
  • Phosphine can also be prepared by hydrolysis of metal phosphides: \(\mathrm{Ca_3P_2+6H_2O\rightarrow 3Ca(OH)_2+2PH_3}\). Phosphonium salts with alkali also liberate \(\mathrm{PH_3}\).
  • Phosphorous acid on heating disproportionates to phosphoric acid and phosphine: \(\mathrm{4H_3PO_3\xrightarrow{\Delta}PH_3+3H_3PO_4}\). This reaction is linked with the reducing nature of lower phosphorus oxoacids.
  • Phosphine reacts with copper sulphate to give copper phosphide: \(\mathrm{4PH_3+6CuSO_4\rightarrow 2Cu_3P_2+6H_2SO_4}\). This reaction is useful for identifying \(\mathrm{PH_3}\) in sequence questions.

    Phosphorus halides
  • \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) is pyramidal because phosphorus has three bond pairs and one lone pair. Its hydrolysis gives phosphorous acid: \(\mathrm{PCl_3+3H_2O\rightarrow H_3PO_3+3HCl}\).
  • \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) is trigonal bipyramidal in the gaseous state, with three equatorial and two axial bonds. In the solid state it is ionic: \(\mathrm{[PCl_4]^+[PCl_6]^-}\).
  • Sulphuryl chloride acts as a chlorinating agent and can convert phosphorus to \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\). Complete hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) gives orthophosphoric acid: \(\mathrm{PCl_5+4H_2O\rightarrow H_3PO_4+5HCl}\).
  • Partial hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) may pass through \(\mathrm{POCl_3}\), but complete hydrolysis must be remembered as ending in \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).

    Oxoacids of phosphorus
  • Basicity of phosphorus oxoacids is decided only by hydrogens attached to oxygen. Hydrogens directly attached to phosphorus as \(\mathrm{P-H}\) are not ionisable.
  • The standard basicity pattern is: \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) is monobasic, \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is dibasic, and \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) is tribasic. Structurally, \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is written as \(\mathrm{HP(=O)(OH)_2}\), while \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) is \(\mathrm{P(=O)(OH)_3}\).
  • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) is hypophosphorous acid. It is monobasic, has phosphorus in \(+1\) oxidation state, and is a reducing agent because it contains \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds.
  • Reducing nature of phosphorus oxoacids depends mainly on the presence of \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds, not on basicity. \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) is a strong reducing agent and can reduce \(\mathrm{Ag^+}\) to metallic silver while being oxidised to \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).
  • Hydration of phosphorus oxides gives important oxoacids: \(\mathrm{P_2O_3+3H_2O\rightarrow 2H_3PO_3}\) and \(\mathrm{P_4O_{10}+6H_2O\rightarrow 4H_3PO_4}\). In these structures, each acid molecule is commonly represented with one terminal \(\mathrm{P=O}\) bond.
  • Condensed phosphorus oxoacids may contain \(\mathrm{P-O-P}\) bridges. Pyrophosphoric acid \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_7}\) has \(4\) \(\mathrm{P-O-H}\) bonds, \(2\) \(\mathrm{P=O}\) bonds and one \(\mathrm{P-O-P}\) linkage.
  • \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_5}\), \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_7}\), and cyclic metaphosphoric acid \((\mathrm{HPO_3})_3\) contain \(\mathrm{P-O-P}\) linkages. \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_6}\) is a special case with a direct \(\mathrm{P-P}\) bond.
  • Calgon is sodium hexametaphosphate and acts as a water-softening agent by complexing \(\mathrm{Ca^{2+}}\) and \(\mathrm{Mg^{2+}}\). The name “hexametaphosphate” is the key memory point.

    Group 16: occurrence, configuration and general features
  • Group 16 elements have outer configuration \(\mathrm{ns^2np^4}\) and are called chalcogens. The name is associated with ore-forming elements because many ores occur as oxides and sulphides.
  • Oxygen mainly shows \(-2\) oxidation state, except in peroxides, superoxides and compounds with fluorine. Sulphur can also show \(+4\) and \(+6\) oxidation states, which is central to the chemistry of \(\mathrm{SO_2}\), \(\mathrm{SO_3}\), oxoacids and sulphuric acid.
  • Oxides are classified as acidic, basic, amphoteric and neutral. Non-metal oxides such as \(\mathrm{CO_2}\), \(\mathrm{SO_2}\), and \(\mathrm{N_2O_5}\) are acidic; \(\mathrm{CO}\), \(\mathrm{NO}\), and \(\mathrm{N_2O}\) are neutral oxides.
  • Among oxides in a period, acidic character generally increases with increasing oxidation state of the central atom. This supports comparison questions such as increasing acidic character of nitrogen oxides.

    Dioxygen, ozone and sulphur allotropes
  • Dioxygen is essential for combustion and respiration and is paramagnetic because it has unpaired electrons. This makes \(\mathrm{O_2}\) a repeated example in magnetic-property questions.
  • Ozone is an allotrope of oxygen. It has a bent structure with bond angle close to \(117^\circ\), and its bonding is described by resonance.
  • Ozone is thermodynamically less stable than \(\mathrm{O_2}\), but it is a strong oxidising agent. It oxidises substances such as ferrous salts and is used as a disinfectant, germicide and water purifier.
  • Ozone is more soluble in water than oxygen and is used for water and air purification. It is also used as a dry bleaching agent and in ozonolysis of unsaturated organic compounds.
  • Rhombic sulphur is the stable allotrope of sulphur at room temperature. Both rhombic and monoclinic sulphur contain \(\mathrm{S_8}\) molecules, but the \(\mathrm{S_8}\) ring is crown-shaped and non-planar.
  • Important structural bond angles to remember are: \(\mathrm{P_4}=60^\circ\), \(\mathrm{S_6}\approx102.2^\circ\), \(\mathrm{S_8}\approx107^\circ\), and \(\mathrm{O_3}\approx116.8^\circ\). Hence the order is \(\mathrm{P_4<S_6<S_8<O_3}\).

    Group 16 hydrides, sulphur dioxide and simple oxides
  • For group 16 hydrides, \(\mathrm{H_2O}\) has the highest boiling point due to strong intermolecular hydrogen bonding. Among the heavier hydrides, boiling point increases down the group due to increasing molar mass and dispersion forces: \(\mathrm{H_2S<H_2Se<H_2Te}\).
  • The complete boiling point order is \(\mathrm{H_2O>H_2Te>H_2Se>H_2S}\). Hence, among these hydrides, lowest boiling point is \(\mathrm{H_2S}\) and highest is \(\mathrm{H_2O}\).
  • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) is a colourless gas with pungent/choking smell and behaves as an acidic oxide. It also acts as a reducing agent because sulphur in \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) is in \(+4\) oxidation state and can be oxidised to \(+6\).
  • With aqueous \(\mathrm{KMnO_4}\), \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) is oxidised to sulphate while permanganate is reduced to \(\mathrm{Mn^{2+}}\). This is a standard reaction showing reducing nature of sulphur dioxide.
  • \(\mathrm{SO_3}\) has sulphur in \(+6\) oxidation state and is not a reducing agent like \(\mathrm{SO_2}\). In such comparisons, first check the oxidation state of the central atom.
  • Selenium dioxide, \(\mathrm{SeO_2}\), is a colourless solid at room temperature, while \(\mathrm{SeO_3}\) is acidic. Physical-state traps are common in group 16 oxide questions.
  • \(\mathrm{Se_2Cl_2}\) disproportionates because selenium in \(+1\) oxidation state changes to both \(+4\) and \(0\): \(\mathrm{2Se_2Cl_2\rightarrow SeCl_4+3Se}\).

    Oxoacids of sulphur and sulphuric acid
  • Oxoacids of sulphur are structure-sensitive. Pyrosulphuric acid \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_7}\) contains an \(\mathrm{S-O-S}\) bridge, while peroxydisulphuric acid \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_8}\) contains an \(\mathrm{O-O}\) peroxy bond.
  • In peroxo compounds, oxygen in the \(\mathrm{O-O}\) bond has oxidation state \(-1\). Therefore, in \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_8}\), both sulphur atoms are in \(+6\) oxidation state, and the structure contains two \(\mathrm{S-OH}\) bonds.
  • In sulphur oxoacid questions, identify the special linkage first: \(\mathrm{O-O}\) for peroxo acids, \(\mathrm{S-O-S}\) for pyro acids, and \(\mathrm{S-S}\) in some lower sulphur oxoacids. Formula alone is not always enough.
  • Sulphuric acid is manufactured mainly by the contact process. The main steps are: formation of \(\mathrm{SO_2}\), purification of \(\mathrm{SO_2}\), catalytic oxidation to \(\mathrm{SO_3}\), absorption of \(\mathrm{SO_3}\) in concentrated \(\mathrm{H_2SO_4}\) to form oleum, and dilution of oleum.
  • The catalytic step in the contact process is \(\mathrm{2SO_2+O_2 \xrightarrow[V_2O_5]{720\,K} 2SO_3}\). The plant conditions include about \(720\ \mathrm{K}\) and moderate pressure.
  • \(\mathrm{SO_3}\) is absorbed in concentrated \(\mathrm{H_2SO_4}\), not directly in water, because direct dissolution forms a corrosive mist. The absorbed product is oleum, \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_7}\), which is then diluted to obtain sulphuric acid.
  • Arsenic impurities poison the \(\mathrm{V_2O_5}\) catalyst in the contact process. The arsenic purifier contains hydrated ferric oxide, \(\mathrm{Fe_2O_3\cdot xH_2O}\).
  • Hot concentrated sulphuric acid acts as an oxidising agent. With carbon: \(\mathrm{C+2H_2SO_4(conc.)\xrightarrow{\Delta}CO_2+2SO_2+2H_2O}\). Both \(\mathrm{CO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) are acidic oxides.
  • Concentrated sulphuric acid is also a dehydrating agent. It removes water of crystallisation from blue copper sulphate and chars carbohydrates, leaving carbon.

    Group 17: occurrence, configuration and general properties
  • Group 17 elements have outer configuration \(\mathrm{ns^2np^5}\) and are called halogens. They are highly electronegative and form halide ions or covalent halides depending on the reacting element.
  • Important fluorine minerals are fluorspar \(\mathrm{CaF_2}\), cryolite \(\mathrm{Na_3AlF_6}\), and fluoroapatite. Carnallite, \(\mathrm{KCl\cdot MgCl_2\cdot 6H_2O}\), is a chloride mineral, not a fluorine mineral.
  • Halogen colours are direct memory points: \(\mathrm{F_2}\) is pale yellow, \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) is greenish-yellow, \(\mathrm{Br_2}\) is red-brown, and \(\mathrm{I_2}\) is violet.
  • Fluorine is the strongest oxidising halogen and is prepared by electrolysis of potassium hydrogen fluoride in anhydrous hydrogen fluoride. Because of its very high oxidising power, it cannot be prepared by ordinary oxidation of fluoride ions.
  • Fluorine reacts with many metals and non-metals to form fluorides. It also oxidises hydrogen halides; therefore, reactivity and oxidising power are strongest for fluorine among halogens.

    Chlorine and hydrogen chloride
  • Chlorine is a greenish-yellow gas with pungent smell. It is reactive, forms chlorides with metals and non-metals, oxidises ammonia, and is widely used in bleaching, plastics, antiseptics and insecticides.
  • Deacon’s process prepares chlorine by catalytic oxidation of hydrogen chloride: \(\mathrm{4HCl+O_2\xrightarrow[723\,K]{CuCl_2}2Cl_2+2H_2O}\). The key identifiers are \(\mathrm{HCl}\), \(\mathrm{O_2}\), \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\), \(723\ \mathrm{K}\), and \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\).
  • Chlorine is also obtained on large scale as a by-product during electrolysis of sodium chloride solution. Do not confuse this with Deacon’s method, which is oxidation of \(\mathrm{HCl}\).
  • Hydrogen chloride is prepared in the laboratory by heating sodium chloride with concentrated sulphuric acid. High-purity \(\mathrm{HCl}\) can be obtained by direct combination: \(\mathrm{H_2+Cl_2\rightarrow 2HCl}\).
  • The acid strength of hydrogen halides in water increases down the group: \(\mathrm{HF<HCl<HBr<HI}\). The H-X bond becomes longer and weaker down the group, so release of \(\mathrm{H^+}\) becomes easier.
  • \(\mathrm{HF}\) has an unusually high boiling point because of intermolecular hydrogen bonding. The boiling point anomaly is not explained by molar mass alone.

    Oxides and oxoacids of halogens
  • Oxygen is less electronegative than fluorine, so compounds such as \(\mathrm{OF_2}\) are called oxygen fluorides rather than fluorine oxides. \(\mathrm{OF_2}\) is stable at room temperature in the tested context.
  • Important oxides of chlorine include \(\mathrm{Cl_2O}\), \(\mathrm{ClO_2}\), \(\mathrm{Cl_2O_6}\), and \(\mathrm{Cl_2O_7}\). \(\mathrm{ClO_2}\) is used as a bleaching agent.
  • The oxoacids of chlorine are \(\mathrm{HClO}\), \(\mathrm{HClO_2}\), \(\mathrm{HClO_3}\), and \(\mathrm{HClO_4}\), where chlorine has oxidation states \(+1\), \(+3\), \(+5\), and \(+7\) respectively.
  • For oxoacids with the same central atom, acidity increases with the number of oxygen atoms. Therefore, among chlorine oxoacids, \(\mathrm{HClO_4}\) is the strongest because chlorine is in the highest oxidation state and the conjugate base is most stabilised.
  • For hypohalous acids \(\mathrm{HOX}\), the number of oxygen atoms is the same, so acidity follows electronegativity of the halogen: \(\mathrm{HClO>HBrO>HIO}\). This should not be confused with hydrogen-halide acid order.
  • \(\mathrm{I_2O_5}\) is used for estimation of carbon monoxide: \(\mathrm{5CO+I_2O_5\rightarrow I_2+5CO_2}\). This works because \(\mathrm{I_2O_5}\) oxidises \(\mathrm{CO}\) to \(\mathrm{CO_2}\).
  • Stability statements for halogen oxides must be read carefully. In the PYQ set, the statement \(\mathrm{I>Br>Cl}\) as an oxide-stability order was rejected; rely on the exact compound context rather than applying a single memorised order blindly.

    Interhalogen compounds
  • Interhalogen compounds are binary compounds of two different halogens. Their general types are \(\mathrm{XX'}\), \(\mathrm{XX'_3}\), \(\mathrm{XX'_5}\), and \(\mathrm{XX'_7}\), where \(X\) is usually the larger halogen.
  • They are prepared by direct combination of halogens or by the action of a halogen on a lower interhalogen compound. Their reactivity is usually higher than that of the parent halogen molecule because the interhalogen bond is more polar and often weaker.
  • Common structures are VSEPR-based. \(\mathrm{ClF_3}\) is T-shaped, \(\mathrm{BrF_5}\) is square pyramidal, and \(\mathrm{IF_7}\) is pentagonal bipyramidal.
  • Bromine with excess fluorine forms \(\mathrm{BrF_5}\). Since \(\mathrm{BrF_5}\) is \(AX_5E\), it has octahedral electron-pair geometry and square pyramidal molecular shape.
  • Hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{ICl}\) gives \(\mathrm{HOI+HCl}\), not \(\mathrm{HI+HOCl}\). In such products, iodine forms hypoiodous acid and chlorine forms hydrochloric acid.
  • Fluorine-containing interhalogen compounds can act as fluorinating agents. In exam questions, this property is commonly linked with the high electronegativity and reactivity of fluorine.

    Group 18: noble gases and general properties
  • Group 18 elements are called noble gases because they have stable closed-shell configurations. Helium has \(\mathrm{1s^2}\); the others have \(\mathrm{ns^2np^6}\).
  • Their high ionisation enthalpy and very low tendency to gain or lose electrons explain their low chemical reactivity. Xenon forms the most important compounds because its larger size and lower ionisation enthalpy make reaction with fluorine and oxygen possible.
  • Helium is the smallest noble gas and can diffuse through silica glass. It is also used as a diluent for oxygen in modern diving apparatus.
  • Argon is used to provide an inert atmosphere in high-temperature metallurgical processes. Neon is colourless and odourless, but gives a red-orange glow in discharge tubes.
  • Enthalpy of vaporisation of noble gases increases down the group because atomic size and London dispersion forces increase: \(\mathrm{Xe>Kr>Ar>Ne>He}\).

    Xenon fluorides and xenon oxides
  • Xenon reacts with fluorine to form \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\), \(\mathrm{XeF_4}\), and \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\). The product depends on temperature, pressure and xenon-fluorine ratio.
  • \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\), \(\mathrm{XeF_4}\), and \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) are white solids and sublime readily. Their xenon oxidation states are \(+2\), \(+4\), and \(+6\) respectively.
  • Oxidising and fluorinating ability increases with oxidation state and fluorine content: \(\mathrm{XeF_6>XeF_4>XeF_2}\). \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) is the strongest of the three in this comparison.
  • Shape and lone-pair counts are central. \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\) is \(AX_2E_3\), has three lone pairs on Xe and is linear. \(\mathrm{XeF_4}\) has four bond pairs and two lone pairs and is square planar. \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) has six bond pairs and one lone pair and is distorted octahedral.
  • \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\) has maximum lone pair-lone pair repulsion among common choices such as \(\mathrm{ClF_3}\), \(\mathrm{IF_5}\), \(\mathrm{SF_4}\), and \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\), because it has three lone pairs on the central atom.
  • \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) undergoes partial hydrolysis to \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\): \(\mathrm{XeF_6+H_2O\rightarrow XeOF_4+2HF}\). \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\) has five bond pairs and one lone pair, so its shape is square pyramidal.
  • Complete hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) gives \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\): \(\mathrm{XeF_6+3H_2O\rightarrow XeO_3+6HF}\). \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\) has three Xe-O bonding regions and one lone pair, giving pyramidal molecular shape.
  • In bond-counting questions, \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\) is represented with three \(\mathrm{Xe=O}\) bonds, giving \(3\sigma\) and \(3\pi\) bonds. Do not count only sigma bonds.
  • \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\), \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\), and \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) each have one lone pair on xenon. This is a repeated exam point because students often mark zero lone pairs for highly bonded xenon compounds.
  • Hydrolysis/disproportionation of \(\mathrm{XeF_4}\) is balanced as \(\mathrm{6XeF_4+12H_2O\rightarrow 4Xe+2XeO_3+24HF+3O_2}\). Fluorine fixes the \(\mathrm{HF}\) count, and xenon is split between elemental Xe and \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\).
  • Compound Formula Oxidation
    of Xe    		 State of
    Hybridisation    		 Structure
    Xenon difluoride XeF2 +2 sp3d Linear
    Xenon tetrafluoride XeF4 +4 sp3d2 Square planar
    Xenon hexafluoride XeF6 +6 sp3d3 Distorted octahedral*
    Xenon oxydifluoride XeOF2 +4 sp3d T shaped
    Xenon oxytetrafluoride XeOF4 +6 sp3d2 Square pyramidal
    Xenon trioxide XeOF3 +6 sp3 Pyramidal

    VSEPR and molecular-shape integration
  • VSEPR theory should be applied by first counting bond pairs and lone pairs around the central atom, then distinguishing electron-pair geometry from molecular shape.
  • Important examples are: \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\) linear, \(\mathrm{XeF_4}\) square planar, \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) distorted octahedral, \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\) square pyramidal, and \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\) pyramidal.
  • Interhalogen examples are: \(\mathrm{ClF_3}\) T-shaped, \(\mathrm{BrF_5}\) square pyramidal, and \(\mathrm{IF_7}\) pentagonal bipyramidal.
  • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\), \(\mathrm{SnCl_2}\), and \(\mathrm{PbCl_2}\) are bent/angular due to lone-pair effects, while \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\) is linear.
  • When comparing lone pair-lone pair repulsion, count central-atom lone pairs directly. More central lone pairs generally means stronger lone pair-lone pair repulsion.

    Practical uses and identification facts
  • \(\mathrm{I_2O_5}\) is used in estimation of carbon monoxide because it oxidises \(\mathrm{CO}\) to \(\mathrm{CO_2}\): \(\mathrm{5CO+I_2O_5\rightarrow I_2+5CO_2}\).
  • \(\mathrm{O_3}\) is used as a disinfectant and germicide. \(\mathrm{ClO_2}\) is used as a bleaching agent, and \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) is used in preparation of phosgene: \(\mathrm{CO+Cl_2\rightarrow COCl_2}\).
  • Aqua regia dissolves both gold and platinum by forming chloro-complexes. Therefore the statement that gold dissolves in aqua regia but platinum does not is incorrect.
  • Common physical identifications: \(\mathrm{N_2}\) is colourless, \(\mathrm{NO_2}\) is brown, \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) is greenish-yellow, \(\mathrm{PH_3}\) has rotten fish smell, \(\mathrm{H_2S}\) has rotten egg smell, and \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) has a choking pungent smell.
  1. 2025 Q147 Core concept — phosphorus chlorination by sulphuryl chloride and complete hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\). Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{SO_2Cl_2}\) acts as a chlorinating agent, forming \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\); complete hydrolysis gives \(\mathrm{PCl_5+4H_2O\rightarrow H_3PO_4+5HCl}\). Trap: \(\mathrm{POCl_3}\) may appear in partial hydrolysis, but complete hydrolysis ends at \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\). Crux: Sulphuryl chloride converts phosphorus to \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\), and complete hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) gives orthophosphoric acid.
  2. 2025 Q148 Core concept — partial hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) and VSEPR shape of \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\). Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{XeF_6+H_2O\rightarrow XeOF_4+2HF}\); \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\) has \(5\) bond pairs and \(1\) lone pair around xenon, giving square pyramidal shape. Trap: do not call it tetrahedral or linear after identifying five bonded atoms around Xe. Crux: Partial hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) gives \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\), whose shape is square pyramidal.
  3. 2025 Q149 Core concept — basicity of oxoacids. Key formula/fact/rule: basicity of an oxoacid depends on the number of ionisable \(\mathrm{O-H}\) hydrogens, not total hydrogens in the formula. Trap: \(\mathrm{P-H}\) hydrogen is not ionisable, so \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is dibasic, not tribasic. Crux: \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3=HP(=O)(OH)_2}\) has two \(\mathrm{O-H}\) groups and \(\mathrm{H_2SO_4}\) is also dibasic; both have basicity \(2\).
  4. 2025 Q147 Core concept — reducing nature of phosphorus oxoacids. Key formula/fact/rule: reducing character depends on \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds; \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) has two \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds and reduces \(\mathrm{AgNO_3}\) to metallic Ag while being oxidised to \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\). Trap: reducing nature depends on \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds, while basicity depends on \(\mathrm{O-H}\) bonds. Crux: \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) is a strong reducing phosphorus oxoacid and is oxidised to \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).
  5. 2025 Q148 Core concept — reaction of zinc with nitric acid and oxidation numbers of nitrogen oxides. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{Zn}\) with concentrated \(\mathrm{HNO_3}\) gives \(\mathrm{NO_2}\), while with dilute \(\mathrm{HNO_3}\) gives \(\mathrm{N_2O}\); nitrogen oxidation states are \(+4\) in \(\mathrm{NO_2}\) and average \(+1\) in \(\mathrm{N_2O}\). Trap: nitric acid usually gives nitrogen oxides with metals, not \(\mathrm{H_2}\). Crux: \(\mathrm{Zn+conc.\ HNO_3\rightarrow NO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{Zn+dil.\ HNO_3\rightarrow N_2O}\), so oxidation numbers are \(+4,+1\).
  6. 2025 Q149 Core concept — Deacon’s process for chlorine preparation. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{4HCl+O_2\xrightarrow[723\,K]{CuCl_2}2Cl_2+2H_2O}\). Trap: do not confuse Deacon’s process with laboratory preparation of \(\mathrm{HCl}\) from \(\mathrm{NaCl}\) and \(\mathrm{H_2SO_4}\). Crux: Deacon’s process is oxidation of \(\mathrm{HCl}\) to \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) using \(\mathrm{O_2}\), \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\) catalyst, and about \(723\ \mathrm{K}\).
  7. 2025 Q147 Core concept — \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) from thionyl chloride, hydrolysis, shape and basicity. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{P_4+8SOCl_2\rightarrow4PCl_3+4SO_2+2S_2Cl_2}\); \(\mathrm{PCl_3+3H_2O\rightarrow H_3PO_3+3HCl}\); \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) is pyramidal and \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is dibasic. Trap: \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) has one non-ionisable \(\mathrm{P-H}\) hydrogen, so it is not monobasic or tribasic. Crux: Thionyl chloride gives \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\); its hydrolysis gives dibasic \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\), and \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) is pyramidal due to one lone pair on phosphorus.
  8. 2025 Q148 Core concept — chemical properties of chlorine. Key formula/fact/rule: chlorine oxidises ferrous salts to ferric salts, bleaches by oxidation, and is manufactured by Deacon’s process. Trap: chlorine does not directly oxidise iodine to periodic acid in water in this standard statement set. Crux: The incorrect statement is that chlorine oxidises iodine to periodic acid in water.
  9. 2025 Q149 Core concept — nitrogen vs phosphorus chloride formation. Key formula/fact/rule: phosphorus forms \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) and \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\), while nitrogen forms \(\mathrm{NCl_3}\) but not \(\mathrm{NCl_5}\); nitrogen has no vacant \(d\)-orbitals and cannot expand its octet. Trap: higher electronegativity of nitrogen is true but not the correct explanation for absence of \(\mathrm{NCl_5}\). Crux: Assertion and Reason are both correct, but the reason does not explain the assertion; the real deciding factor is inability of nitrogen to expand its octet.
  10. 2025 Q147 Core concept — correct periodic/property trend among p-block compounds. Key formula/fact/rule: acidic nature of nitrogen oxides increases with oxidation state: \(\mathrm{N_2O<NO<N_2O_3<N_2O_4<N_2O_5}\). Trap: group-16 hydride boiling point is distorted by strong hydrogen bonding in \(\mathrm{H_2O}\), hydrogen halide acidity increases as \(\mathrm{HF<HCl<HBr<HI}\), and group-16 hydride bond angle decreases down the group. Crux: The correctly matched order is increasing acidic nature of nitrogen oxides with increasing oxidation state of nitrogen.
  11. 2025 Q148 Core concept — uses of noble gases. Key formula/fact/rule: helium is used as a diluent for oxygen in modern diving apparatus; argon is used to provide inert atmosphere in high-temperature metallurgical processes. Trap: the question asks \(Y\) and \(X\) respectively, so the answer order is \(\mathrm{Ar,He}\), not \(\mathrm{He,Ar}\). Crux: \(X=\mathrm{He}\) for diving and \(Y=\mathrm{Ar}\) for inert metallurgical atmosphere; asked order \(Y,X=\mathrm{Ar,He}\).
  12. 2025 Q149 Core concept — disproportionation of dibasic phosphorus oxoacid. Key formula/fact/rule: the dibasic phosphorus oxoacid is \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\), and on disproportionation it gives \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) and \(\mathrm{PH_3}\). Trap: identify the dibasic acid first from \(\mathrm{O-H}\) count; do not choose \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\), which is monobasic. Crux: \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) disproportionates to phosphoric acid and phosphine, so \(A,B=\mathrm{H_3PO_4,PH_3}\).
  13. 2025 Q147 Core concept — bond-angle order in important p-block molecules/allotropes. Key formula/fact/rule: approximate angles are \(\mathrm{P_4}\approx60^\circ\), \(\mathrm{S_6}\approx102.2^\circ\), \(\mathrm{S_8}\approx107^\circ\), and \(\mathrm{O_3}\approx116.8^\circ\). Trap: \(\mathrm{P_4}\) must be the smallest due to tetrahedral cage strain, while \(\mathrm{O_3}\) is the largest among the given set. Crux: Increasing bond-angle order is \(\mathrm{P_4<S_6<S_8<O_3}\), i.e. \(B<C<A<D\).
  14. 2025 Q148 Core concept — thermal decomposition of ammonium dichromate. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{(NH_4)_2Cr_2O_7\xrightarrow{\Delta}N_2+Cr_2O_3+4H_2O}\). Trap: the chromium product is \(\mathrm{Cr_2O_3}\), not \(\mathrm{CrO_3}\), and nitrogen gas is formed, not \(\mathrm{NO_2}\) or \(\mathrm{N_2O}\). Crux: Thermal decomposition of ammonium dichromate gives \(\mathrm{N_2}\), \(\mathrm{Cr_2O_3}\), and \(\mathrm{H_2O}\).
  15. 2025 Q149 Core concept — formation of sulphuryl chloride and properties of \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\). Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{SO_2+Cl_2\xrightarrow{charcoal}SO_2Cl_2}\), and sulphuryl chloride reacts with white phosphorus to give \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\); solid \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) exists as \((\mathrm{PCl_4})^+(\mathrm{PCl_6})^-\). Trap: gaseous \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) is trigonal bipyramidal with non-equivalent axial/equatorial bonds, and hydrolysis gives \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\), not \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\). Crux: \(B=\mathrm{PCl_5}\), and the correct statement is that solid \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) is ionic.
  16. 2025 Q147 Core concept — acidification of salts and formation of multiple oxides. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{NaNO_2+HCl\rightarrow NaCl+HNO_2}\), and unstable \(\mathrm{HNO_2}\) can decompose to nitrogen oxides such as \(\mathrm{NO}\) and \(\mathrm{NO_2}\). Trap: carbonates/bicarbonates give mainly \(\mathrm{CO_2}\), and sulphites give mainly \(\mathrm{SO_2}\); nitrite is the one that can lead to more than one oxide. Crux: \(\mathrm{NaNO_2}\) gives more number of oxides on reaction with \(\mathrm{HCl}\) because it first forms unstable nitrous acid.
  17. 2025 Q148 Core concept — lone-pair count on xenon in xenon oxides/oxyfluorides/fluorides. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\), \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\), and \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) each have one lone pair on the central xenon atom. Trap: \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) still has one lone pair; do not mark zero just because it has six Xe–F bonds. Crux: The lone-pair sequence on Xe in \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\), \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\), and \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) is \(1,1,1\).
  18. 2025 Q149 Core concept — properties and physical state of group-16 oxides. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) is a reducing agent, \(\mathrm{SeO_3}\) is acidic, and \(\mathrm{SeO_2}\) is a colourless solid at room temperature. Trap: the false statement is a physical-state trap; \(\mathrm{SeO_2}\) is not a gas. Crux: The incorrect statement is “\(\mathrm{SeO_2}\) is a gas”; selenium dioxide is a solid.
  1. 2024 Q147 Core concept — thermal decomposition products that liberate dinitrogen. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{2NaNO_3 \xrightarrow{\Delta} 2NaNO_2+O_2}\), \(\mathrm{(NH_4)_2Cr_2O_7 \xrightarrow{\Delta} Cr_2O_3+N_2+4H_2O}\), and \(\mathrm{Ba(N_3)_2 \xrightarrow{\Delta} Ba+3N_2}\). Trap: do not assume every nitrogen-containing compound gives \(\mathrm{N_2}\) on heating; sodium nitrate gives oxygen, not dinitrogen. Crux: Ammonium dichromate and barium azide liberate \(\mathrm{N_2}\), while sodium nitrate does not; answer is \((ii),(iii)\) only.
  2. 2024 Q148 Core concept — Deacon’s process for chlorine preparation. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{4HCl+O_2\xrightarrow[723\,K]{CuCl_2}2Cl_2+2H_2O}\) is catalytic oxidation of \(\mathrm{HCl}\) to \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\). Trap: do not confuse this with metallurgy processes such as Hall-Heroult, van-Arkel, or Serpeck’s process. Crux: \(\mathrm{HCl}\) oxidised by \(\mathrm{O_2}\) in presence of \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\) at about \(723\ \mathrm{K}\) is Deacon’s process.
  3. 2024 Q149 Core concept — physical properties and odours of important p-block gases. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{PH_3}\) is colourless with rotten fish smell, \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) is greenish-yellow with pungent smell, \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) is colourless with pungent/choking smell, and \(\mathrm{Ne}\) is colourless and odourless under ordinary conditions. Trap: rotten egg smell belongs to \(\mathrm{H_2S}\), not neon; discharge colour is not the normal colour of the gas. Crux: The incorrectly matched set is \(\mathrm{Ne}\) as fluorescent green gas with rotten egg smell; neon is colourless, odourless, and gives red-orange glow in discharge tubes.
  4. 2024 Q147 Core concept — preparation of phosphine from white phosphorus and its reaction with copper sulphate. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{P_4+3NaOH+3H_2O\rightarrow PH_3+3NaH_2PO_2}\), and \(\mathrm{4PH_3+6CuSO_4\rightarrow 2Cu_3P_2+6H_2SO_4}\). Trap: after identifying \(X=\mathrm{PH_3}\), do not choose copper phosphate, hydroxide, or carbonate; the product with copper sulphate is copper phosphide. Crux: White phosphorus with alkali gives phosphine, and phosphine with \(\mathrm{CuSO_4}\) gives \(\mathrm{Cu_3P_2}\); hence \(Y=\mathrm{Cu_3P_2}\).
  5. 2024 Q148 Core concept — arsenic purifier in the contact process for sulphuric acid. Key formula/fact/rule: arsenic impurities poison the \(\mathrm{V_2O_5}\) catalyst, and the arsenic purifier contains hydrated ferric oxide, \(\mathrm{Fe_2O_3\cdot xH_2O}\). Trap: do not confuse ferric oxide hydrate with alumina hydrate, ferrous oxide hydrate, or chromium oxide hydrate. Crux: In the contact process, arsenic compounds are removed before the catalytic chamber using \(\mathrm{Fe_2O_3\cdot xH_2O}\).
  6. 2024 Q148 Core concept — boiling point trend of group-15 hydrides. Key formula/fact/rule: boiling point generally increases down the group due to increasing molar mass and dispersion forces, while \(\mathrm{NH_3}\) is anomalously high compared with \(\mathrm{PH_3}\) due to hydrogen bonding. Trap: do not make \(\mathrm{NH_3}\) the highest when very heavy \(\mathrm{BiH_3}\) is included; also do not ignore the hydrogen-bonding rise of \(\mathrm{NH_3}\) over \(\mathrm{PH_3}\). Crux: Among \(\mathrm{NH_3}\), \(\mathrm{PH_3}\), and \(\mathrm{BiH_3}\), the order is \(\mathrm{PH_3<NH_3<BiH_3}\); highest \(X=\mathrm{BiH_3}\), lowest \(Y=\mathrm{PH_3}\).
  7. 2024 Q149 Core concept — complete hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) and bond counting in \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\). Key formula/fact/rule: complete hydrolysis of xenon(VI) fluoride gives \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\), commonly represented with three \(\mathrm{Xe=O}\) bonds; each double bond has one \(\sigma\) and one \(\pi\) bond. Trap: do not stop at partial hydrolysis products such as \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\), and do not count only sigma bonds. Crux: \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) on complete hydrolysis gives \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\); three \(\mathrm{Xe=O}\) bonds give \(3\sigma+3\pi=6\) total bonds.
  8. 2024 Q148 Core concept — hydration of phosphorus oxides and \(\mathrm{P=O}\) bond count in oxoacids. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{P_2O_3+3H_2O\rightarrow 2H_3PO_3}\) and \(\mathrm{P_4O_{10}+6H_2O\rightarrow 4H_3PO_4}\); \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3=HP(=O)(OH)_2}\) and \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4=P(=O)(OH)_3}\). Trap: do not count total oxygen atoms as \(\mathrm{P=O}\) bonds; each product molecule has only one terminal \(\mathrm{P=O}\) bond. Crux: \(X=\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) and \(Y=\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\), and both contain one \(\mathrm{P=O}\) bond; answer is \(1,1\).
  9. 2024 Q149 Core concept — oxidising action of hot concentrated sulphuric acid on carbon and nature of products. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{C+2H_2SO_4(conc.)\xrightarrow{\Delta}CO_2+2SO_2+2H_2O}\); both \(\mathrm{CO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) are acidic oxides. Trap: do not classify \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) as basic or neutral just because sulphuric acid is reduced to it. Crux: Hot concentrated \(\mathrm{H_2SO_4}\) oxidises carbon to \(\mathrm{CO_2}\) and itself reduces to \(\mathrm{SO_2}\); both oxides are acidic.
  10. 2024 Q147 Core concept — products of carbon with hot concentrated sulphuric acid. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{C+2H_2SO_4(conc.)\xrightarrow{\Delta}CO_2+2SO_2+2H_2O}\); carbon is oxidised to \(\mathrm{CO_2}\), while sulphur in \(\mathrm{H_2SO_4}\) is reduced from \(+6\) to \(+4\) in \(\mathrm{SO_2}\). Trap: do not choose \(\mathrm{CO}\) or \(\mathrm{SO_3}\); concentrated sulphuric acid acts as an oxidising agent here and forms \(\mathrm{SO_2}\). Crux: In reaction with hot concentrated \(\mathrm{H_2SO_4}\), \(X,Y=\mathrm{CO_2,SO_2}\).
  11. 2024 Q148 Core concept — \(\mathrm{P-O-P}\) linkage in condensed phosphorus oxoacids. Key formula/fact/rule: pyrophosphorous acid \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_5}\), pyrophosphoric acid \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_7}\), and cyclic metaphosphoric acid \((\mathrm{HPO_3})_3\) contain \(\mathrm{P-O-P}\) bonds, while hypophosphoric acid \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_6}\) contains a direct \(\mathrm{P-P}\) bond instead. Trap: the chemically complete set is I, III and IV; if options force only a two-item pair, the provided options are inconsistent with the complete structural fact. Crux: \(\mathrm{P-O-P}\) bonds occur in condensed/pyro/metaphosphoric structures such as \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_5}\), \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_7}\), and \((\mathrm{HPO_3})_3\); \(\mathrm{H_4P_2O_6}\) has \(\mathrm{P-P}\), not \(\mathrm{P-O-P}\).
  12. 2024 Q149 Core concept — bond angles in planar nitric acid structure. Key formula/fact/rule: in \(\mathrm{HNO_3}\), the \(\mathrm{H-O-N}\) angle is about \(102^\circ\) and the \(\mathrm{O-N-O}\) angle is about \(130^\circ\). Trap: do not reverse the two angles; the angle at oxygen in \(\mathrm{H-O-N}\) is smaller, while the angle around nitrogen between oxygens is larger due to trigonal planar arrangement and resonance. Crux: For nitric acid, \(\angle\mathrm{H-O-N}=102^\circ\) and \(\angle\mathrm{O-N-O}=130^\circ\).
  1. 2023 Q145 Core concept — identification of phosphine and reactions that do or do not produce it. Key formula/fact/rule: \(\mathrm{P_4+3NaOH+3H_2O\rightarrow PH_3+3NaH_2PO_2}\), so \(Q=\mathrm{PH_3}\); metal phosphides, heated \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\), and phosphonium salts can give \(\mathrm{PH_3}\), but \(\mathrm{PCl_3+3H_2O\rightarrow H_3PO_3+3HCl}\). Trap: hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) gives phosphorous acid, not phosphine. Crux: \(Q\) is phosphine; the reaction in which \(Q\) is not formed is hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\).
  2. 2023 Q146 Core concept — oxidation state and \(\mathrm{S-OH}\) bonds in peroxydisulphuric acid. Key formula/fact/rule: peroxydisulphuric acid is \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_8}\) with structure \(\mathrm{HO-S(=O)_2-O-O-S(=O)_2-OH}\); peroxide oxygens are \(-1\), so both sulphur atoms are \(+6\), and there are two \(\mathrm{S-OH}\) bonds. Trap: do not treat all eight oxygens as \(-2\), and do not count four \(\mathrm{S-OH}\) bonds from the formula. Crux: In \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_8}\), sulphur oxidation states are \((+6,+6)\), and the number of \(\mathrm{S-OH}\) bonds is \(2\).
  3. 2023 Q147 Core concept — correct statements about aqua regia, chlorine oxoacids, hydrogen halides and halogen oxides.   4. 2023 Q148 Core concept — preparation, hydrolysis and structures of xenon(VI) fluoride. Key formula/fact/rule: under excess fluorine conditions, xenon forms \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\); partial hydrolysis gives \(Y=\mathrm{XeOF_4}\), and complete hydrolysis gives \(Z=\mathrm{XeO_3}\). \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\) is square pyramidal, while \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\) has \(3\sigma\), \(3\pi\) bonds and one lone pair on Xe. Trap: do not call \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\) linear, and do not call \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\) tetrahedral in molecular shape. Crux: Correct statements are I and III only.
  5. 2023 Q130 Core concept — chemical name and use of calgon. Key formula/fact/rule: calgon is sodium hexametaphosphate, used for softening hard water by complexing \(\mathrm{Ca^{2+}}\) and \(\mathrm{Mg^{2+}}\) ions. Trap: do not confuse phosphate with phosphite, and do not replace sodium with potassium or calcium. Crux: Calgon \(=\) sodium hexametaphosphate.
  6. 2023 Q145 Core concept — nitrogen pentahalides, oxidation state and covalency. Trap: oxidation state and covalency are not the same; \(+5\) oxidation state does not explain pentahalide formation. Crux: Assertion and Reason are both true, but the Reason is not the correct explanation of the Assertion.
  7. 2023 Q146 Core concept — increasing bond-angle order in \(\mathrm{P_4}\), \(\mathrm{S_6}\), \(\mathrm{S_8}\), and \(\mathrm{O_3}\). Crux: The correct increasing order is \(\mathrm{P_4<S_6<S_8<O_3}\).
  8. 2023 Q147 Core concept — Deacon’s method for preparation of chlorine.   10. 2023 Q145 Core concept — trends in group-15 hydrides.   11. 2023 Q146 Core concept — strongest reducing agent among group-16 oxides.   12. 2023 Q147 Core concept — VSEPR shapes of xenon and interhalogen compounds.
