Trap: do not call \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\) planar or \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\) square planar. Crux: \(\mathrm{XeO_3}\) is pyramidal and \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\) is square pyramidal.
  22. 2022 Q148 Core concept — peroxy bond in sulphur oxoacids. Key formula/fact/rule: peroxydisulphuric acid \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_8}\) contains an \(\mathrm{O-O}\) peroxy bond; \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_7}\) contains an \(\mathrm{S-O-S}\) bridge, not a peroxy bond. Trap: do not confuse pyro linkage with peroxy linkage. Crux: The oxoacid with a peroxy bond is \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_8}\).
  23. 2022 Q149 Core concept — oxidation of excess ammonia by chlorine. Key formula/fact/rule: excess ammonia reacts with chlorine as \(\mathrm{8NH_3+3Cl_2\rightarrow N_2+6NH_4Cl}\); therefore \(3\) moles of \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) oxidise \(8\) moles of \(\mathrm{NH_3}\). Trap: the question asks moles of \(\mathrm{NH_3}\) oxidised by \(1\) mole of \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\), so divide \(8\) by \(3\). Crux: For \(1\) mole of chlorine, \(Z=\dfrac{8}{3}\) moles of ammonia.
  24. 2022 Q150 Core concept — enthalpy of vaporisation of noble gases. Key formula/fact/rule: enthalpy of vaporisation increases down group 18 because atomic size, molar mass, and London dispersion forces increase. Trap: do not place helium or neon above heavier noble gases; their dispersion forces are weakest. Crux: The correct order is \(\mathrm{Xe>Kr>Ar>Ne>He}\).
    2025
  1. TTR imp 1
    • Correct answer: (c) \(\mathrm{PCl_5, H_3PO_4}\) p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR

      Solution:

    • Explanation: Phosphorus reacts with sulphuryl chloride to form phosphorus pentachloride, and on complete hydrolysis \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) finally gives orthophosphoric acid \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • The first part of the question asks what compound is formed when phosphorus reacts with sulphuryl chloride \((\mathrm{SO_2Cl_2})\).
      • \(\mathrm{SO_2Cl_2}\) acts as a chlorinating agent, so phosphorus gets converted into phosphorus pentachloride:
        • \(\mathrm{P + SO_2Cl_2 \rightarrow PCl_5}\)
      • So, \(X = \mathrm{PCl_5}\).
      • Now the question says that \(X\) undergoes complete hydrolysis.
      • When \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) is hydrolysed fully with water, all the chlorine atoms are ultimately replaced and the final product becomes orthophosphoric acid:
        • \(\mathrm{PCl_5 + 4H_2O \rightarrow H_3PO_4 + 5HCl}\)
      • So, \(Y = \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This is a reaction-based inorganic chemistry question from phosphorus compounds.
      • \(\mathrm{SO_2Cl_2}\) is commonly used as a source of chlorine in chlorination reactions.
      • \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) is a well-known covalent chloride of phosphorus.
      • On hydrolysis, phosphorus chlorides give oxyacids of phosphorus or related oxygen-containing products depending on the extent of hydrolysis.
      • The word complete hydrolysis is the key clue here. It tells us to think about the final stable hydrolysis product, not an intermediate.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (c) correctly identifies \(X\) as \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\).
      • It also correctly identifies the final hydrolysis product \(Y\) as \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).
    • Why other options are wrong
      • (a) is wrong because the reaction does not give \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) here, and complete hydrolysis of the required product does not stop at \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\).
      • (b) is wrong because \(\mathrm{POCl_3}\) can appear in partial hydrolysis pathways, but the question clearly asks for complete hydrolysis, whose final product is \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).
      • (d) is wrong because \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) is hypophosphorous acid and is not the product formed by complete hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\).
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Whenever you see complete hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\), immediately think of \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).
      • Do not get trapped by intermediate products like \(\mathrm{POCl_3}\).
      • In such questions, words like partial and complete completely change the final answer.
    • Final result
      • \(X = \mathrm{PCl_5}\)
      • \(Y = \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\)
      • Hence, the correct option is (c).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic
      • Chapter: \(p\)-Block Elements
      • Topic: Phosphorus and its compounds
      • Subtopic: Chlorides of phosphorus and hydrolysis reactions
    • Correct answer: (c) square pyramidal

      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
      Chemical Bonding and Molecular Structure TTR

      Solution:

    • Explanation: On partial hydrolysis, \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) forms \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\), and the shape of \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\) is square pyramidal.
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • The question says xenon hexafluoride undergoes partial hydrolysis.
      • The relevant reaction is:
        • \(\mathrm{XeF_6 + H_2O \rightarrow XeOF_4 + 2HF}\)
      • So the compound \(X\) is \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\).
      • Now we find the shape of \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\).
      • In \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\), xenon is the central atom.
      • It is bonded to one oxygen atom and four fluorine atoms, so there are 5 bonded atoms around xenon.
      • Xenon also has 1 lone pair in this molecule.
      • That means the steric number is \(6\), corresponding to an octahedral electron-pair arrangement.
      • Out of these six positions, one is occupied by a lone pair and five are occupied by atoms.
      • When an octahedral arrangement has one lone pair, the molecular shape becomes square pyramidal.
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This is an application of hydrolysis of noble gas compounds plus VSEPR theory.
      • \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) is one of the important xenon fluorides.
      • On hydrolysis, xenon fluorides form oxyfluorides or oxides depending on how far the hydrolysis proceeds.
      • For partial hydrolysis, \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) gives \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\).
      • According to VSEPR:
        • \(6\) electron domains \(\rightarrow\) octahedral electron geometry
        • \(5\) bond pairs + \(1\) lone pair \(\rightarrow\) square pyramidal shape
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (c) is correct because \(X = \mathrm{XeOF_4}\).
      • \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\) has five bonded atoms and one lone pair around xenon.
      • This gives a square pyramidal molecular shape.
    • Why other options are wrong
      • (a) pyramidal is too vague and not the correct specific geometry asked here.
      • (b) tetrahedral is impossible because there are more than four electron domains around xenon.
      • (d) linear is clearly wrong because the molecule has five atoms bonded to the central atom, not two.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Memorise these common xenon hydrolysis products:
        • \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\), \(\mathrm{XeF_4}\), \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\)
        • Partial hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) gives \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\)
      • Then apply VSEPR quickly: \(5\) bonds + \(1\) lone pair means square pyramidal.
      • In exams, once you identify \(\mathrm{XeOF_4}\), the shape question becomes direct.
    • Why the given figure fits the answer
      • The oxygen occupies one position above the square base.
      • The four fluorine atoms form the square plane around xenon.
      • The lone pair occupies the remaining octahedral position, giving the square pyramidal arrangement.
    • Final result
      • \(X = \mathrm{XeOF_4}\)
      • Shape of \(X\) = square pyramidal
      • Hence, the correct option is (c).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic
      • Chapter: \(p\)-Block Elements
      • Topic: Noble gases and xenon compounds
      • Subtopic: Hydrolysis and shapes of xenon fluorides / oxyfluorides
    • Correct answer: (a) \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3, H_2SO_4}\)
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
      Chemical Equilibrium and Acids-Bases TTR

      Solution:

    • Explanation: Basicity of an oxoacid depends on the number of ionisable hydrogen atoms attached to oxygen. Both \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) and \(\mathrm{H_2SO_4}\) have two such hydrogens, so both have basicity \(2\).
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • The key idea is that basicity of an oxoacid means the number of replaceable or ionisable \(\mathrm{H^+}\) ions it can give in solution.
      • In oxoacids, only those hydrogens which are attached to oxygen are ionisable.
      • Hydrogens directly attached to the central atom like \(\mathrm{P-H}\) are not ionisable in the usual acid-base sense.
      • Now check each acid carefully.
      • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) has the structure \(\mathrm{HPO(OH)_2}\).
      • That means it has:
        • two \(\mathrm{O-H}\) bonds
        • one \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bond
      • Only the two \(\mathrm{O-H}\) hydrogens are ionisable, so basicity of \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is \(2\).
      • \(\mathrm{H_2SO_4}\) has two acidic hydrogens attached through oxygen, so its basicity is also \(2\).
      • Thus the pair in option (a) both has basicity \(2\).
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • For oxoacids, basicity is not equal to the total number of hydrogen atoms written in the molecular formula.
      • It depends only on the number of hydrogens bonded to oxygen as \(\mathrm{O-H}\).
      • This is a very important exam rule for phosphorus oxoacids.
      • Common examples:
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\): one \(\mathrm{O-H}\) bond \(\rightarrow\) basicity \(1\)
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\): two \(\mathrm{O-H}\) bonds \(\rightarrow\) basicity \(2\)
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\): three \(\mathrm{O-H}\) bonds \(\rightarrow\) basicity \(3\)
    • Why the correct option is right
      • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is dibasic because it has two \(\mathrm{O-H}\) groups.
      • \(\mathrm{H_2SO_4}\) is also dibasic because it can release two \(\mathrm{H^+}\) ions.
      • So option (a) is the only pair in which both acids have basicity \(2\).
    • Why other options are wrong
      • (b) \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) has basicity \(1\), while \(\mathrm{H_2SO_3}\) has basicity \(2\). So both are not \(2\).
      • (c) \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) has basicity \(3\), and \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) has basicity \(1\).
      • (d) \(\mathrm{H_2S_2O_8}\) is dibasic, but \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) is monobasic, so this pair also fails.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • For phosphorus oxoacids, always draw or remember the structural pattern first.
      • Count only \(\mathrm{O-H}\) hydrogens, never total hydrogens.
      • A fast memory trick:
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) \(\rightarrow\) \(1\)
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) \(\rightarrow\) \(2\)
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) \(\rightarrow\) \(3\)
    • Final result
      • Basicity of \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3} = 2\)
      • Basicity of \(\mathrm{H_2SO_4} = 2\)
      • Hence, the correct option is (a).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic
      • Chapter: \(p\)-Block Elements
      • Topic: Oxoacids of phosphorus and sulphur
      • Subtopic: Basicity of oxoacids

    Answer: \((b)\ \mathrm{H_3PO_2},\ \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\)

    p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR

    Solution:

    The question says that an oxoacid of phosphorus \(X\) reduces silver nitrate solution to metallic silver and itself gets oxidised to another compound \(Y\).

    So \(X\) must be a reducing agent.

    1. Use the NCERT rule for phosphorus oxoacids

      The reducing character of an oxoacid of phosphorus depends on the presence of \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds, not on \(\mathrm{O-H}\) bonds.

      Important examples are:

      • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\): contains \(2\) \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds, so it is a strong reducing agent
      • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\): contains \(1\) \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bond, so it is also reducing
      • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\): contains no \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bond, so it is not reducing

      Since the question asks for an acid that reduces \(\mathrm{AgNO_3}\) to metallic silver, the best fit is \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\).

    2. Identify the oxidation product

      Hypophosphorous acid \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) gets oxidised to phosphoric acid \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).

      So:

      \(X = \mathrm{H_3PO_2}\)

      \(Y = \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\)

      Therefore the correct answer is:

      \(\boxed{(b)\ \mathrm{H_3PO_2},\ \mathrm{H_3PO_4}}\)

    3. Check quickly by oxidation state

      In \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\), let oxidation state of phosphorus be \(x\):

      \(x + 3(+1) + 2(-2) = 0\)

      \(x + 3 - 4 = 0\)

      \(x = +1\)

      In \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\):

      \(x + 3(+1) + 4(-2) = 0\)

      \(x + 3 - 8 = 0\)

      \(x = +5\)

      So phosphorus is oxidised from \(+1\) to \(+5\), which confirms that \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) acts as a reducing agent and is oxidised to \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).

    Why this method works:

    • In phosphorus oxoacids, the key factor for reducing character is the number of \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds.
    • More \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds generally means stronger reducing behavior.
    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) has two \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds, so it is more reducing than \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\).
    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) has no \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bond, so it does not act as a reducing acid.
    • Since silver nitrate is reduced to metallic silver, the acid must be one that can itself get oxidised, and \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) satisfies this condition.

    JEE / NCERT takeaway:

    • Reducing character of phosphorus oxoacids depends on \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds.
    • Basicity of phosphorus oxoacids depends on the number of ionisable \(\mathrm{O-H}\) bonds.
    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) is monobasic because it has only one \(\mathrm{O-H}\) bond.
    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is dibasic because it has two \(\mathrm{O-H}\) bonds.
    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) is tribasic because it has three \(\mathrm{O-H}\) bonds.
    • Order of reducing character is:

      \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2} > \mathrm{H_3PO_3} > \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\)

    Structural understanding:

    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\):

      one \(\mathrm{P=O}\), one \(\mathrm{P-OH}\), and two \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds

    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\):

      one \(\mathrm{P=O}\), two \(\mathrm{P-OH}\), and one \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bond

    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\):

      one \(\mathrm{P=O}\), three \(\mathrm{P-OH}\), and zero \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds

    Why the other options are wrong:

    1. \((a)\ \mathrm{HPO_3},\ \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\)

      \(\mathrm{HPO_3}\) does not have the required \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bond needed for this reducing behavior.

    2. \((c)\ \mathrm{H_3PO_3},\ \mathrm{H_3PO_2}\)

      \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is reducing, but conversion to \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) would represent reduction, not oxidation.

    3. \((d)\ \mathrm{H_3PO_2},\ \mathrm{HNO_3}\)

      If a phosphorus oxoacid gets oxidised, the product should still be a phosphorus compound, not nitric acid.

    1-minute solving trick:

    • See the phrase “reduces silver nitrate to metallic silver” and immediately think of a reducing phosphorus acid.
    • Then recall:

      \(\mathrm{P-H}\ \text{bond} \Rightarrow \text{reducing nature}\)

    • Among common oxoacids:

      \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) has the maximum reducing character

    • Oxidation product is commonly:

      \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\)

    • So you can directly mark:

      \(\boxed{(b)}\)

    Common mistakes to avoid:

    1. Judging reducing character from total number of hydrogens in the formula instead of counting \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds.
    2. Confusing basicity with reducing nature.
    3. Thinking that \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) is reducing just because it has more oxygen atoms.
    4. Choosing an option where the oxidation product belongs to another element, such as \(\mathrm{HNO_3}\).

    Chapter / Topic / Subtopic:

    • Chapter: The p-Block Elements
    • Topic: Oxoacids of phosphorus
    • Subtopic: Reducing character of phosphorus oxoacids and relation with \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds

    Final exam-style answer:

    The phosphorus oxoacid that reduces silver nitrate solution to metallic silver is \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\), because it contains \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds and acts as a reducing agent.

    On oxidation, it forms \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).

    Therefore:

    \(\boxed{(b)\ \mathrm{H_3PO_2},\ \mathrm{H_3PO_4}}\)

    Quick cheatsheet:

    • Reducing nature depends on:

      \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bonds

    • Basicity depends on:

      \(\mathrm{O-H}\) bonds

    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\):

      monobasic, strong reducing acid

    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\):

      dibasic, reducing acid

    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\):

      tribasic, non-reducing

    • Order of reducing character:

      \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2} > \mathrm{H_3PO_3} > \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\)

    • Correct pair here:

      \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2},\ \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\)

    Answer: \((a)\ +4,\ +1\)

    p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    Stoichiometry TTR

    Solution:

    1. Identify the oxide formed with concentrated nitric acid

      Zinc with concentrated nitric acid gives nitrogen dioxide:

      \(\mathrm{Zn + 4HNO_3 \rightarrow Zn(NO_3)_2 + 2NO_2 + 2H_2O}\)

      So, \((A) = \mathrm{NO_2}\).

    2. Identify the oxide formed with dilute nitric acid

      Zinc with dilute nitric acid gives nitrous oxide:

      \(\mathrm{4Zn + 10HNO_3 \rightarrow 4Zn(NO_3)_2 + N_2O + 5H_2O}\)

      So, \((B) = \mathrm{N_2O}\).

    3. Find oxidation number of nitrogen in \((A)\)

      For \(\mathrm{NO_2}\):

      Let oxidation number of nitrogen \(= x\)

      \(x + 2(-2) = 0\)

      \(x - 4 = 0\)

      \(x = +4\)

    4. Find oxidation number of nitrogen in \((B)\)

      For \(\mathrm{N_2O}\):

      Let oxidation number of each nitrogen \(= x\)

      \(2x + (-2) = 0\)

      \(2x = +2\)

      \(x = +1\)

    Therefore:

    \(\boxed{(A) = +4,\ (B) = +1}\)

    \(\boxed{(a)\ +4,\ +1}\)

    Why this happens:

    • Nitric acid is a strong oxidising agent.
    • With metals like zinc, \(\mathrm{HNO_3}\) is reduced to different nitrogen oxides depending on concentration.
    • Concentrated \(\mathrm{HNO_3}\) usually gives a higher oxidation-state oxide such as \(\mathrm{NO_2}\), where nitrogen is \(+4\).
    • Dilute \(\mathrm{HNO_3}\) can reduce further and may give \(\mathrm{N_2O}\), where average oxidation state of nitrogen is \(+1\).

    JEE / NCERT takeaway:

    • Metals with nitric acid generally do not evolve \(\mathrm{H_2}\), because \(\mathrm{HNO_3}\) acts as an oxidising agent.
    • Different reduction products of nitric acid include:

      \(\mathrm{NO_2,\ NO,\ N_2O,\ N_2,\ NH_4NO_3}\)

    • The product depends on:
      • nature of metal
      • concentration of \(\mathrm{HNO_3}\)
      • temperature
    • Oxidation number is calculated using the rule:

      Sum of oxidation numbers \(=\) overall charge of species

    • In most oxides, oxygen has oxidation number \(-2\).

    1-minute solving trick:

    • Memorise the common result:

      \(\mathrm{Zn + conc.\ HNO_3 \rightarrow NO_2}\)

      \(\mathrm{Zn + dil.\ HNO_3 \rightarrow N_2O}\)

    • Then quickly calculate:

      \(\mathrm{NO_2} \Rightarrow N = +4\)

      \(\mathrm{N_2O} \Rightarrow\) average \(N = +1\)

    • For \(\mathrm{N}_{x}\mathrm{O}_{y}\), use fast formula:

      \(\text{Oxidation state of N} = \frac{2y}{x}\)

      So for \(\mathrm{N_2O}\): \(\frac{2\times 1}{2} = +1\)

    Common mistakes to avoid:

    1. Confusing \(\mathrm{NO_2}\) with \(\mathrm{NO}\). In \(\mathrm{NO}\), nitrogen is \(+2\), not \(+4\).
    2. Forgetting that in \(\mathrm{N_2O}\), the value \(+1\) is the average oxidation state of nitrogen.
    3. Assuming nitric acid behaves like ordinary acids and gives \(\mathrm{H_2}\) with zinc.

    Chapter / Topic / Subtopic:

    • Chapter: General Principles and Processes of Isolation of Elements / Redox Reactions / p-Block basic inorganic reactions
    • Topic: Oxidising nature of nitric acid and oxidation number
    • Subtopic: Products formed when metals react with concentrated and dilute \(\mathrm{HNO_3}\)

    Quick exam-style answer:

    Zinc reacts with concentrated nitric acid to give \(\mathrm{NO_2}\), so \((A)=\mathrm{NO_2}\). Zinc reacts with dilute nitric acid to give \(\mathrm{N_2O}\), so \((B)=\mathrm{N_2O}\).

    In \(\mathrm{NO_2}\): \(x+2(-2)=0 \Rightarrow x=+4\)

    In \(\mathrm{N_2O}\): \(2x+(-2)=0 \Rightarrow x=+1\)

    Hence, the correct answer is \(\boxed{(a)\ +4,\ +1}\).

    Answer: \((b)\)

    p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR

    Solution:

    Deacon's process is the industrial method used for the preparation of chlorine gas from hydrogen chloride.

    The reaction is:

    \(4\mathrm{HCl} + \mathrm{O_2} \xrightarrow[\mathrm{723\ K}]{\mathrm{CuCl_2}} 2\mathrm{Cl_2} + 2\mathrm{H_2O}\)

    1. Identify what Deacon's process does

      In Deacon's process, hydrogen chloride is oxidised by oxygen to form chlorine gas.

      So the key pattern to remember is:

      \(\mathrm{HCl} \rightarrow \mathrm{Cl_2}\)

    2. Match the given options
      • Option \((a)\) shows reaction of chlorine with water in sunlight, so it is not Deacon's process.
      • Option \((b)\) shows:

        \(4\mathrm{HCl} + \mathrm{O_2} \xrightarrow[\mathrm{723\ K}]{\mathrm{CuCl_2}} 2\mathrm{Cl_2} + 2\mathrm{H_2O}\)

        This is exactly Deacon's process.

      • Option \((c)\) is laboratory preparation of \(\mathrm{HCl}\) from sodium chloride and sulphuric acid.
      • Option \((d)\) is a different reaction involving sodium thiosulphate and chlorine.
    3. Therefore

      The correct reaction related to Deacon's process is:

      \(\boxed{4\mathrm{HCl} + \mathrm{O_2} \xrightarrow[\mathrm{723\ K}]{\mathrm{CuCl_2}} 2\mathrm{Cl_2} + 2\mathrm{H_2O}}\)

      Hence, the correct answer is \(\boxed{(b)}\).

    Why this reaction is called Deacon's process:

    • It is used to manufacture chlorine by oxidation of hydrogen chloride.
    • Oxygen acts as the oxidising agent.
    • \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\) acts as a catalyst.
    • The reaction is carried out at about \(723\ \mathrm{K}\).

    Reaction idea in simple form:

    • Chloride in \(\mathrm{HCl}\) has oxidation state \(-1\).
    • In \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\), chlorine has oxidation state \(0\).
    • So chloride is oxidised:

      \(\mathrm{Cl^-} \rightarrow \mathrm{Cl_2}\)

    JEE / NCERT takeaway:

    • Deacon's process:

      \(4\mathrm{HCl} + \mathrm{O_2} \xrightarrow[\mathrm{723\ K}]{\mathrm{CuCl_2}} 2\mathrm{Cl_2} + 2\mathrm{H_2O}\)

    • It is an important reaction in the chemistry of chlorine.
    • \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\) is the catalyst commonly associated with this reaction.
    • This is an oxidation of hydrogen chloride to chlorine.
    • Many exam questions ask you to identify the reaction just by spotting:

      \(\mathrm{HCl + O_2 \rightarrow Cl_2 + H_2O}\)

    1-minute solving trick:

    • Memorise one line:

      Deacon's process = preparation of \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) from \(\mathrm{HCl}\)

    • So in options, directly look for:

      \(\mathrm{HCl}\) on reactant side and \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) on product side

    • If the option also contains \(\mathrm{O_2}\), \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\), and about \(723\ \mathrm{K}\), it is almost certainly the correct one.

    Common mistakes to avoid:

    1. Confusing Deacon's process with laboratory preparation of \(\mathrm{HCl}\).
    2. Confusing chlorine-water reactions with industrial manufacture of chlorine.
    3. Forgetting that the catalyst is \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\), not sulphuric acid.
    4. Choosing a reaction that forms \(\mathrm{HCl}\) instead of one that forms \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\).

    Chapter / Topic / Subtopic:

    • Chapter: p-Block Elements
    • Topic: Preparation and properties of chlorine
    • Subtopic: Deacon's process

    Quick exam-style answer:

    Deacon's process is used to prepare chlorine gas from hydrogen chloride.

    The reaction is:

    \(4\mathrm{HCl} + \mathrm{O_2} \xrightarrow[\mathrm{723\ K}]{\mathrm{CuCl_2}} 2\mathrm{Cl_2} + 2\mathrm{H_2O}\)

    Therefore, the correct option is \(\boxed{(b)}\).

    Quick cheatsheet:

    • Name: Deacon's process
    • Purpose: preparation of chlorine gas
    • Reactants:

      \(\mathrm{HCl}\) and \(\mathrm{O_2}\)

    • Products:

      \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) and \(\mathrm{H_2O}\)

    • Catalyst:

      \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\)

    • Temperature:

      \(723\ \mathrm{K}\)

    • Core memory line:

      \(\mathrm{HCl}\) is oxidised to \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\)

    Answer: \((d)\ \text{I, II and IV only}\)


    p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR

    Solution:

    1. First identify compound \(C\)

      White phosphorus reacts with thionyl chloride to give phosphorus trichloride:

      \(\mathrm{P_4 + 8SOCl_2 \rightarrow 4PCl_3 + 4SO_2 + 2S_2Cl_2}\)

      So, \(C = \mathrm{PCl_3}\).



    2. Now identify oxo acid \(O\)

      \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) undergoes hydrolysis as:

      \(\mathrm{PCl_3 + 3H_2O \rightarrow H_3PO_3 + 3HCl}\)

      Hence, \(O = \mathrm{H_3PO_3}\), that is phosphorous acid.

    3. Check statement I

      In \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\), phosphorus has three bond pairs and one lone pair.

      So its electron pair geometry is tetrahedral, but the molecular shape is trigonal pyramidal.

      Therefore statement I is correct.

      •       Cl
       \
        P:
      /   \
    Cl     Cl

(trigonal pyramidal; one lone pair on P)
    4. Check statements II and III using structure of \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\)

      The correct structure of phosphorous acid is:

      \(\mathrm{HP(=O)(OH)_2}\)

      This means:

      • There are two \(\mathrm{O-H}\) hydrogens, which are ionisable.
      • There is one \(\mathrm{P-H}\) hydrogen, which is not ionisable.

      So \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is a dibasic acid, not a monobasic acid.

      Therefore:

      • Statement II is correct.
      • Statement III is incorrect.
      •         O
        ||
    H - P - OH
        |
       OH

Only the two OH hydrogens are acidic.
The P-H hydrogen is not replaceable by ionisation.
    5. Check statement IV

      \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) also reacts with acetic acid to form phosphorous acid:

      \(\mathrm{PCl_3 + 3CH_3COOH \rightarrow H_3PO_3 + 3CH_3COCl}\)

      So statement IV is correct.

    Therefore:

    \(\boxed{(d)\ \text{I, II and IV only}}\)

    Why this question is important:

    • It tests both phosphorus halides and oxoacids of phosphorus.
    • The key NCERT/JEE idea is that basicity of an oxo acid depends on the number of ionisable \(\mathrm{O-H}\) hydrogens, not on the total number of hydrogens present in the formula.
    • So \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) is monobasic, \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is dibasic, and \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) is tribasic.

    JEE / NCERT theory takeaway:

    • Shape of \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\):

      \(\mathrm{P}\) has one lone pair, so the shape is trigonal pyramidal.

    • Hydrolysis of \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\):

      \(\mathrm{PCl_3 + 3H_2O \rightarrow H_3PO_3 + 3HCl}\)

    • Structure of phosphorous acid:

      \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3 = HP(=O)(OH)_2}\)

    • Basicity rule:

      Only hydrogens attached to oxygen are acidic in oxoacids of phosphorus.

    • Important distinction:

      \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bond hydrogen is non-ionisable, so it does not contribute to basicity.

    • Reaction with carboxylic acid:

      \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) converts \(\mathrm{RCOOH}\) into \(\mathrm{RCOCl}\), and itself gets converted to \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\).

    1-minute solving trick:

    • Immediately recall: \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) on hydrolysis gives \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\).
    • Then recall the shortcut structure: \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3 = HP(=O)(OH)_2}\).
    • Count only \(\mathrm{OH}\) hydrogens for basicity.
    • So \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is dibasic, which makes statement II correct and III wrong.
    • \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) is a classic lone-pair molecule, so it is pyramidal.
    • Once III is false, eliminate all options containing III.

    Common mistakes to avoid:

    1. Thinking \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is tribasic just because it has three hydrogens.
    2. Forgetting that \(\mathrm{P-H}\) hydrogen is not acidic.
    3. Confusing \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) with \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\); \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) is pyramidal, while \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) is trigonal bipyramidal.
    4. Not recognizing that phosphorus trichloride reacts with carboxylic acids to produce acyl chlorides.

    Chapter / Topic / Subtopic:

    • Chapter: \(p\)-Block Elements
    • Topic: Compounds of phosphorus
    • Subtopic: \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\), hydrolysis, and oxoacids of phosphorus

    Final exam-style answer:

    \(\mathrm{P_4 + 8SOCl_2 \rightarrow 4PCl_3 + 4SO_2 + 2S_2Cl_2}\), so \(C = \mathrm{PCl_3}\).

    \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) has one lone pair on phosphorus, hence its shape is trigonal pyramidal.

    On hydrolysis:

    \(\mathrm{PCl_3 + 3H_2O \rightarrow H_3PO_3 + 3HCl}\)

    Thus \(O = \mathrm{H_3PO_3}\).

    The structure of \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is \(\mathrm{HP(=O)(OH)_2}\), so it has only two ionisable \(\mathrm{OH}\) hydrogens and is therefore dibasic.

    Also, \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) reacts with acetic acid to give \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\).

    Hence statements I, II and IV are correct.

    \(\boxed{(d)\ \text{I, II and IV only}}\)

    Quick cheatsheet:

    • \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) shape:

      trigonal pyramidal

    • Hydrolysis:

      \(\mathrm{PCl_3 + 3H_2O \rightarrow H_3PO_3 + 3HCl}\)

    • Phosphorous acid structure:

      \(\mathrm{HP(=O)(OH)_2}\)

    • Basicity rule:

      basicity \(=\) number of ionisable \(\mathrm{OH}\) hydrogens

    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\):

      dibasic

    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\):

      monobasic

    • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\):

      tribasic

    Answer: \((b)\)


    p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR

    Solution:

    1. Identify what the question is asking

      We have to find the incorrect statement about chlorine.

    2. Check option \((a)\)

      Chlorine is a strong oxidising agent.

      It oxidises ferrous salts \(\mathrm{Fe^{2+}}\) to ferric salts \(\mathrm{Fe^{3+}}\) in acidic medium.

      A representative change is:

      \(\mathrm{2Fe^{2+} + Cl_2 \rightarrow 2Fe^{3+} + 2Cl^-}\)

      So option \((a)\) is correct.

    3. Check option \((b)\)

      The statement says that chlorine oxidises iodine to periodic acid in water.

      This is not correct.

      Chlorine does not directly oxidise iodine to periodic acid in water under the usual NCERT-level discussion.

      So option \((b)\) is the incorrect statement.

    4. Check option \((c)\)

      Chlorine acts as a bleaching agent due to oxidation.

      In water:

      \(\mathrm{Cl_2 + H_2O \rightleftharpoons HCl + HOCl}\)

      The hypochlorous acid formed gives nascent oxygen in the usual school-level explanation:

      \(\mathrm{HOCl \rightarrow HCl + [O]}\)

      This oxygen oxidises coloured substances to colourless substances.

      So option \((c)\) is correct.

    5. Check option \((d)\)

      Chlorine is manufactured by Deacon's process.

      In this process, hydrogen chloride is oxidised by oxygen:

      \(\mathrm{4HCl + O_2 \xrightarrow{CuCl_2,\ 723\ K} 2Cl_2 + 2H_2O}\)

      So option \((d)\) is correct.

    Therefore:

    \(\boxed{(b)}\)

    Why this method works:

    • Chlorine commonly shows oxidising behaviour.
    • So statements based on oxidation of \(\mathrm{Fe^{2+}}\), bleaching action, and Deacon's process are standard correct facts.
    • The unusual statement is the one claiming direct oxidation of iodine to periodic acid in water, so that becomes the incorrect one.

    JEE / NCERT takeaway:

    • Oxidising nature of chlorine:

      \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) readily gains electrons and gets reduced to \(\mathrm{Cl^-}\).

    • Bleaching action of chlorine:

      Bleaching by chlorine is due to oxidation and is generally permanent.

    • Reaction with water:

      \(\mathrm{Cl_2 + H_2O \rightleftharpoons HCl + HOCl}\)

    • Deacon's process:

      \(\mathrm{4HCl + O_2 \rightarrow 2Cl_2 + 2H_2O}\)

    • Important exam point:

      Questions on chlorine often mix up oxidising action, bleaching action, and manufacture.

    1-minute solving trick:

    • Immediately recall three standard facts:
    • Chlorine oxidises \(\mathrm{Fe^{2+}}\) to \(\mathrm{Fe^{3+}}\).
    • Chlorine bleaches due to oxidation.
    • Chlorine is obtained by Deacon's process.
    • So \((a)\), \((c)\), and \((d)\) look familiar and correct.
    • That leaves \((b)\) as the incorrect statement.

    Common mistakes to avoid:

    1. Reading “not correct” as “correct”.
    2. Confusing bleaching by chlorine with reduction; in standard chemistry treatment it is due to oxidation.
    3. Assuming every higher oxyacid of iodine is formed directly by chlorine in water.
    4. Forgetting Deacon's process as a method for manufacture of chlorine.

    Chapter / Topic / Subtopic:

    • Chapter: \(p\)-Block Elements
    • Topic: Group 17 elements
    • Subtopic: Chemical properties, bleaching action, oxidising nature, and manufacture of chlorine

    Final exam-style answer:

    Option \((b)\) is incorrect.

    Chlorine oxidises ferrous salts to ferric salts in acidic medium, so \((a)\) is correct.

    Its bleaching action is due to oxidation, so \((c)\) is correct.

    Chlorine is manufactured by Deacon's process, so \((d)\) is also correct.

    But chlorine does not directly oxidise iodine to periodic acid in water.

    \(\boxed{(b)}\)

    Quick cheatsheet:

    • Chlorine in water:

      \(\mathrm{Cl_2 + H_2O \rightleftharpoons HCl + HOCl}\)

    • Bleaching:

      due to oxidation

    • Ferrous to ferric:

      \(\mathrm{Fe^{2+} \rightarrow Fe^{3+}}\)

    • Deacon's process:

      \(\mathrm{4HCl + O_2 \rightarrow 2Cl_2 + 2H_2O}\)

    • Incorrect statement here:

      chlorine directly oxidises iodine to periodic acid in water

    Answer: \((c)\)


    p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR

    Solution:

    1. Check the Assertion \((A)\)

      Phosphorus forms both \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) and \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\).

      Nitrogen forms \(\mathrm{NCl_3}\), but it does not form \(\mathrm{NCl_5}\).

      So Assertion \((A)\) is correct.

    2. Why phosphorus can form \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) but nitrogen cannot form \(\mathrm{NCl_5}\)

      Phosphorus belongs to the third period and has vacant \(3d\)-orbitals available in its valence shell.

      Because of this, phosphorus can expand its octet and accommodate more than eight electrons around the central atom.

      Hence phosphorus can form five covalent bonds in \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\).

      Nitrogen belongs to the second period and has no \(d\)-orbitals in its valence shell.

      So nitrogen cannot expand its octet beyond eight electrons, and therefore \(\mathrm{NCl_5}\) is not formed.

    3. Check the Reason \((R)\)

      The electronegativity of nitrogen is indeed greater than that of phosphorus.

      So Reason \((R)\) is also correct as a statement.

    4. But does \((R)\) explain \((A)\)?

      No.

      The real reason for the difference is availability of vacant \(d\)-orbitals and expansion of octet in phosphorus, not the electronegativity difference.

      So both \((A)\) and \((R)\) are correct, but \((R)\) is not the correct explanation of \((A)\).

    Therefore:

    \(\boxed{(c)\text{ Both (A) and (R) are correct, (R) is not the correct explanation of (A).}}\)

    Why this method works:

    • Assertion–Reason questions must be checked in two steps.
    • First see whether \((A)\) is true or false.
    • Then separately check whether \((R)\) is true or false.
    • Even if both are true, the answer is \((a)\) only when \((R)\) actually explains \((A)\).
    • Here both are true, but the explanation is based on octet expansion, not electronegativity.

    JEE / NCERT takeaway:

    • Group 15 valence shell:

      \(\mathrm{ns^2 np^3}\)

    • Nitrogen:

      Second period element, no vacant \(d\)-orbitals

    • Phosphorus:

      Third period element, vacant \(d\)-orbitals available

    • Maximum covalency of nitrogen:

      Generally does not exceed \(4\)

    • Maximum covalency of phosphorus:

      Can exceed \(4\), as in \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\)

    • Key comparison:

      \(\mathrm{NCl_3}\) exists, but \(\mathrm{NCl_5}\) does not; \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) and \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) both exist.

    1-minute solving trick:

    • Whenever you see \(\mathrm{NCl_5}\) versus \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\), immediately think:
    • nitrogen = second period = no \(d\)-orbitals = no octet expansion
    • phosphorus = third period = octet expansion possible
    • Then check the reason carefully.
    • If the reason talks only about electronegativity, it is not the main explanation here.
    • So the answer becomes \((c)\).

    Common mistakes to avoid:

    1. Marking \((a)\) just because both statements are true.
    2. Thinking higher electronegativity is the reason for non-existence of \(\mathrm{NCl_5}\).
    3. Forgetting that the main idea is octet expansion.
    4. Confusing valency with covalency in \(p\)-block comparisons.

    Chapter / Topic / Subtopic:

    • Chapter: \(p\)-Block Elements
    • Topic: Group 15 elements
    • Subtopic: Anomalous behaviour of nitrogen and comparison with phosphorus

    Final exam-style answer:

    Assertion \((A)\) is correct because phosphorus forms both \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) and \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\), whereas nitrogen does not form \(\mathrm{NCl_5}\).

    Reason \((R)\) is also correct because nitrogen is more electronegative than phosphorus.

    However, this is not the correct explanation of the assertion.

    The correct explanation is that phosphorus has vacant \(d\)-orbitals and can expand its octet, whereas nitrogen has no \(d\)-orbitals and cannot expand its octet.

    Hence, the correct answer is:

    \(\boxed{(c)}\)

    Quick cheatsheet:

    • Nitrogen:

      second period, no \(d\)-orbitals

    • Phosphorus:

      third period, vacant \(d\)-orbitals available

    • \(\mathrm{NCl_3}\):

      exists

    • \(\mathrm{NCl_5}\):

      does not exist

    • \(\mathrm{PCl_3}\) and \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\):

      both exist

    • Main reason:

      octet expansion in phosphorus

    • Assertion–Reason trap:

      both true does not always mean explanation is correct

    Answer: \((b)\ \mathrm{N_2O < NO < N_2O_3 < N_2O_4 < N_2O_5}\) (Acidic nature)


    p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR

    Solution:

    1. Check option \((b)\): acidic nature of oxides of nitrogen

      As the oxidation state of nitrogen increases, the acidic nature of its oxides generally increases.

      Oxidation states here are:

      • \(\mathrm{N_2O}\): \(+1\)
      • \(\mathrm{NO}\): \(+2\)
      • \(\mathrm{N_2O_3}\): \(+3\)
      • \(\mathrm{N_2O_4}\): average \(+4\)
      • \(\mathrm{N_2O_5}\): \(+5\)

      So the acidic character increases in the order:

      \(\mathrm{N_2O < NO < N_2O_3 < N_2O_4 < N_2O_5}\)

      Hence option \((b)\) is correctly matched.

    2. Why the other options are incorrect

      Option \((a)\): boiling point

      The given order is:

      \(\mathrm{H_2S < H_2O < H_2Se < H_2Te}\)

      This is incorrect.

      Among group 16 hydrides, boiling point generally increases down the group because of increasing molecular mass:

      \(\mathrm{H_2S < H_2Se < H_2Te}\)

      But \(\mathrm{H_2O}\) has abnormally high boiling point because of strong intermolecular hydrogen bonding.

      Therefore the correct order is:

      \(\mathrm{H_2S < H_2Se < H_2Te < H_2O}\)

    3. Option \((c)\): acidic nature of hydrogen halides

      The given order is:

      \(\mathrm{HI < HCl < HBr < HF}\)

      This is incorrect.

      Acidic strength of hydrogen halides in aqueous solution increases down the group because the \(\mathrm{H-X}\) bond becomes weaker:

      \(\mathrm{HF < HCl < HBr < HI}\)

      So option \((c)\) is wrong.

    4. Option \((d)\): bond angle

      The given order is:

      \(\mathrm{H_2O < H_2S < H_2Se < H_2Te}\)

      This is incorrect.

      For group 16 hydrides, bond angle decreases down the group.

      Approximate trend:

      \(\mathrm{H_2O > H_2S > H_2Se > H_2Te}\)

      So in increasing order:

      \(\mathrm{H_2Te < H_2Se < H_2S < H_2O}\)

    Therefore:

    \(\boxed{(b)\ \mathrm{N_2O < NO < N_2O_3 < N_2O_4 < N_2O_5}}\)

    Why this method works:

    • Many such questions are solved by recalling a few standard periodic and molecular trends.
    • For non-metal oxides, acidic nature generally increases with oxidation state and covalent character.
    • For hydrides of a group:
      • boiling point depends on intermolecular forces and molecular mass,
      • bond angle depends on hybridization and size/electronegativity of central atom,
      • acid strength depends on bond dissociation tendency.

    JEE / NCERT takeaway:

    • Oxides of nitrogen: acidic nature increases with oxidation state.
    • \(\mathrm{N_2O}\) and \(\mathrm{NO}\) are nearly neutral oxides, while higher oxides become acidic.
    • \(\mathrm{N_2O_3}\), \(\mathrm{N_2O_4}\), and \(\mathrm{N_2O_5}\) behave as acidic oxides.
    • Group 16 hydrides boiling point: normally increases down the group, but \(\mathrm{H_2O}\) is anomalously high because of hydrogen bonding.
    • Hydrogen halide acidity: \(\mathrm{HF < HCl < HBr < HI}\)
    • Bond angle in group 16 hydrides: decreases down the group.

    Useful trend summary:

    • Boiling point of group 16 hydrides:

      \(\mathrm{H_2S < H_2Se < H_2Te < H_2O}\)

    • Acidic strength of hydrogen halides:

      \(\mathrm{HF < HCl < HBr < HI}\)

    • Bond angle of group 16 hydrides:

      \(\mathrm{H_2Te < H_2Se < H_2S < H_2O}\)

    • Acidic nature of oxides of nitrogen:

      \(\mathrm{N_2O < NO < N_2O_3 < N_2O_4 < N_2O_5}\)

    1-minute solving trick:

    • Check the most familiar trend first: hydrogen halide acidity.
    • Since everyone should know:

      \(\mathrm{HF < HCl < HBr < HI}\)

      option \((c)\) is immediately wrong.
    • Next remember:

      \(\mathrm{H_2O}\) has unusually high boiling point due to hydrogen bonding.

      So option \((a)\) is also wrong.
    • Bond angle in group 16 hydrides decreases down the group, so option \((d)\) is wrong.
    • Therefore \((b)\) must be correct even before detailed verification.

    Common mistakes to avoid:

    1. Thinking molecular mass alone controls boiling point and forgetting the special hydrogen bonding in \(\mathrm{H_2O}\).
    2. Reversing hydrogen halide acidity by focusing only on electronegativity.
    3. Forgetting that weaker \(\mathrm{H-X}\) bond means stronger acid in water.
    4. Confusing bond angle trend in \(\mathrm{H_2E}\) molecules.
    5. Forgetting that higher oxidation state oxides of non-metals are usually more acidic.

    Chapter / Topic / Subtopic:

    • Chapter: \(p\)-Block Elements
    • Topic: Trends in hydrides and oxides of \(p\)-block elements
    • Subtopic: Boiling point, acidic nature, and bond angle trends

    Final exam-style answer:

    Acidic nature of oxides of nitrogen increases with increase in oxidation state of nitrogen.

    Hence:

    \(\mathrm{N_2O < NO < N_2O_3 < N_2O_4 < N_2O_5}\)

    Therefore option \((b)\) is correct.

    The correct forms of the remaining options are:

    • \((a)\ \mathrm{H_2S < H_2Se < H_2Te < H_2O}\) (Boiling point)
    • \((c)\ \mathrm{HF < HCl < HBr < HI}\) (Acidic nature)
    • \((d)\ \mathrm{H_2Te < H_2Se < H_2S < H_2O}\) (Bond angle)

    \(\boxed{(b)}\)

    Quick cheatsheet:

    • Hydrogen bonding anomaly:

      \(\mathrm{H_2O}\) has unusually high boiling point.

    • Hydrogen halide acidity:

      \(\mathrm{HF < HCl < HBr < HI}\)

    • Group 16 hydride bond angle:

      \(\mathrm{H_2O > H_2S > H_2Se > H_2Te}\)

    • Oxides of nitrogen:

      higher oxidation state \(\rightarrow\) more acidic oxide

    • Correct option here:

      \((b)\)

    Answer: \((b)\ \mathrm{Ar,\ He}\)


    p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR

    Solution:

    1. Identify noble gas \(X\)

      The question says noble gas \(X\) is used as a diluent for oxygen in modern diving apparatus.

      This is helium.

      Helium is preferred in deep-sea diving mixtures because it is:

      • chemically inert,
      • less soluble in blood than nitrogen-related gas mixtures in this context,
      • useful in reducing problems like nitrogen narcosis in deep diving.

      So:

      \(X = \mathrm{He}\)

    2. Identify noble gas \(Y\)

      The question says noble gas \(Y\) is used mainly to provide an inert atmosphere in high temperature metallurgical processes.

      This is argon.

      Argon is widely used because it is inert and provides a protective atmosphere during processes involving hot metals.

      So:

      \(Y = \mathrm{Ar}\)

    3. Now arrange \(Y\) and \(X\) respectively

      We found:

      • \(Y = \mathrm{Ar}\)
      • \(X = \mathrm{He}\)

      Therefore:

      \(Y,\ X = \mathrm{Ar,\ He}\)

      Hence the correct option is:

      \(\boxed{(b)}\)

    Therefore:

    \(\boxed{(b)\ \mathrm{Ar,\ He}}\)

    Why this method works:

    • This is a direct application-based question from noble gases.
    • You solve it by matching the use with the correct noble gas.
    • Helium is associated with modern diving apparatus.
    • Argon is associated with inert atmosphere for metallurgical and welding/high-temperature work.

    JEE / NCERT takeaway:

    • Helium:
      • used in airships and balloons because it is light and non-inflammable,
      • used in diving apparatus mixed with oxygen,
      • used where an inert and light gas is needed.
    • Argon:
      • used to provide inert atmosphere,
      • used in high-temperature metallurgical processes,
      • used in electric bulbs and welding/protective environments.
    • Noble gases are used in such applications because they are chemically inert due to stable electronic configuration.

    1-minute solving trick:

    • Remember this fixed pair:

      Diving apparatus \(\rightarrow\) Helium

    • Remember this second fixed pair:

      Inert atmosphere in metallurgy \(\rightarrow\) Argon

    • The question asks \(Y\) and \(X\) respectively, not \(X\) and \(Y\).
    • Since \(X = \mathrm{He}\) and \(Y = \mathrm{Ar}\), answer becomes:

      \(\mathrm{Ar,\ He}\)

    Common mistakes to avoid:

    1. Reading the order incorrectly. The question asks \(Y\) and \(X\), not \(X\) and \(Y\).
    2. Marking \(\mathrm{He,\ Ar}\) after identifying gases correctly but reversing the required order.
    3. Confusing argon and helium applications.
    4. Choosing krypton because it is also a noble gas, even though it is not the standard gas used in these applications.

    Chapter / Topic / Subtopic:

    • Chapter: \(p\)-Block Elements
    • Topic: Noble gases
    • Subtopic: Uses of helium and argon

    Final exam-style answer:

    Helium is used as a diluent for oxygen in modern diving apparatus.

    Argon is used to provide an inert atmosphere in high-temperature metallurgical processes.

    Therefore \(Y\) and \(X\) respectively are:

    \(\mathrm{Ar,\ He}\)

    \(\boxed{(b)\ \mathrm{Ar,\ He}}\)

    Quick cheatsheet:

    • Helium: diving apparatus with oxygen
    • Argon: inert atmosphere in metallurgy
    • Why noble gases are useful: they are chemically inert
    • Question order check: \(Y,\ X\)
    • Correct option: \((b)\)

    Answer: \((c)\ \mathrm{H_3PO_4,\ PH_3}\)


    p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR

    Solution:

    1. First identify the dibasic oxoacid of phosphorus

      The dibasic oxoacid of phosphorus is phosphorous acid:

      \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\)

      It is called dibasic because only two hydrogens are ionisable.

      Its structure is:

      \(\mathrm{HPO(OH)_2}\)

      So it contains:

      • two \(\mathrm{P-OH}\) hydrogens \(\rightarrow\) acidic,
      • one \(\mathrm{P-H}\) hydrogen \(\rightarrow\) non-ionisable.
    2. Recall the disproportionation reaction of \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\)

      On heating, phosphorous acid undergoes disproportionation:

      \(\mathrm{4H_3PO_3 \rightarrow 3H_3PO_4 + PH_3}\)

      Therefore the two products are:

      • \(A = \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\)
      • \(B = \mathrm{PH_3}\)
    3. Why this is called disproportionation

      In \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\), the oxidation state of phosphorus is:

      \(x + 3(+1) + 3(-2) = 0\)

      \(x + 3 - 6 = 0\)

      \(x = +3\)

      In the products:

      • In \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\), phosphorus is \(+5\)
      • In \(\mathrm{PH_3}\), phosphorus is \(-3\)

      So the same phosphorus species in oxidation state \(+3\) undergoes both:

      • oxidation to \(+5\), and
      • reduction to \(-3\).

      That is exactly the meaning of disproportionation.

    4. Match with the options

      Since \(A = \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) and \(B = \mathrm{PH_3}\), the correct option is:

      \(\boxed{(c)}\)

    Therefore:

    \(\boxed{(c)\ \mathrm{H_3PO_4,\ PH_3}}\)

    Why this method works:

    • This question is based on a standard named reaction/fact from phosphorus oxoacids.
    • Once you identify the dibasic phosphorus oxoacid as \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\), the disproportionation reaction is direct:

      \(\mathrm{4H_3PO_3 \rightarrow 3H_3PO_4 + PH_3}\)

    • The presence of \(\mathrm{P-H}\) bond in lower oxoacids of phosphorus is also a clue that such acids often act as reducing agents and can disproportionate.

    JEE / NCERT takeaway:

    • Phosphorous acid:

      \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3 = HPO(OH)_2}\)

    • It is dibasic, not tribasic, because only two hydrogens are attached through oxygen as \(\mathrm{P-OH}\).
    • The hydrogen directly bonded to phosphorus \(\mathrm{(P-H)}\) is not ionisable.
    • On heating:

      \(\mathrm{4H_3PO_3 \rightarrow 3H_3PO_4 + PH_3}\)

    • This is a classic example of disproportionation.
    • Phosphoric acid \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) is tribasic because it has three \(\mathrm{P-OH}\) groups.
    • Hypophosphorous acid \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) is monobasic because it has only one \(\mathrm{P-OH}\) group.

    1-minute solving trick:

    • Memorise these three phosphorus oxoacid basics:
      • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) \(\rightarrow\) monobasic
      • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) \(\rightarrow\) dibasic
      • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) \(\rightarrow\) tribasic
    • Then remember the fixed disproportionation:

      \(\mathrm{4H_3PO_3 \rightarrow 3H_3PO_4 + PH_3}\)

    • So without calculation, immediately mark:

      \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4,\ PH_3}\)

    Common mistakes to avoid:

    1. Thinking \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is tribasic just because it has three hydrogens.
    2. Forgetting that basicity depends on number of ionisable \(\mathrm{OH}\) hydrogens, not total number of hydrogens.
    3. Mixing up \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) and \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\).
    4. Missing that the question asks for products \(A\) and \(B\) respectively.
    5. Confusing disproportionation with simple decomposition.

    Chapter / Topic / Subtopic:

    • Chapter: \(p\)-Block Elements
    • Topic: Oxoacids of phosphorus
    • Subtopic: Basicity, structure, and disproportionation of \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\)

    Final exam-style answer:

    The dibasic oxoacid of phosphorus is phosphorous acid, \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\).

    On heating, it disproportionates according to:

    \(\mathrm{4H_3PO_3 \rightarrow 3H_3PO_4 + PH_3}\)

    Hence:

    \(A = \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\), \(\quad B = \mathrm{PH_3}\)

    \(\boxed{(c)\ \mathrm{H_3PO_4,\ PH_3}}\)

    Quick cheatsheet:

    • Monobasic phosphorus oxoacid:

      \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\)

    • Dibasic phosphorus oxoacid:

      \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\)

    • Tribasic phosphorus oxoacid:

      \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\)

    • Rule: basicity = number of \(\mathrm{P-OH}\) bonds
    • Disproportionation of phosphorous acid:

      \(\mathrm{4H_3PO_3 \rightarrow 3H_3PO_4 + PH_3}\)

    • Correct option:

      \((c)\ \mathrm{H_3PO_4,\ PH_3}\)

    • Correct answer: (d) B < C < A < D
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: Here \(A=\mathrm{S_8}\), \(B=\mathrm{P_4}\), \(C=\mathrm{S_6}\) and \(D=\mathrm{O_3}\). Their approximate bond angles are: \(\mathrm{P_4}\approx 60^\circ\), \(\mathrm{S_6}\approx 102.2^\circ\), \(\mathrm{S_8}\approx 107^\circ\), and \(\mathrm{O_3}\approx 116.8^\circ\). Hence the increasing order is \(\mathrm{P_4<S_6<S_8<O_3}\), that is \(B<C<A<D\).
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • The symbols in the table represent:
        • \(A=\mathrm{S_8}\)
        • \(B=\mathrm{P_4}\)
        • \(C=\mathrm{S_6}\)
        • \(D=\mathrm{O_3}\)
      • Now recall the characteristic bond angles:
        • \(\mathrm{P_4}\): tetrahedral cage, bond angle \(\approx 60^\circ\)
        • \(\mathrm{S_6}\): chair-like ring, bond angle \(\approx 102.2^\circ\)
        • \(\mathrm{S_8}\): crown-shaped ring, bond angle \(\approx 107^\circ\)
        • \(\mathrm{O_3}\): bent molecule, bond angle \(\approx 116.8^\circ\)
      • Therefore the increasing order is:
        • \(\mathrm{P_4 < S_6 < S_8 < O_3}\)
      • Converting back into letters:
        • \(B < C < A < D\)
      • Hence option (d) is correct.
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This question is based on remembering the structures and geometries of important molecular forms of \(p\)-block elements.
      • \(\mathrm{P_4}\)
        • \(\mathrm{P_4}\) has a tetrahedral cage structure.
        • The bond angle is unusually small, about \(60^\circ\), which causes angle strain.
      • \(\mathrm{S_6}\) and \(\mathrm{S_8}\)
        • \(\mathrm{S_6}\) is chair-like.
        • \(\mathrm{S_8}\) is crown-shaped.
        • Their bond angles are near the tetrahedral value but differ slightly due to ring structure.
      • \(\mathrm{O_3}\)
        • Ozone is bent due to the central oxygen having lone pair-lone pair and lone pair-bond pair effects.
        • Its bond angle is about \(116.8^\circ\).
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Using the known bond angles:
        • \(60^\circ < 102.2^\circ < 107^\circ < 116.8^\circ\)
      • That means:
        • \(\mathrm{P_4 < S_6 < S_8 < O_3}\)
        • \(B < C < A < D\)
      • So option (d) exactly matches.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable
      • The wrong options place at least one molecule out of its correct angle position.
      • The biggest fixed anchor is \(\mathrm{P_4}\), which must be the smallest because of its \(60^\circ\) angle.
      • The largest is \(\mathrm{O_3}\), so it must come last in increasing order.
      • Any option violating either of these is immediately eliminated.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • For quick solving, remember just these:
        • \(\mathrm{P_4} \rightarrow 60^\circ\)
        • \(\mathrm{S_6} \rightarrow 102.2^\circ\)
        • \(\mathrm{S_8} \rightarrow 107^\circ\)
        • \(\mathrm{O_3} \rightarrow 116.8^\circ\)
      • Then arrange from smallest to largest.
    • Final result: The correct order is \(B < C < A < D\).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic: \(p\)-Block Elements / Structures of allotropes and molecules / Bond angles in \(\mathrm{P_4}\), \(\mathrm{S_6}\), \(\mathrm{S_8}\) and \(\mathrm{O_3}\)
    • Correct answer: (d) \(\mathrm{N_2,\ Cr_2O_3,\ H_2O}\)
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: On heating, ammonium dichromate decomposes to give nitrogen gas, chromium(III) oxide and water vapour. The balanced reaction is \(\mathrm{(NH_4)_2Cr_2O_7 \rightarrow Cr_2O_3 + N_2 + 4H_2O}\).
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • This is a direct decomposition reaction of ammonium dichromate.
      • On heating:
        • \(\mathrm{(NH_4)_2Cr_2O_7 \rightarrow Cr_2O_3 + N_2 + 4H_2O}\)
      • So the products are:
        • \(\mathrm{N_2}\)
        • \(\mathrm{Cr_2O_3}\)
        • \(\mathrm{H_2O}\)
      • Hence option (d) is correct.
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This is a standard laboratory decomposition reaction often called the volcano experiment.
      • Ammonium dichromate contains both oxidising and reducing parts within the same compound.
      • On heating:
        • nitrogen in ammonium is converted to \(\mathrm{N_2}\)
        • chromium ends up as \(\mathrm{Cr_2O_3}\)
        • hydrogen forms water vapour
      • The green residue observed is chromium(III) oxide.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (d) exactly lists the actual decomposition products from the balanced equation.
      • No other gaseous nitrogen oxide is formed as the main product here.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable
      • (a): \(\mathrm{NO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{H_2}\) are not formed in this decomposition.
      • (b): \(\mathrm{N_2O}\) is not the product here; the product is \(\mathrm{N_2}\).
      • (c): \(\mathrm{CrO_3}\) is incorrect; the chromium oxide formed is \(\mathrm{Cr_2O_3}\).
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Memorise this one very standard equation:
        • \(\mathrm{(NH_4)_2Cr_2O_7 \rightarrow Cr_2O_3 + N_2 + 4H_2O}\)
      • If you remember the green residue is \(\mathrm{Cr_2O_3}\), it becomes much easier to eliminate wrong options.
    • Final result: Thermal decomposition of ammonium dichromate gives \(\mathrm{N_2,\ Cr_2O_3}\) and \(\mathrm{H_2O}\).

    • Correct answer: (c) ‘\(B\)’ in solid state exists as an ionic solid.
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) reacts with \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) in presence of charcoal to form sulfuryl chloride, \(\mathrm{SO_2Cl_2}\) \((A)\). This reacts with white phosphorus to form \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) \((B)\) and \(\mathrm{SO_2}\). In the solid state, \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) exists as \((\mathrm{PCl_4})^+(\mathrm{PCl_6})^-\), so it behaves as an ionic solid. Therefore option (c) is correct.
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • First identify compound \(A\):
        • \(\mathrm{SO_2 + Cl_2 \xrightarrow{charcoal} SO_2Cl_2}\)
      • So, \(A = \mathrm{SO_2Cl_2}\), which is sulfuryl chloride.
      • Now sulfuryl chloride reacts with white phosphorus to produce phosphorus pentachloride and sulphur dioxide.
      • Hence:
        • \(B = \mathrm{PCl_5}\)
      • Now check each statement about \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\).
      • In gaseous state, \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) has trigonal bipyramidal geometry, not pyramidal.
      • On hydrolysis, \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) gives phosphoric acid \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\), not phosphorus acid \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\).
      • In solid state, \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) exists as ionic species:
        • \((\mathrm{PCl_4})^+(\mathrm{PCl_6})^-\)
      • Also, in gaseous \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\), all \(\mathrm{P-Cl}\) bonds are not equivalent because axial and equatorial bonds differ.
      • Therefore only option (c) is correct.
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • Sulfuryl chloride formation
        • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) reacts with chlorine in presence of charcoal to form \(\mathrm{SO_2Cl_2}\).
        • This is a standard preparation reaction of sulfuryl chloride.
      • Properties of \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\)
        • In vapour phase, \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) is covalent and trigonal bipyramidal.
        • It has 3 equatorial bonds and 2 axial bonds.
        • So all bonds are not equivalent.
        • In solid state, it becomes ionic:
          • \((\mathrm{PCl_4})^+(\mathrm{PCl_6})^-\)
      • Hydrolysis
        • \(\mathrm{PCl_5 + 4H_2O \rightarrow H_3PO_4 + 5HCl}\)
        • So the acid formed is phosphoric acid, not phosphorus acid.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) in solid form does not remain as simple discrete covalent molecules.
      • It forms an ionic lattice containing \((\mathrm{PCl_4})^+\) and \((\mathrm{PCl_6})^-\).
      • That makes option (c) the correct statement.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable
      • (a): \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) is trigonal bipyramidal in gaseous state, not pyramidal.
      • (b): Hydrolysis gives phosphoric acid \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\), not phosphorus acid \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\).
      • (d): In trigonal bipyramidal \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\), axial and equatorial bonds are different, so all bonds are not equivalent.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • For \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\), remember this three-part memory set:
        • gas phase \(\rightarrow\) trigonal bipyramidal
        • solid phase \(\rightarrow\) ionic \((\mathrm{PCl_4})^+(\mathrm{PCl_6})^-\)
        • hydrolysis \(\rightarrow\) \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\)
      • If any MCQ says all bonds in \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\) are equal, that is false.
    • Final result: \(B\) is \(\mathrm{PCl_5}\), and in solid state it exists as an ionic solid.

    • Correct answer: (b) \(NaNO_2\).
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: On reaction with \(HCl\), sodium nitrite forms nitrous acid \((HNO_2)\), which is unstable and decomposes to give a mixture of nitrogen oxides. Because it can produce more than one oxide of nitrogen, it gives the more number of oxides compared with the other options.
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • Let us check each option one by one.
      • \(Na_2CO_3 + 2HCl \rightarrow 2NaCl + H_2O + CO_2\)
      • Here only one oxide-type gaseous product appears, namely \(CO_2\).
      • \(NaHCO_3 + HCl \rightarrow NaCl + H_2O + CO_2\)
      • Again, only \(CO_2\) is formed.
      • \(Na_2SO_3 + 2HCl \rightarrow 2NaCl + H_2O + SO_2\)
      • Here only one oxide is formed, namely \(SO_2\).
      • Now consider sodium nitrite:
        • \(NaNO_2 + HCl \rightarrow NaCl + HNO_2\)
      • \(HNO_2\) is unstable and decomposes.
      • Its decomposition can lead to nitrogen oxides such as:
        • \(NO\)
        • \(NO_2\)
      • So compared with the other options, this one gives more number of oxides.
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This question tests your understanding of unstable acids formed by acidifying salts.
      • Carbonates and bicarbonates on treatment with acids release \(CO_2\).
      • Sulphites on treatment with acids release \(SO_2\).
      • But nitrites form \(HNO_2\), and nitrous acid is unstable.
      • Because of this instability, it can decompose and produce multiple nitrogen oxides.
      • That is the key idea behind the question.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • \(NaNO_2\) first forms \(HNO_2\) with HCl.
      • \(HNO_2\) is unstable and can give a mixture of oxides of nitrogen.
      • Hence it gives more number of oxides than the other options, each of which mainly gives only one oxide gas.
    • Why other options are wrong
      • \(Na_2CO_3\) gives mainly \(CO_2\) only.
      • \(NaHCO_3\) also gives mainly \(CO_2\) only.
      • \(Na_2SO_3\) gives mainly \(SO_2\) only.
      • None of these give multiple oxides in the way nitrite does after acidification.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Remember the quick acidification rules:
        • carbonate / bicarbonate \(\rightarrow CO_2\)
        • sulphite \(\rightarrow SO_2\)
        • nitrite \(\rightarrow HNO_2\) unstable \(\rightarrow\) nitrogen oxides
      • Whenever an unstable acid is formed first, expect a trick question.
    • Final result
      • \(NaNO_2\) gives more number of oxides on reacting with \(HCl\).

      • Topic: Reactions of salts with acids
      • Subtopic: Behaviour of nitrites, carbonates and sulphites on acidification
    • Correct answer: (a) 1, 1, 1.
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: In all three species, xenon has one lone pair on the central atom. This is found by counting the total electron domains around Xe using valence electrons and VSEPR logic.
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • We check each compound separately.
      • For \(XeO_3\)
        • Xenon has 8 valence electrons.
        • It forms three bonds with three oxygen atoms.
        • After arranging bonding and completing the structure, one lone pair remains on xenon.
        • So lone pairs on Xe in \(XeO_3 = 1\).
      • For \(XeOF_4\)
        • Xenon is bonded to one oxygen and four fluorine atoms.
        • That makes five bonded regions around xenon.
        • In addition to these, xenon has one lone pair.
        • So total electron domains are six, giving octahedral electron pair arrangement and square pyramidal shape.
        • Hence lone pairs on Xe in \(XeOF_4 = 1\).
      • For \(XeF_6\)
        • Xenon is bonded to six fluorine atoms.
        • Even after forming six Xe–F bonds, one lone pair remains on xenon.
        • So lone pairs on Xe in \(XeF_6 = 1\).
      • Therefore, the required sequence is:
        • \(1,\ 1,\ 1\)
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • Xenon is a noble gas, but it can form compounds with highly electronegative atoms such as oxygen and fluorine.
      • To find lone pairs on the central atom, use VSEPR idea:
        • count valence electrons on the central atom
        • count how many bonding regions are attached
        • see how many non-bonding electron pairs remain
      • For xenon oxyfluorides and fluorides, standard shapes and lone-pair counts are commonly tested:
        • \(XeO_3\): one lone pair
        • \(XeOF_4\): one lone pair
        • \(XeF_6\): one lone pair
      • In \(XeF_6\), the geometry is somewhat distorted because of the lone pair and is often described as distorted octahedral / monocapped octahedron type description in advanced discussions, but for this exam the key fact is the lone pair count.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Each of the three species has exactly one lone pair on xenon.
      • So the sequence is \(1,1,1\), which matches option (a).
    • Why other options are wrong
      • Options with 2 or 3 lone pairs are inconsistent with the accepted Lewis/VSEPR structures of these xenon compounds.
      • \(XeOF_4\) does not have two lone pairs; it has one lone pair and five bonded atoms.
      • \(XeF_6\) also does not have zero lone pairs; one lone pair remains on xenon.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • For common xenon compounds, it helps to remember these standard results directly.
      • A very useful memory cluster is:
        • \(XeF_2\): 3 lone pairs on Xe
        • \(XeF_4\): 2 lone pairs on Xe
        • \(XeF_6\): 1 lone pair on Xe
      • Similarly, \(XeO_3\) and \(XeOF_4\) also have one lone pair on xenon.
      • This makes elimination much faster in MCQs.
    • Final result
      • The number of lone pairs on the central atom in \(XeO_3\), \(XeOF_4\) and \(XeF_6\) respectively is 1, 1, 1.

      • Chapter: \(p\)-Block Elements
      • Topic: Noble gas compounds
      • Subtopic: Structure and lone pairs in xenon compounds
    • Correct answer: (c) \(SeO_2\) is a gas.
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: The incorrect statement is option (c) because \(SeO_2\) is not a gas at room temperature. It is a colourless solid. The other statements are accepted as correct in this exam context.
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • This is a direct fact-based \(p\)-block question, so the fastest method is to test each statement one by one.
      • \(TeO_2\) can act as an oxidising agent, so statement (a) is taken as correct.
      • \(SeO_3\) is an acidic oxide, so statement (b) is correct.
      • \(SO_2\) commonly behaves as a reducing agent, so statement (d) is also correct.
      • Now look at statement (c): \(SeO_2\) is a gas.
      • This is wrong because selenium dioxide is a colourless solid at room temperature.
      • Hence option (c) is the incorrect statement.
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This question belongs to the chemistry of group 16 oxides.
      • Non-metal oxides are often acidic in nature. That is why \(SeO_3\) is treated as an acidic oxide.
      • \(SO_2\) is well known as a reducing agent because sulphur in \(+4\) oxidation state can be oxidised further to \(+6\).
      • \(SeO_2\) is an important laboratory reagent and exists as a solid under ordinary conditions.
      • So the trap here is physical state, not formula or oxidation number.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (c) says \(SeO_2\) is a gas.
      • But at room temperature \(SeO_2\) is actually a colourless solid.
      • Therefore option (c) is the one that is not correct.
    • Why other options are not wrong
      • \(TeO_2\) is treated as an oxidising agent in this exam memory-based context.
      • \(SeO_3\) is acidic in nature because it is an acidic oxide.
      • \(SO_2\) is a reducing agent and is commonly used as one.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • In such questions, first check whether one option contains a physical-state trap such as solid, liquid, or gas.
      • These are often used to test memory directly.
      • Remember:
        • \(SO_2\): reducing agent
        • \(SeO_2\): colourless solid
        • \(SeO_3\): acidic oxide
    • Final result
      • The incorrect statement is (c) \(SeO_2\) is a gas.

    2024

    Answer: \((b)\ (ii), (iii)\ \text{only}\)


    p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR

    Solution:

    1. Check each compound on heating

      (i) Sodium nitrate

      On heating, alkali metal nitrates except lithium nitrate generally give nitrite and oxygen.

      \(2\mathrm{NaNO_3} \xrightarrow{\Delta} 2\mathrm{NaNO_2} + \mathrm{O_2}\uparrow\)

      So, sodium nitrate does not liberate dinitrogen.

      (ii) Ammonium dichromate

      On heating, ammonium dichromate decomposes to chromium(III) oxide, dinitrogen and water vapour.

      \((\mathrm{NH_4})_2\mathrm{Cr_2O_7} \xrightarrow{\Delta} \mathrm{Cr_2O_3} + \mathrm{N_2}\uparrow + 4\mathrm{H_2O}\uparrow\)

      So, ammonium dichromate does liberate dinitrogen.

      (iii) Barium azide

      Metal azides on heating liberate nitrogen gas.

      \(\mathrm{Ba(N_3)_2} \xrightarrow{\Delta} \mathrm{Ba} + 3\mathrm{N_2}\uparrow\)

      So, barium azide does liberate dinitrogen.

    2. Now compare with the options
      • \((i)\) does not give \(\mathrm{N_2}\)
      • \((ii)\) gives \(\mathrm{N_2}\)
      • \((iii)\) gives \(\mathrm{N_2}\)

      Hence the correct choice is \((ii)\) and \((iii)\) only.

    Therefore:

    \(\boxed{(b)\ (ii), (iii)\ \text{only}}\)

    Why this method works:

    • These questions are solved by recalling the standard thermal decomposition products of important inorganic compounds.
    • Nitrates, ammonium salts and azides show very characteristic decomposition patterns.
    • So instead of doing any long calculation, you identify the gas evolved from each compound.

    JEE / NCERT takeaway:

    • Alkali metal nitrates \((\text{except lithium})\) on heating give nitrite and oxygen:
    • \(2\mathrm{MNO_3} \xrightarrow{\Delta} 2\mathrm{MNO_2} + \mathrm{O_2}\uparrow\)
    • Ammonium dichromate on heating gives:
    • \((\mathrm{NH_4})_2\mathrm{Cr_2O_7} \xrightarrow{\Delta} \mathrm{Cr_2O_3} + \mathrm{N_2} + 4\mathrm{H_2O}\)
    • This is the famous “volcano” decomposition reaction.
    • Metal azides on heating liberate nitrogen gas:
    • \(\mathrm{M(N_3)_x} \xrightarrow{\Delta} \text{metal} + \mathrm{N_2}\)
    • So azides are important laboratory sources of dinitrogen.

    Useful pattern memory:

    • Nitrate \(\Rightarrow\) usually think \(\mathrm{O_2}\)
    • Azide \(\Rightarrow\) think \(\mathrm{N_2}\)
    • Ammonium dichromate \(\Rightarrow\) think \(\mathrm{N_2 + H_2O + Cr_2O_3}\)

    1-minute solving trick:

    • Do not try to reason from scratch. Recall the standard reactions directly.
    • \(\mathrm{NaNO_3}\) on heating gives \(\mathrm{O_2}\), not \(\mathrm{N_2}\).
    • Azides almost immediately suggest liberation of \(\mathrm{N_2}\).
    • Ammonium dichromate is a well-known reaction that also gives \(\mathrm{N_2}\).
    • So mark \((ii)\) and \((iii)\) only.

    Common mistakes to avoid:

    1. Assuming every nitrogen-containing compound gives nitrogen gas on heating.
    2. Confusing nitrate decomposition with azide decomposition.
    3. Forgetting that sodium nitrate gives oxygen, not dinitrogen.
    4. Mixing up alkali metal nitrate behavior with ammonium salts.

    Chapter / Topic / Subtopic:

    • Chapter: The p-Block Elements
    • Topic: Dinitrogen and nitrogen compounds
    • Subtopic: Preparation / liberation of dinitrogen by thermal decomposition

    Final exam-style answer:

    On heating, sodium nitrate decomposes as:

    \(2\mathrm{NaNO_3} \xrightarrow{\Delta} 2\mathrm{NaNO_2} + \mathrm{O_2}\uparrow\)

    So it does not liberate dinitrogen.

    Ammonium dichromate decomposes as:

    \((\mathrm{NH_4})_2\mathrm{Cr_2O_7} \xrightarrow{\Delta} \mathrm{Cr_2O_3} + \mathrm{N_2}\uparrow + 4\mathrm{H_2O}\uparrow\)

    Barium azide decomposes as:

    \(\mathrm{Ba(N_3)_2} \xrightarrow{\Delta} \mathrm{Ba} + 3\mathrm{N_2}\uparrow\)

    Hence, the compounds which liberate dinitrogen are \((ii)\) and \((iii)\) only.

    \(\boxed{(b)\ (ii), (iii)\ \text{only}}\)

    Quick cheatsheet:

    • \(\mathrm{NaNO_3}\) on heating: \(\mathrm{NaNO_2 + O_2}\)
    • \((\mathrm{NH_4})_2\mathrm{Cr_2O_7}\) on heating: \(\mathrm{Cr_2O_3 + N_2 + H_2O}\)
    • \(\mathrm{Ba(N_3)_2}\) on heating: \(\mathrm{Ba + 3N_2}\)
    • Azides: very important source of \(\mathrm{N_2}\)
    • Nitrates: commonly give \(\mathrm{O_2}\) on thermal decomposition

    Answer: \((d)\ \text{Deacon's process}\)


    p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR

    Solution:

    1. Identify the reaction

      The given reaction is:

      \(4\mathrm{HCl} + \mathrm{O_2} \xrightarrow[\ 723\ \mathrm{K}\ ]{\ \mathrm{CuCl_2}\ } 2\mathrm{Cl_2} + 2\mathrm{H_2O}\)

      This is the catalytic oxidation of hydrogen chloride to chlorine gas.

      This named process is Deacon's process.

    2. Why option \((d)\) is correct

      In Deacon's process, hydrogen chloride is oxidised by atmospheric oxygen in the presence of a catalyst such as \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\) at high temperature.

      The main purpose is to prepare chlorine gas.

      So the reaction directly matches Deacon's process.

    3. Understand the redox idea

      Here, chloride ion from \(\mathrm{HCl}\) is converted into chlorine:

      \(\mathrm{2Cl^- \rightarrow Cl_2 + 2e^-}\)

      So \(\mathrm{HCl}\) is oxidised.

      Oxygen acts as the oxidising agent and finally forms water.

    4. Why the other options are wrong
      • van-Arkel process: used for purification of metals like titanium and zirconium through volatile iodides.
      • Hall-Heroult process: used for extraction of aluminium from alumina.
      • Serpeck's process: used for purification/conversion related to bauxite in metallurgy.
      • Deacon's process: specifically associated with preparation of chlorine from \(\mathrm{HCl}\).

    Therefore:

    \(\boxed{(d)\ \text{Deacon's process}}\)

    Why this method works:

    • Named-process questions are usually solved by matching the reaction pattern with the industrial method.
    • Whenever you see \(\mathrm{HCl}\) being oxidised by \(\mathrm{O_2}\) to give \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\), think of Deacon's process.
    • The catalyst \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\) and temperature around \(723\ \mathrm{K}\) are classic clues.

    JEE / NCERT takeaway:

    • Deacon's process: preparation of chlorine by oxidation of \(\mathrm{HCl}\).
    • Reaction:
    • \(4\mathrm{HCl} + \mathrm{O_2} \xrightarrow[\ 723\ \mathrm{K}\ ]{\ \mathrm{CuCl_2}\ } 2\mathrm{Cl_2} + 2\mathrm{H_2O}\)
    • \(\mathrm{HCl}\) acts as a reducing agent and gets oxidised to \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\).
    • \(\mathrm{O_2}\) is reduced to water.
    • This is an important industrial method for obtaining chlorine from hydrogen chloride.

    Related theory you should remember:

    • Chlorine is an important p-block industrial chemical.
    • It can be prepared by several methods, but Deacon's process is the classic named reaction involving oxidation of \(\mathrm{HCl}\).
    • The catalyst speeds up the reaction and improves conversion.
    • High temperature is needed, but the process is catalytic, so the catalyst is not consumed.

    Quick process-identification map:

    • \(\mathrm{HCl \rightarrow Cl_2}\) with \(\mathrm{O_2}\), catalyst, heat \(\Rightarrow\) Deacon's process
    • Aluminium extraction from alumina \(\Rightarrow\) Hall-Heroult process
    • Metal purification through volatile iodide \(\Rightarrow\) van-Arkel process
    • Bauxite-related purification step \(\Rightarrow\) Serpeck's process

    1-minute solving trick:

    • Look only at the product side first.
    • If \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) is being formed from \(\mathrm{HCl}\), immediately suspect Deacon's process.
    • Then confirm by spotting \(\mathrm{O_2}\), catalyst \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\), and high temperature.
    • This is enough to mark the answer in a few seconds.

    Common mistakes to avoid:

    1. Confusing all named industrial processes with metallurgy-only processes.
    2. Choosing Hall-Heroult just because it is a familiar industrial name.
    3. Ignoring the actual reaction and answering from memory without matching reactants and products.
    4. Not noticing the clue that chlorine gas is produced from \(\mathrm{HCl}\).

    Chapter / Topic / Subtopic:

    • Chapter: The p-Block Elements
    • Topic: Chlorine and its preparation
    • Subtopic: Deacon's process

    Final exam-style answer:

    The reaction

    \(4\mathrm{HCl} + \mathrm{O_2} \xrightarrow[\ 723\ \mathrm{K}\ ]{\ \mathrm{CuCl_2}\ } 2\mathrm{Cl_2} + 2\mathrm{H_2O}\)

    represents oxidation of hydrogen chloride to chlorine gas in presence of \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\) catalyst.

    This is called Deacon's process.

    \(\boxed{(d)\ \text{Deacon's process}}\)

    Quick cheatsheet:

    • Deacon's process: chlorine from \(\mathrm{HCl}\)
    • Key reaction: \(4\mathrm{HCl} + \mathrm{O_2} \rightarrow 2\mathrm{Cl_2} + 2\mathrm{H_2O}\)
    • Catalyst: \(\mathrm{CuCl_2}\)
    • Temperature clue: around \(723\ \mathrm{K}\)
    • Hall-Heroult: extraction of aluminium
    • van-Arkel: purification via volatile iodides
    • Serpeck's process: related to bauxite/aluminium ore treatment
