    p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR

    Solution:

    1. Check each match one by one

      (a) \(\mathrm{PH_3}\), colourless gas, rotten fish smell

      This is correct.

      Phosphine \(\mathrm{PH_3}\) is a colourless gas and usually has a foul smell like rotten fish.

      (b) \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\), greenish yellow gas, pungent smell

      This is also correct.

      Chlorine is a greenish yellow gas with a sharp, pungent smell.

      (c) \(\mathrm{Ne}\), fluorescent green gas, rotten egg smell

      This is incorrect.

      Neon is a colourless and odourless noble gas under normal conditions.

      It does not have rotten egg smell.

      Also, neon is not a fluorescent green gas. In discharge tubes, neon shows a bright red-orange glow.

      (d) \(\mathrm{SO_2}\), colourless gas, pungent smell

      This is correct.

      Sulphur dioxide is a colourless gas with a pungent, choking smell.

    2. Find the wrongly matched set

      Only option \((c)\) is not correctly matched.

    Therefore:

    \(\boxed{(c)}\)

    Correct form of option \((c)\):

    • \(\mathrm{Ne}\) is a colourless, odourless gas.
    • When electric discharge is passed through it, it gives a bright red-orange glow.

    Why this method works:

    • This is a direct property-matching question.
    • Such questions are solved by remembering the standard physical properties of important gases.
    • Noble gases are especially easy to identify because they are generally colourless, odourless and chemically inert.

    JEE / NCERT takeaway:

    • \(\mathrm{PH_3}\): colourless gas, rotten fish smell
    • \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\): greenish yellow gas, pungent smell
    • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\): colourless gas, pungent/choking smell
    • \(\mathrm{Ne}\): colourless, odourless noble gas
    • Neon shows a red-orange glow in discharge tubes, which is why it is used in advertising signs.
    • Rotten egg smell is commonly associated with \(\mathrm{H_2S}\), not neon.

    Useful comparison table in words:

    • \(\mathrm{PH_3}\) \(\Rightarrow\) rotten fish smell
    • \(\mathrm{H_2S}\) \(\Rightarrow\) rotten egg smell
    • \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\) \(\Rightarrow\) greenish yellow, pungent
    • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) \(\Rightarrow\) colourless, pungent
    • \(\mathrm{Ne}\) \(\Rightarrow\) colourless, odourless, red-orange glow in discharge

    1-minute solving trick:

    • First look for the most obviously strange option.
    • \(\mathrm{Ne}\) is a noble gas, so it should immediately suggest colourless and odourless.
    • So option \((c)\) stands out at once as wrong.
    • Also remember: rotten egg smell = \(\mathrm{H_2S}\), not neon.

    Common mistakes to avoid:

    1. Confusing rotten fish smell of \(\mathrm{PH_3}\) with rotten egg smell of \(\mathrm{H_2S}\).
    2. Thinking the colour seen in discharge tube is the natural colour of the gas.
    3. Forgetting that noble gases are generally colourless and odourless under ordinary conditions.
    4. Mixing up chlorine and sulphur dioxide properties.

    \(\boxed{(c)}\)

    Quick cheatsheet:

    • \(\mathrm{PH_3}\): colourless, rotten fish smell
    • \(\mathrm{Cl_2}\): greenish yellow, pungent smell
    • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\): colourless, pungent smell

    • Correct answer: (b) \(Cu_3P_2\)
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: In alkaline medium, white phosphorus reacts with \(NaOH\) and water to give phosphine \((PH_3)\) and sodium hypophosphite \((NaH_2PO_2)\). So product \(X\) is \(PH_3\). Then \(PH_3\) reacts with \(CuSO_4\) to form copper phosphide, \(Cu_3P_2\). Hence \(Y = Cu_3P_2\).
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • The first reaction is a well-known reaction of white phosphorus with hot alkali.
      • Its balanced form is: \(P_4 + 3NaOH + 3H_2O \rightarrow PH_3 + 3NaH_2PO_2\)
      • So from the first step, we identify: \(X = PH_3\)
      • Now substitute this into the second reaction: \(PH_3 + CuSO_4 \rightarrow Y + H_2SO_4\)
      • The complete balanced reaction is: \(4PH_3 + 6CuSO_4 \rightarrow 2Cu_3P_2 + 6H_2SO_4\)
      • Therefore, the product formed is \(Y = Cu_3P_2\).
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • White phosphorus \((P_4)\) reacts with alkali and water to produce phosphine and a phosphorus oxyanion salt.
      • Here the salt formed is sodium hypophosphite, \(NaH_2PO_2\).
      • Phosphine \((PH_3)\) behaves as a reducing and reactive phosphorus compound, and with copper sulfate it gives copper phosphide.
      • This is a standard inorganic chemistry memory-based reaction from phosphorus chemistry.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • The whole question depends on correctly identifying \(X\).
      • Once we know \(X = PH_3\), the second reaction directly gives \(Cu_3P_2\).
      • So option (b) is the only chemically correct product.
    • Why other options are wrong:
      • (a) \(Cu_3(PO_4)_2\): This is copper phosphate, which would require phosphate ion \((PO_4^{3-})\), not phosphine.
      • (c) \(Cu(OH)_2\): Copper hydroxide is not the product of \(PH_3\) reacting with \(CuSO_4\).
      • (d) \(CuCO_3\): Carbonate is unrelated here. The \(CO_2\) written over the first arrow is only part of the reaction condition as shown in the question and does not make copper carbonate in the second step.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • Memorize this standard reaction: \(P_4 + NaOH + H_2O \rightarrow PH_3 + NaH_2PO_2\)
      • In exams, if white phosphorus + alkali appears, think phosphine immediately.
      • Then if phosphine reacts with a copper salt, think copper phosphide.
    • Final result:
      • \(X = PH_3\)
      • \(Y = Cu_3P_2\)
      • Hence, the correct answer is (b) \(Cu_3P_2\).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic:
      • Chapter: \(p\)-Block Elements
      • Topic: Phosphorus and its compounds
      • Subtopic: Reaction of white phosphorus with alkali, phosphine chemistry
    • Correct answer: (d) \(Fe_2O_3 \cdot xH_2O\)
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: In the contact process, arsenic impurities must be removed because they poison the catalyst. The arsenic purifier used for this purpose contains hydrated ferric oxide, \(Fe_2O_3 \cdot xH_2O\).
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • In the manufacture of sulfuric acid by the contact process, sulfur or pyrites are first burned to produce \(SO_2\).
      • The gas obtained may contain impurities such as dust and arsenic compounds.
      • These impurities are dangerous because the catalyst used later in the process gets poisoned.
      • The key catalyst in the contact process is \(V_2O_5\).
      • If arsenic is not removed, the efficiency of conversion of \(SO_2\) to \(SO_3\) drops badly.
      • So before the catalytic oxidation step, the gases are purified.
      • The purifier used for removing arsenic contains hydrated ferric oxide, \(Fe_2O_3 \cdot xH_2O\).
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • The contact process has several important stages:
        • formation of \(SO_2\)
        • purification of gases
        • catalytic oxidation of \(SO_2\) to \(SO_3\)
        • absorption of \(SO_3\)
      • Gas purification is a very important industrial step because catalyst poisons reduce the activity of the catalyst.
      • Arsenic compounds are classic catalyst poisons in the contact process.
      • Hydrated ferric oxide is used to trap and remove these arsenic impurities.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • \(Fe_2O_3 \cdot xH_2O\) is the standard arsenic purifier used in the contact process.
      • This is a direct fact-based industrial chemistry question.
      • So option (d) is correct.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable:
      • (a) \(Al_2O_3 \cdot xH_2O\): Not the standard arsenic purifier used in the contact process.
      • (b) \(FeO \cdot xH_2O\): Ferrous oxide hydrate is not the prescribed purifier here.
      • (c) \(Cr_2O_3 \cdot xH_2O\): Also not used for arsenic removal in this process.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • For contact process, remember this chain: arsenic impurity poisons catalyst \(\rightarrow\) remove it before catalytic chamber \(\rightarrow\) purifier is hydrated ferric oxide.
      • If a question asks specifically about the arsenic purifier, the answer is \(Fe_2O_3 \cdot xH_2O\).
      • This is a common one-line memory fact from industrial chemistry.
    • Final result:
      • The arsenic purifier used in the contact process contains \(Fe_2O_3 \cdot xH_2O\).
      • Hence, the correct answer is (d).

      • Chapter: \(p\)-Block Elements / Industrial Chemistry
      • Topic: Manufacture of sulfuric acid by contact process
      • Subtopic: Purification of gases, catalyst poisoning, arsenic purifier
    • Correct answer: (c) \( \mathrm{BiH_3,\ PH_3} \)
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: In group 15 hydrides, boiling point generally increases down the group because molecular mass and van der Waals forces increase. \( \mathrm{NH_3} \) is an exception because of hydrogen bonding, so its boiling point is higher than \( \mathrm{PH_3} \), but still lower than the very heavy \( \mathrm{BiH_3} \). Therefore, the highest boiling point is for \( \mathrm{BiH_3} \), and the lowest is for \( \mathrm{PH_3} \).
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • We compare the boiling points of:
        • \( \mathrm{NH_3} \)
        • \( \mathrm{PH_3} \)
        • \( \mathrm{BiH_3} \)
      • Normally, as we move down group 15, boiling point increases because:
        • size increases,
        • molecular mass increases,
        • dispersion forces become stronger.
      • So from this trend alone, heavier hydrides tend to have higher boiling points.
      • However, \( \mathrm{NH_3} \) is special because it shows intermolecular hydrogen bonding.
      • That is why \( \mathrm{NH_3} \) has a higher boiling point than \( \mathrm{PH_3} \), even though nitrogen hydride is lighter.
      • But when we compare \( \mathrm{NH_3} \) with the much heavier \( \mathrm{BiH_3} \), the large molecular mass and stronger van der Waals forces in \( \mathrm{BiH_3} \) dominate.
      • Thus:
        • highest boiling point \( \rightarrow \mathrm{BiH_3} \)
        • lowest boiling point \( \rightarrow \mathrm{PH_3} \)
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • Boiling point depends on the strength of intermolecular forces.
      • For hydrides of group 15:
        • down the group, London dispersion forces increase,
        • so boiling point tends to increase.
      • \( \mathrm{NH_3} \) is anomalous because it forms hydrogen bonds.
      • Hence its boiling point is higher than expected and becomes higher than \( \mathrm{PH_3} \).
      • But \( \mathrm{BiH_3} \) is very heavy, so its boiling point becomes the highest among the three given hydrides.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (c) correctly identifies:
        • \( \mathrm{X = BiH_3} \) as highest boiling
        • \( \mathrm{Y = PH_3} \) as lowest boiling
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable
      • (a) is wrong because \( \mathrm{PH_3} \) does not have the highest boiling point.
      • (b) is wrong because \( \mathrm{NH_3} \) is not the highest among these three.
      • (d) is invalid as written and also does not match the correct boiling point order.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • For hydrides, always check two things:
        • hydrogen bonding,
        • molecular mass.
      • So here:
        • \( \mathrm{NH_3} \) beats \( \mathrm{PH_3} \) because of hydrogen bonding,
        • but \( \mathrm{BiH_3} \) beats both because of very high mass and strong dispersion forces.
      • This gives the order:
        • \( \mathrm{PH_3 < NH_3 < BiH_3} \)
    • Final result: The hydride with highest boiling point is \( \mathrm{BiH_3} \) and the one with lowest boiling point is \( \mathrm{PH_3} \). Hence, the correct option is (c).

    • Correct answer: (c) 6
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: Xenon(VI) fluoride is \( \mathrm{XeF_6} \). On complete hydrolysis it gives \( \mathrm{XeO_3} \). In \( \mathrm{XeO_3} \), there are three \( \mathrm{Xe=O} \) double bonds, and each double bond contains one \( \mathrm{\sigma} \)-bond and one \( \mathrm{\pi} \)-bond. So total \( \mathrm{\sigma + \pi = 3 + 3 = 6} \).
    • Complete hydrolysis sequence of \( \mathrm{XeF_6} \)
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • The compound mentioned is xenon (VI) fluoride, which is:
        • \( \mathrm{XeF_6} \)
      • Its hydrolysis occurs stepwise:
        • \( \mathrm{XeF_6 + H_2O \rightarrow XeOF_4 + 2HF} \)
        • \( \mathrm{XeOF_4 + H_2O \rightarrow XeO_2F_2 + 2HF} \)
        • \( \mathrm{XeO_2F_2 + H_2O \rightarrow XeO_3 + 2HF} \)
      • So the oxide formed on complete hydrolysis is:
        • \( \mathrm{XeO_3} \)
      • Now look at bonding in \( \mathrm{XeO_3} \).
      • The usual exam representation treats it as having three \( \mathrm{Xe=O} \) double bonds.
      • Each double bond contains:
        • one \( \mathrm{\sigma} \)-bond
        • one \( \mathrm{\pi} \)-bond
      • Therefore:
        • Total \( \mathrm{\sigma} \)-bonds \( \mathrm{= 3} \)
        • Total \( \mathrm{\pi} \)-bonds \( \mathrm{= 3} \)
        • Total \( \mathrm{\sigma + \pi = 6} \)
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This question is from noble gas compounds, especially xenon fluorides and their hydrolysis products.
      • \( \mathrm{XeF_2} \), \( \mathrm{XeF_4} \), and \( \mathrm{XeF_6} \) are important xenon fluorides commonly asked in exams.
      • \( \mathrm{XeF_6} \) on hydrolysis does not directly stop at one stage; it can proceed stepwise to oxygen-containing xenon compounds.
      • On complete hydrolysis, the final oxide is \( \mathrm{XeO_3} \).
      • For bond counting questions, remember:
        • single bond \( \rightarrow \) 1 \( \mathrm{\sigma} \)
        • double bond \( \rightarrow \) 1 \( \mathrm{\sigma} \) + 1 \( \mathrm{\pi} \)
        • triple bond \( \rightarrow \) 1 \( \mathrm{\sigma} \) + 2 \( \mathrm{\pi} \)
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (c) is correct because \( \mathrm{XeO_3} \) has three double bonds, giving total bond count:
        • \( \mathrm{3\ \sigma + 3\ \pi = 6} \)
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable
      • (a) and (b) are too small because \( \mathrm{XeO_3} \) already has three Xe–O connections.
      • (d) would require four double-bond equivalents or some extra bond count, which is not the case here.
      • A common mistake is counting only sigma bonds and forgetting the pi bonds present in double bonds.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Whenever a question asks for total \( \mathrm{\sigma} \) and \( \mathrm{\pi} \)-bonds, first identify the final product correctly.
      • Then draw or recall its basic bond structure.
      • For \( \mathrm{XeF_6} \), remember:
        • complete hydrolysis \( \rightarrow \mathrm{XeO_3} \)
      • Once you recall three \( \mathrm{Xe=O} \) bonds, the answer becomes immediate.
    • Final result: The oxide formed is \( \mathrm{XeO_3} \), and total number of \( \mathrm{\sigma} \) and \( \mathrm{\pi} \)-bonds in it is 6. Hence, the correct option is (c).

    • Correct answer: (d) 1, 1
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: \( \mathrm{P_2O_3} \) reacts with water to form phosphorous acid \( (\mathrm{H_3PO_3}) \), and \( \mathrm{P_4O_{10}} \) reacts with water to form phosphoric acid \( (\mathrm{H_3PO_4}) \). In both of these acids, each molecule contains one \( \mathrm{P=O} \) bond.
    • Structures of products \(X\) and \(Y\)
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • First balance the reactions conceptually.
      • \( \mathrm{P_2O_3} \) is the acidic oxide of phosphorus in lower oxidation state and on hydration it forms phosphorous acid:
        • \(\mathrm{P_2O_3 + 3H_2O \rightarrow 2H_3PO_3}\)
      • So \(X = \mathrm{H_3PO_3}\).
      • \( \mathrm{P_4O_{10}} \) is phosphorus pentoxide and on hydration it forms phosphoric acid:
        • \(\mathrm{P_4O_{10} + 6H_2O \rightarrow 4H_3PO_4}\)
      • So \(Y = \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\).
      • Now count the \( \mathrm{P=O} \) bonds in one molecule of each product.
      • In \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\), the structure is commonly represented as:
        • \(\mathrm{HP(=O)(OH)_2}\)
      • So it contains one \( \mathrm{P=O} \) bond.
      • In \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\), the structure is:
        • \(\mathrm{P(=O)(OH)_3}\)
      • So it also contains one \( \mathrm{P=O} \) bond.
      • Hence the required numbers are \(1,1\).
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • Acidic oxides of non-metals react with water to form corresponding oxyacids.
      • Phosphorus forms several oxides and corresponding oxyacids.
      • A very important structural fact is:
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) is monobasic
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is dibasic
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) is tribasic
      • This basicity depends on the number of ionisable \(\mathrm{OH}\) hydrogens, not on total hydrogens present.
      • In \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\), one hydrogen is directly bonded to phosphorus, so only two \(\mathrm{OH}\) hydrogens are ionisable.
      • But despite that difference, both \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) and \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) contain one terminal \( \mathrm{P=O} \) bond in the usual representation.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (d) is right because:
        • \(X = \mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) has one \( \mathrm{P=O} \) bond
        • \(Y = \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) has one \( \mathrm{P=O} \) bond
      • So the pair is \(1,1\).
    • Why other options are wrong
      • (a), (b), and (c) give incorrect counts for one or both products.
      • The common trap is to overcount oxygen atoms and assume more than one \( \mathrm{P=O} \) bond in the final oxyacid structure.
      • The question asks for the number of \( \mathrm{P=O} \) bonds in the product molecule, not the number of oxygen atoms present.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Memorise these standard structures:
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3 = HP(=O)(OH)_2}\)
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4 = P(=O)(OH)_3}\)
      • Once these are known, the answer becomes immediate.
      • This is also useful in questions on basicity and structure-based acidity.
    • Final result
      • The number of \( \mathrm{P=O} \) bonds in \(X\) and \(Y\) are respectively \(1,1\).
      • So the correct answer is (d).

      • Subtopic: Hydration products and \( \mathrm{P=O} \) bond counting
    • Correct answer: (a) Both are acidic
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: Carbon reacts with hot concentrated sulphuric acid to form \(\mathrm{CO_2}\), \(\mathrm{SO_2}\), and \(\mathrm{H_2O}\). The two oxides formed are \(\mathrm{CO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{SO_2}\), and both of them are acidic oxides.
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • The reaction of carbon with hot concentrated sulphuric acid is:
        • \(\mathrm{C + 2H_2SO_4 \rightarrow CO_2 + 2SO_2 + 2H_2O}\)
      • So the two oxides produced are:
        • \(\mathrm{CO_2}\)
        • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\)
      • Now identify their nature.
      • \(\mathrm{CO_2}\) is an acidic oxide because it reacts with water to form carbonic acid and reacts with bases to form carbonates or bicarbonates.
      • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) is also an acidic oxide because it reacts with water to form sulphurous acid and reacts with bases to form sulphites or bisulphites.
      • Therefore, both oxides are acidic in nature.
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • Oxides are commonly classified as:
        • acidic oxides
        • basic oxides
        • amphoteric oxides
        • neutral oxides
      • Most non-metal oxides are acidic in nature.
      • \(\mathrm{CO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) are oxides of non-metals, so they show acidic character.
      • Examples:
        • \(\mathrm{CO_2 + H_2O \rightarrow H_2CO_3}\)
        • \(\mathrm{SO_2 + H_2O \rightarrow H_2SO_3}\)
      • They also react with alkalis:
        • \(\mathrm{CO_2 + 2NaOH \rightarrow Na_2CO_3 + H_2O}\)
        • \(\mathrm{SO_2 + 2NaOH \rightarrow Na_2SO_3 + H_2O}\)
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (a) is correct because the two oxides actually formed are \(\mathrm{CO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{SO_2}\), and both are acidic oxides.
      • This matches both oxide classification and the given reaction.
    • Why other options are wrong
      • (b) is wrong because neither \(\mathrm{CO_2}\) nor \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) is a basic oxide.
      • (c) is wrong because neutral oxides usually do not show acidic or basic behaviour, but these clearly react with bases.
      • (d) is wrong because amphoteric oxides react with both acids and bases; \(\mathrm{CO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) are not amphoteric.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Whenever oxides of non-metals such as carbon and sulphur appear, first think of acidic oxides.
      • Also remember:
        • \(\mathrm{CO}\) is neutral
        • \(\mathrm{CO_2}\) is acidic
        • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{SO_3}\) are acidic
      • This helps avoid a common trap between \(\mathrm{CO}\) and \(\mathrm{CO_2}\).
    • Final result
      • The two oxides formed are \(\mathrm{CO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{SO_2}\).
      • Both are acidic oxides.
      • So the correct answer is (a).

      • Topic: Oxides of non-metals
      • Subtopic: Acidic nature of \(\mathrm{CO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{SO_2}\)
    • Correct answer: (b) \(CO_2, SO_2\)
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: Concentrated sulphuric acid acts as a strong oxidising agent on heating. It oxidises carbon to \(CO_2\) and itself gets reduced to \(SO_2\). So the products are carbon dioxide and sulphur dioxide.
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • Carbon is being heated with concentrated \(H_2SO_4\).
      • Hot concentrated sulphuric acid behaves as an oxidising agent.
      • So carbon is oxidised: \(C \rightarrow CO_2\)
      • At the same time, sulphur in \(H_2SO_4\) is reduced from oxidation state \(+6\) to \(+4\) in \(SO_2\).
      • The balanced reaction is: \( C + 2H_2SO_4 \rightarrow CO_2 + 2SO_2 + 2H_2O \)
      • Hence:
        • \(X = CO_2\)
        • \(Y = SO_2\)
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • Concentrated \(H_2SO_4\) can behave in different ways:
        • as an acid,
        • as a dehydrating agent,
        • and as an oxidising agent.
      • With carbon on heating, its oxidising nature is important.
      • Carbon is oxidised completely to \(CO_2\), while sulphuric acid is reduced to \(SO_2\).
      • This is a standard redox reaction often asked in entrance exams.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (b) is correct because it matches the balanced redox reaction with hot concentrated sulphuric acid.
    • Why the other options are wrong or less suitable
      • Option (a) is wrong because \(SO_3\) is not the reduction product here.
      • Option (c) is wrong because carbon is oxidised to \(CO_2\), not \(CO\), under this standard reaction condition.
      • Option (d) is incorrect and not relevant to this reaction.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Whenever hot concentrated \(H_2SO_4\) reacts with a reducing substance, think of \(SO_2\) as a common reduction product.
      • For carbon specifically, remember the direct balanced equation: \( C + 2H_2SO_4 \rightarrow CO_2 + 2SO_2 + 2H_2O \).
    • Final result
      • The products are \(CO_2\) and \(SO_2\).

    • Planar structure and bond angles of nitric acid
      • The image shows the experimentally accepted planar arrangement used in this question.
      • From the structure, \(H-O-N = 102^\circ\) and \(O-N-O = 130^\circ\).
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • Nitric acid is \(HNO_3\).
      • Its structure is planar because the nitrogen center is approximately \(sp^2\)-hybridised.
      • There are three oxygen atoms around nitrogen, but the bond angles are not all equal because one oxygen is present as \(-OH\) and the others are involved in \(N-O\) bonding with resonance.
      • The angle at the hydroxyl oxygen, \(H-O-N\), is smaller and is \(102^\circ\).
      • The angle around nitrogen between the two oxygen directions considered here, \(O-N-O\), is larger and is \(130^\circ\).
      • Therefore the correct pair is \(102^\circ\) and \(130^\circ\).
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This is a structure-based memory question from nitrogen oxoacids.
      • The nitric acid molecule is planar due to resonance and the geometry around nitrogen.
      • Because the surrounding atoms are not all identical in bonding environment, the bond angles are unequal.
      • Such questions are usually solved by recalling the standard geometry values rather than deriving them fully from VSEPR alone.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (b) exactly matches the known bond angles in planar nitric acid: \(H-O-N = 102^\circ\) and \(O-N-O = 130^\circ\).
    • Why the other options are wrong or less suitable
      • Option (a) reverses the two angles.
      • Options (c) and (d) use incorrect values for nitric acid geometry.
      • The main exam trap is mixing up which angle belongs at oxygen and which belongs at nitrogen.

      • Remember this standard pair for nitric acid: \(H-O-N = 102^\circ\), \(O-N-O = 130^\circ\).
      • If the same two numbers appear in reversed order in another option, be careful.
    • Final result
      • The bond angles are \(102^\circ\) and \(130^\circ\).

      • (a) \( \mathrm{Ca_3P_2 + 6H_2O \rightarrow 3Ca(OH)_2 + 2PH_3} \)

      • But hydrolysis of covalent halides like \( \mathrm{PCl_3} \) usually gives oxyacids, not hydrides.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Only option (d) fails to give \( \mathrm{PH_3} \), so it is the required answer.
    • Why other options are wrong:
      • Options (a), (b), and (c) are all standard preparations of phosphine.

        • Metal phosphide + water \( \rightarrow \mathrm{PH_3} \)
        • \( \mathrm{PCl_3 + H_2O \rightarrow H_3PO_3} \)
      • If you identify \(Q\) as phosphine quickly, option (d) is eliminated immediately.
    • Final result: \(Q = \mathrm{PH_3}\), and the reaction in which \(Q\) is not obtained is (d).

    • Correct answer: (c)
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: Peroxydisulphuric acid is \( \mathrm{H_2S_2O_8} \). In its structure, both sulphur atoms are in the \(+6\) oxidation state, and there are exactly two \( \mathrm{S{-}OH} \) bonds.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • The formula of peroxydisulphuric acid is \( \mathrm{H_2S_2O_8} \).
      • Its structure contains a peroxide linkage: \( \mathrm{HO{-}S(=O)_2{-}O{-}O{-}S(=O)_2{-}OH} \)
      • Each sulphur is bonded to:
        • two \( \mathrm{S{=}O} \) bonds,
        • one \( \mathrm{S{-}OH} \) bond,
        • one \( \mathrm{S{-}O} \) bond connected to the peroxide bridge.
      • So each sulphur has the same environment and hence the same oxidation state.
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • In peroxo compounds, oxygen in the \( \mathrm{O{-}O} \) bond has oxidation state \( -1 \), not \( -2 \).
      • Let oxidation state of each sulphur be \(x\).
      • Then for \( \mathrm{H_2S_2O_8} \): \( \mathrm{2x + 2(+1) + 6(-2) + 2(-1) = 0} \)
      • \( \mathrm{2x + 2 - 12 - 2 = 0} \)
      • \( \mathrm{2x - 12 = 0} \Rightarrow x = +6 \)
      • Also, from the structure, there are only two \(\mathrm{-OH}\) groups, so the number of \( \mathrm{S{-}OH} \) bonds is \(2\).
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Both sulphur atoms are \(+6\), and total \( \mathrm{S{-}OH} \) bonds are \(2\). Hence option (c) matches exactly.
    • Why other options are wrong:
      • (a) and (d) wrongly assign \(+5\) to sulphur.
      • (b) gives correct oxidation states but wrongly counts \(4\) \( \mathrm{S{-}OH} \) bonds.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • For peroxo acids, first identify the \( \mathrm{O{-}O} \) bond. Those two oxygens must be taken as \( -1 \).
      • Then count actual \(\mathrm{-OH}\) groups directly from the structure rather than from the formula name.
    • Final result: Oxidation states \( = (+6, +6)\), number of \( \mathrm{S{-}OH} \) bonds \( = 2\). So the answer is (c).

    • Explanation: Statements (II) and (III) are correct, while (I) and (IV) are incorrect.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • (I) “Au is soluble in aqua regia but not Pt.”
        This is incorrect because both gold and platinum dissolve in aqua regia.
      • (II) “Among the oxoacids of chlorine highest oxidation state possible for chlorine is \(+7\).”
        This is correct. In \( \mathrm{HClO_4} \) (perchloric acid), chlorine is in the \(+7\) oxidation state.
      • (III) “Among the hydrogen halides lowest boiling point is for HCl.”
        This is correct. HF has unusually high boiling point due to hydrogen bonding, while among HCl, HBr, and HI, boiling point increases with molecular mass. So HCl has the lowest boiling point overall.
      • (IV) “The order of stability of oxides of halogens is \( \mathrm{Cl > Br > I} \).”
        This is incorrect. The correct order is \( \mathrm{I > Cl > Br} \).
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • Aqua regia is a mixture of concentrated \( \mathrm{HCl} \) and concentrated \( \mathrm{HNO_3} \), usually in the ratio \(3:1\).
      • It dissolves noble metals like Au and Pt by forming complex chloro-species.
      • For chlorine oxoacids:
        • \( \mathrm{HClO} \): \(+1\)
        • \( \mathrm{HClO_2} \): \(+3\)
        • \( \mathrm{HClO_3} \): \(+5\)
        • \( \mathrm{HClO_4} \): \(+7\)
      • Hydrogen halides show an exception because HF forms strong intermolecular hydrogen bonding.
      • Oxides of halogens become more stable down the group in the order reflected by iodine oxides being more stable than bromine oxides.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Only statements (II) and (III) survive careful checking, so option (b) is correct.
    • Why other options are wrong:
      • Any option containing statement (I) is wrong because platinum also dissolves in aqua regia.
      • Any option containing statement (IV) is wrong because the stated stability order is not correct.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • Remember two high-yield facts:
        • Both Au and Pt dissolve in aqua regia.
        • HF is the anomalous hydrogen halide because of hydrogen bonding.
      • That alone often narrows the answer quickly.
    • Final result: Correct statements are II and III only. Therefore, the answer is (b).

    • Correct answer: (a)
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: Under the given conditions, \(X\) is \( \mathrm{XeF_6} \). On hydrolysis, \( \mathrm{XeF_6} \) gives \(Y = \mathrm{XeOF_4}\) and \(Z = \mathrm{XeO_3}\). \( \mathrm{XeOF_4} \) is square pyramidal, and \( \mathrm{XeO_3} \) has \(3\sigma\), \(3\pi\) bonds with one lone pair on xenon.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • Xenon reacts with fluorine in excess fluorine and suitable temperature-pressure conditions to form \( \mathrm{XeF_6} \).
      • So, \( \mathrm{Xe + 3F_2 \rightarrow XeF_6} \)
      • Hence \(X = \mathrm{XeF_6}\).
      • On limited hydrolysis: \( \mathrm{XeF_6 + H_2O \rightarrow XeOF_4 + 2HF} \)
      • So \(Y = \mathrm{XeOF_4}\).
      • On further hydrolysis: \( \mathrm{XeF_6 + 3H_2O \rightarrow XeO_3 + 6HF} \)
      • So \(Z = \mathrm{XeO_3}\).
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • For \(Y = \mathrm{XeOF_4}\):
        • Xenon has 6 electron pairs around it: 5 bond pairs and 1 lone pair.
        • Electron pair geometry is octahedral.
        • With one lone pair, the molecular shape becomes square pyramidal.
      • For \(Z = \mathrm{XeO_3}\):
        • Xenon is bonded to three oxygens and has one lone pair.
        • The electron pair arrangement is tetrahedral, but the molecular geometry is trigonal pyramidal.
        • Each \( \mathrm{Xe{=}O} \) bond contributes one \(\sigma\) and one \(\pi\) bond.
        • Therefore \( \mathrm{XeO_3} \) has \(3\sigma\) and \(3\pi\) bonds, with one lone pair on xenon.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Statement (I) is correct because \( \mathrm{XeOF_4} \) is square pyramidal.
      • Statement (III) is correct because \( \mathrm{XeO_3} \) has three double bonds and one lone pair on xenon.
      • Thus option (a), I and III only, is correct.
    • Why other options are wrong:
      • (II) is wrong because \(Y\) is not linear.
      • (IV) is wrong because \(Z\) is not tetrahedral in molecular shape; it is trigonal pyramidal.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • Do not confuse electron pair geometry with molecular geometry.
      • For \( \mathrm{XeO_3} \), the electron pair arrangement is tetrahedral, but because one position is occupied by a lone pair, the actual shape is trigonal pyramidal.
      • This is a very common exam trap.
    • Final result: Correct statements are I and III only. Therefore, the answer is (a).

    • Correct answer: (b) (A) and (R) are true but (R) is not correct explanation of (A).
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: Nitrogen does not form pentahalides because it does not have vacant \(d\)-orbitals in its valence shell and hence cannot expand its covalency beyond four. However, nitrogen can still show \(+5\) oxidation state in compounds like \(HNO_3\), \(N_2O_5\) and nitrates. So both Assertion and Reason are true, but the Reason does not explain the Assertion.
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • First check the Assertion:
        • Nitrogen belongs to group 15.
        • Its electronic configuration is \(1s^2\,2s^2\,2p^3\).
        • The valence shell is the second shell, which contains only \(2s\) and \(2p\) orbitals.
        • There are no vacant \(d\)-orbitals in the valence shell of nitrogen.
        • So nitrogen cannot increase its covalency to 5 and therefore does not form pentahalides such as \(NCl_5\) or \(NF_5\).
        • Hence, Assertion (A) is true.
      • Now check the Reason:
        • Nitrogen can show oxidation states from \(-3\) to \(+5\).
        • In compounds like \(HNO_3\), \(N_2O_5\), \(NO_3^-\), nitrogen is in the \(+5\) oxidation state.
        • So Reason (R) is also true.
      • Now see whether the Reason explains the Assertion:
        • The statement “nitrogen can exhibit \(+5\) oxidation state” tells us about oxidation state.
        • But pentahalide formation depends on covalency and the availability of orbitals to make five bonds.
        • Oxidation state and covalency are related ideas, but they are not the same thing.
        • Thus the Reason is true, but it is not the correct explanation for why nitrogen does not form pentahalides.
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This is a classic distinction between oxidation state and covalency.
      • Oxidation state is a formal charge-like concept used for electron bookkeeping.
      • Covalency means the number of covalent bonds formed by an atom.
      • Nitrogen can reach \(+5\) oxidation state because when bonded to more electronegative atoms like oxygen, the shared electrons are assigned away from nitrogen in oxidation number calculation.
      • But to form a pentahalide, nitrogen would need to form five covalent bonds around itself.
      • Since nitrogen is a second-period element, it cannot expand its octet and cannot accommodate five bonding pairs around the central atom.
      • This is why nitrogen forms trihalides like \(NCl_3\), but not pentahalides, whereas phosphorus can form \(PCl_5\) and \(PF_5\).
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Assertion is true: nitrogen does not form pentahalides.
      • Reason is true: nitrogen can show \(+5\) oxidation state.
      • But the actual reason for non-formation of pentahalides is the absence of available orbitals for expanding covalency, not inability to show \(+5\) oxidation state.
      • So option (b) is correct.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable
      • Option (a) is wrong because although both statements are true, the Reason does not explain the Assertion.
      • Option (c) is wrong because the Reason is not false; nitrogen really can show \(+5\) oxidation state.
      • Option (d) is wrong because the Assertion is also true.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Whenever you see nitrogen and pentahalides, immediately recall:
        • second period element
        • no octet expansion
        • no pentahalides
      • Also remember this high-yield comparison:
        • \(N\): no \(NX_5\)
        • \(P\): forms \(PX_5\)
      • If the reason talks only about oxidation state, be careful: that often does not fully explain covalency-based behaviour.
    • Final result
      • Both Assertion and Reason are true, but Reason is not the correct explanation of Assertion.
      • Answer: (b)
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic
      • Chapter: The p-Block Elements
      • Topic: Group 15 elements
      • Subtopic: Pentahalide formation, oxidation state and covalency of nitrogen
    • Correct answer: (c) \(P_4 < S_6 < S_8 < O_3\)
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: We compare the approximate bond angles in all four molecules: in \(P_4\), the \(P-P-P\) angle is \(60^\circ\); in \(S_6\), the \(S-S-S\) angle is about \(102.2^\circ\); in \(S_8\), it is about \(107^\circ\); and in \(O_3\), the bond angle is about \(116.8^\circ\). Therefore the increasing order is \(P_4 < S_6 < S_8 < O_3\).
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • To solve this question, we must know or infer the geometry of each molecule and then compare their bond angles.
      • Bond-angle sketches from the given solution
      • \(P_4\)
        • \(P_4\) has a tetrahedral cage-like structure.
        • Each phosphorus atom lies at a corner of a regular tetrahedron.
        • Because of this strained tetrahedral cage, the \(P-P-P\) bond angle is only \(60^\circ\).
      • \(S_6\)
        • \(S_6\) exists in a chair-like ring form.
        • The \(S-S-S\) bond angle is about \(102.2^\circ\).
      • \(S_8\)
        • \(S_8\) is the familiar crown-shaped sulphur ring.
        • The \(S-S-S\) bond angle is about \(107^\circ\).
      • \(O_3\)
        • Ozone is bent.
        • The central oxygen is \(sp^2\)-hybridised in the usual simplified description.
        • The ideal trigonal planar angle would be \(120^\circ\), but because of lone pair-bond pair repulsion, it decreases to about \(116.8^\circ\).
      • Now arrange all values in increasing order:
        • \(60^\circ < 102.2^\circ < 107^\circ < 116.8^\circ\)
        • So, \(P_4 < S_6 < S_8 < O_3\)
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This question mixes molecular shape memory with bond-angle comparison.
      • \(P_4\) is special because it is highly strained. A \(60^\circ\) angle is far smaller than the ideal tetrahedral angle of \(109.5^\circ\), which makes \(P_4\) unusually reactive compared with many other phosphorus forms.
      • \(S_6\) and \(S_8\) are cyclic sulphur allotropes with different ring conformations, so their bond angles are different.
      • \(O_3\) is bent due to the presence of a lone pair on the central oxygen, but its angle still remains much larger than the sulphur ring angles.
      • In such questions, exact values need not always be freshly derived; many are standard remembered structural facts from inorganic chemistry.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • The bond angles are:
        • \(P_4 = 60^\circ\)
        • \(S_6 = 102.2^\circ\)
        • \(S_8 = 107^\circ\)
        • \(O_3 = 116.8^\circ\)
      • This exactly matches option (c).
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable
      • Any option placing \(P_4\) anywhere other than the smallest is incorrect, because \(60^\circ\) is by far the least angle here.
      • Any option placing \(O_3\) below \(S_6\) or \(S_8\) is incorrect because \(116.8^\circ\) is the largest angle in this set.
      • Between \(S_6\) and \(S_8\), remember \(102.2^\circ < 107^\circ\), so \(S_6\) must come before \(S_8\).
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Anchor the two extremes first:
        • \(P_4\) is smallest due to \(60^\circ\) strained cage
        • \(O_3\) is largest at about \(116.8^\circ\)
      • Then only compare \(S_6\) and \(S_8\):
        • \(S_6 \approx 102^\circ\)
        • \(S_8 \approx 107^\circ\)
      • This lets you reach the correct answer very quickly even if you do not remember every value perfectly at first glance.
    • Final result
      • The correct increasing order of bond angles is \(P_4 < S_6 < S_8 < O_3\).

    • Explanation: We count the bond pairs and lone pairs around xenon in each molecule using the valence electron count and the standard structures of xenon fluorides and oxyfluoride. The lone pairs on xenon are: \(XeOF_4 \rightarrow 1\), \(XeF_4 \rightarrow 2\), \(XeF_2 \rightarrow 3\), and \(XeF_6 \rightarrow 1\).
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • Structures and lone pairs around xenon
      • For \(XeOF_4\)
        • Xenon has 8 valence electrons.
        • In \(XeOF_4\), xenon is bonded to four fluorine atoms and one oxygen atom.
        • That gives five bonding regions around xenon.
        • The standard structure has one lone pair on xenon.
        • So, lone pairs in \(XeOF_4 = 1\).
      • For \(XeF_4\)
        • Xenon forms four \(Xe-F\) bonds.
        • There are six electron pairs around xenon in total.
        • Out of these, four are bond pairs and two are lone pairs.
        • That is why \(XeF_4\) has square planar shape.
        • So, lone pairs in \(XeF_4 = 2\).
      • For \(XeF_2\)
        • Xenon forms two \(Xe-F\) bonds.
        • There are five electron pairs around xenon.
        • Out of these, two are bond pairs and three are lone pairs.
        • That gives linear molecular shape after the three lone pairs occupy equatorial positions.
        • So, lone pairs in \(XeF_2 = 3\).
      • For \(XeF_6\)
        • Xenon forms six \(Xe-F\) bonds.
        • One lone pair remains on xenon.
        • So, lone pairs in \(XeF_6 = 1\).
      • Hence the required sequence is:
        • \(1, 2, 3, 1\)
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • Xenon is a noble gas, but it can form compounds with highly electronegative atoms like fluorine and oxygen.
      • To determine lone pairs, we use VSEPR theory and valence electron counting.
      • Important standard results to remember:
        • \(XeF_2\): \(3\) lone pairs
        • \(XeF_4\): \(2\) lone pairs
        • \(XeF_6\): \(1\) lone pair
        • \(XeOF_4\): \(1\) lone pair
      • These are very frequently asked direct-concept questions from noble gas compounds.
      • You should also connect them with shape:
        • \(XeF_2\): linear
        • \(XeF_4\): square planar
        • \(XeF_6\): distorted octahedral
        • \(XeOF_4\): square pyramidal
    • Why the correct option is right
      • The lone pair counts are exactly:
        • \(XeOF_4 = 1\)
        • \(XeF_4 = 2\)
        • \(XeF_2 = 3\)
        • \(XeF_6 = 1\)
      • So the sequence is \(1, 2, 3, 1\), which matches option (a).
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable
      • Any option that gives \(XeF_2\) anything other than 3 lone pairs is wrong.
      • Any option that gives \(XeF_4\) anything other than 2 lone pairs is wrong.
      • \(XeF_6\) does not have zero lone pairs; it has one lone pair.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Memorise the descending pattern:
        • \(XeF_2 \rightarrow 3\) lone pairs
        • \(XeF_4 \rightarrow 2\) lone pairs
        • \(XeF_6 \rightarrow 1\) lone pair
      • Then separately remember \(XeOF_4\) also has \(1\) lone pair.
      • This lets you answer the whole question in seconds.
    • Final result
      • The required numbers of lone pairs are 1, 2, 3, 1.

      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: Out of A, B, C and D, statement C is incorrect because the thermal stability order of group 15 hydrides is actually \( NH_3 > PH_3 > AsH_3 > SbH_3 \), not \( PH_3 > AsH_3 > SbH_3 > NH_3 \).
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • We must check each order one by one instead of trying to guess the final option directly.
      • A. Bond angle: In group 15 hydrides, bond angle decreases down the group:
        • \( NH_3 > PH_3 > AsH_3 > SbH_3 \)
        • This is correct.
      • B. Basic character: Basic character also decreases down the group:
        • \( NH_3 > PH_3 > AsH_3 > SbH_3 \)
        • This is correct.
      • C. Thermal stability: Hydrides become less thermally stable as we move down the group:
        • Actual order: \( NH_3 > PH_3 > AsH_3 > SbH_3 \)
        • Given order places \( NH_3 \) last, which is wrong.
        • So statement C is incorrect.
      • D. Boiling point: Because of strong hydrogen bonding, \( NH_3 \) has an unusually high boiling point. Among the heavier hydrides, boiling point increases with molecular mass:
        • \( PH_3 < AsH_3 < NH_3 < SbH_3 \)
        • So written in descending order, it becomes \( SbH_3 > NH_3 > AsH_3 > PH_3 \).
        • This is correct.
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • Bond angle: Down the group, the central atom becomes larger and hybridisation becomes less effective. The bond angle gradually approaches \( 90^\circ \), so it decreases from \( NH_3 \) to \( SbH_3 \).
      • Basic character: \( NH_3 \) is most basic because the lone pair on nitrogen is more available for donation. Down the group, orbital size increases and overlap with \( H^+ \) becomes less effective, so basic strength decreases.
      • Thermal stability: Thermal stability depends strongly on bond strength. \( N-H \) bond is strongest, while \( Sb-H \) bond is weakest. Hence stability decreases down the group.
      • Boiling point: Normally, boiling point increases with molecular size and van der Waals forces. But \( NH_3 \) shows hydrogen bonding, so its boiling point is higher than expected and becomes greater than \( AsH_3 \) and \( PH_3 \), though still less than \( SbH_3 \).
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • A is correct.
      • B is correct.
      • D is correct.
      • Only C is wrong, so the correct choice is \( (d) \).
    • Why the other options are wrong:
      • Options (a), (b), and (c) all treat statement C as correct in some form.
      • Since C is actually wrong, all those options must be rejected.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • For group 15 hydrides, remember three quick trends:
        • Bond angle decreases down the group.
        • Basic character decreases down the group.
        • Thermal stability decreases down the group.
      • For boiling point, do not forget the hydrogen-bonding exception of \( NH_3 \).
      • If you remember that \( NH_3 \) is the most thermally stable, statement C immediately looks suspicious.
    • Final result:
      • The incorrect statement is C.
      • Therefore, the correct answer is \( (d) \) A, B and D only.

    • Explanation: \( SO_2 \) is the strongest reducing agent here because sulphur is in the \( +4 \) oxidation state in \( SO_2 \), so it can still get oxidised to \( +6 \). A reducing agent reduces others by itself getting oxidised.
    • Oxidation-state comparison:
      • In \( SO_2 \), oxygen is \( -2 \) each, so sulphur is \( +4 \).
      • In \( SO_3 \), sulphur is already \( +6 \), which is the maximum common oxidation state for sulphur in these oxides.
      • In \( TeO_2 \), tellurium is \( +4 \).
      • In \( TeO_3 \), tellurium is \( +6 \).
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • A reducing agent should be able to lose electrons or move to a higher oxidation state.
      • \( SO_2 \) has sulphur in \( +4 \), so it can be oxidised to \( +6 \), for example into sulphate or \( SO_3 \).
      • That means \( SO_2 \) can donate electrons indirectly in a redox process and reduce another substance.
      • \( SO_3 \) already has sulphur at \( +6 \), so it cannot easily go higher. Therefore it is not a good reducing agent.
      • \( TeO_3 \) also has tellurium at \( +6 \), so it is not a reducing agent here.
      • \( TeO_2 \) has tellurium at \( +4 \), but among the given species, \( SO_2 \) is the well-known stronger reducing oxide.
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • A reducing agent is a species that causes reduction of another species and itself undergoes oxidation.
      • So while checking reducing strength, first look at the oxidation state of the central atom.
      • If the central atom is already at its highest common oxidation state, that compound usually behaves as an oxidising agent or is not reducing.
      • If the central atom is in an intermediate oxidation state, it may either get oxidised or reduced, depending on conditions.
      • \( SO_2 \) is a classic example of a reducing agent because sulphur in \( +4 \) easily goes to \( +6 \).
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • \( SO_2 \) contains sulphur in \( +4 \) oxidation state.
      • It can be oxidised to \( +6 \).
      • Hence it acts as a reducing agent, and among the given choices it is the strongest one.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable:
      • \( SO_3 \): sulphur is already at \( +6 \), so it cannot act as a reducing agent in the usual sense.
      • \( TeO_3 \): tellurium is at \( +6 \), so again it is not reducing here.
      • \( TeO_2 \): tellurium is at \( +4 \), but it is not as strong a reducing agent as \( SO_2 \) in the context of this question.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • First eliminate species where the central atom is already in the highest oxidation state.
      • That immediately removes \( SO_3 \) and \( TeO_3 \).
      • Then compare the remaining \( +4 \) oxides. In standard \( p \)-block redox chemistry, \( SO_2 \) is the familiar strong reducing agent.
    • Final result:
      • The strongest reducing agent among the given compounds is \( SO_2 \).

    • Correct answer: (c) A-III; B-IV; C-I; D-II.
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: We identify the shape of each molecule using VSEPR theory by counting bond pairs and lone pairs around the central atom, then match them with the given list.
    • Molecular shapes from the given structures:
      • \( XeF_4 \) is square planar.
      • \( ClF_3 \) is bent T-shape, that is, T-shaped.
      • \( BrF_5 \) is square pyramidal.
      • \( IF_7 \) is pentagonal bipyramidal.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • A. \( XeF_4 \):
        • Xenon has 8 valence electrons.
        • It forms 4 bonds with fluorine and has 2 lone pairs.
        • So total electron pairs around Xe = 6.
        • Electronic geometry = octahedral.
        • With 2 lone pairs opposite to each other, the molecular shape becomes square planar.
        • So \( A \to III \).
      • B. \( ClF_3 \):
        • Chlorine forms 3 bonds and has 2 lone pairs.
        • Total electron pairs = 5.
        • Electronic geometry = trigonal bipyramidal.
        • Two lone pairs occupy equatorial positions to minimise repulsion.
        • The remaining atoms form a T-shaped molecule.
        • In the list, this is written as bent T-shape.
        • So \( B \to IV \).
      • C. \( BrF_5 \):
        • Bromine forms 5 bonds and has 1 lone pair.
        • Total electron pairs = 6.
        • Electronic geometry = octahedral.
        • With one lone pair, the molecular shape becomes square pyramidal.
        • So \( C \to I \).
      • D. \( IF_7 \):
        • Iodine forms 7 bonds and has no lone pair.
        • Total electron pairs = 7.
        • The shape is pentagonal bipyramidal.
        • So \( D \to II \).
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • This question is based on VSEPR theory (Valence Shell Electron Pair Repulsion theory).
      • According to VSEPR, electron pairs around the central atom arrange themselves to minimise repulsion.
      • The important order of repulsion is:
        • lone pair-lone pair \( > \) lone pair-bond pair \( > \) bond pair-bond pair
      • So first count total electron pairs to get electronic geometry, then remove lone-pair positions mentally to get the actual molecular shape.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • \( XeF_4 \to \) square planar \( \to III \)
      • \( ClF_3 \to \) bent T-shape / T-shaped \( \to IV \)
      • \( BrF_5 \to \) square pyramidal \( \to I \)
      • \( IF_7 \to \) pentagonal bipyramidal \( \to II \)
      • Therefore, the correct matching is \( A-III,\; B-IV,\; C-I,\; D-II \).
    • Why other options are wrong:
      • Any option that gives \( ClF_3 \) as square pyramidal or pentagonal bipyramidal is immediately wrong.
      • Any option that gives \( IF_7 \) anything other than pentagonal bipyramidal is wrong.
      • \( XeF_4 \) is a standard memory-based shape: square planar. So that is another strong elimination clue.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • Memorise these four high-frequency examples:
        • \( XeF_4 \): square planar
        • \( ClF_3 \): T-shaped
        • \( BrF_5 \): square pyramidal
        • \( IF_7 \): pentagonal bipyramidal
      • In matching questions, one sure molecule like \( IF_7 \) can help eliminate wrong options very fast.
    • Final result:
      • The correct match is \( A-III,\; B-IV,\; C-I,\; D-II \).
      • Hence the correct option is \( (c) \).

    • Explanation: Lone pair-lone pair repulsion depends on how many lone pairs are present on the central atom. Among the given molecules, \( XeF_2 \) has the maximum number of lone pairs on the central atom, so it has the maximum lone pair-lone pair repulsion.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • Let us count bond pairs and lone pairs on the central atom in each molecule.
      • \( ClF_3 \): Cl has 3 bond pairs and 2 lone pairs.
      • \( IF_5 \): I has 5 bond pairs and 1 lone pair.
      • \( SF_4 \): S has 4 bond pairs and 1 lone pair.
      • \( XeF_2 \): Xe has 2 bond pairs and 3 lone pairs.
      • Since \( XeF_2 \) has 3 lone pairs, it has the greatest lone pair-lone pair interaction among the given options.
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • According to VSEPR theory, electron pairs repel each other.
      • The strength of repulsion follows:
        • lone pair-lone pair \( > \) lone pair-bond pair \( > \) bond pair-bond pair
      • This happens because lone pairs are held only by one nucleus, so they spread out more in space and repel more strongly.
      • Therefore, a molecule with more lone pairs on the central atom generally has greater lone pair-lone pair repulsion.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • \( XeF_2 \) has 3 lone pairs on xenon.
      • That is the highest among the four options.
      • Hence it shows maximum lone pair-lone pair repulsion.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable:
      • \( ClF_3 \) has 2 lone pairs, so its lone pair-lone pair repulsion is less than that of \( XeF_2 \).
      • \( IF_5 \) has only 1 lone pair.
      • \( SF_4 \) also has only 1 lone pair.
      • So none of them can exceed \( XeF_2 \).
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • In such questions, do not focus first on shape. First count lone pairs on the central atom.
      • The molecule with the largest number of lone pairs usually gives the maximum lone pair-lone pair repulsion.
      • Quick memory set:
        • \( ClF_3 \): 2 lone pairs
        • \( IF_5 \): 1 lone pair
        • \( SF_4 \): 1 lone pair
        • \( XeF_2 \): 3 lone pairs
    • Final result:
      • The compound having maximum lone pair-lone pair repulsion is \( XeF_2 \).
      • Hence the correct option is \( (d) \).

    • Correct answer: (c) Rhombic sulphur is stable at room temperature.
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: Among the given statements, only statement (c) is correct. \(N_2\) is a colourless gas, not brown. Ozone is thermodynamically less stable than ordinary oxygen. Chlorine gas is greenish-yellow, not colourless. Rhombic sulphur is the stable allotrope of sulphur at room temperature, so option (c) is the correct statement.
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • This is a direct fact-based question, but it tests several important standard properties from p-block chemistry.
      • Let us check each statement one by one.
      • (a) \(N_2\) is a brown coloured gas. This is false.
      • Nitrogen gas is:
        • colourless
        • odourless
        • tasteless
      • The gas commonly remembered as brown is \(NO_2\), not \(N_2\).
      • (b) \(O_3\) is thermodynamically stable compared to oxygen. This is false.
      • Ozone \((O_3)\) is less stable than dioxygen \((O_2)\). It decomposes to oxygen and this conversion is thermodynamically favorable.
      • (c) Rhombic sulphur is stable at room temperature. This is true.
      • At room temperature, the stable allotrope of sulphur is rhombic sulphur. Monoclinic sulphur is stable only at higher temperature, above the transition temperature.
      • (d) \(Cl_2\) is a colourless gas. This is false.
      • Chlorine is a:
        • greenish-yellow gas
        • with pungent smell
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This question comes from the chemistry of nitrogen, oxygen, sulphur, and halogens.
      • Important memory facts:
        • \(N_2\): colourless gas
        • \(NO_2\): brown gas
        • \(O_3\): less stable than \(O_2\)
        • Rhombic sulphur: stable at room temperature
        • \(Cl_2\): greenish-yellow gas
      • Ozone is thermodynamically less stable because its conversion to oxygen is accompanied by favorable energy change.
      • Sulphur shows allotropy, and the rhombic form is the stable one under ordinary conditions.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Only statement (c) matches the known standard property correctly.
      • Therefore the correct option is (c).
    • Why the other options are wrong
      • (a) is wrong because \(N_2\) is colourless.
      • (b) is wrong because ozone is less stable than oxygen.
      • (d) is wrong because chlorine is greenish-yellow, not colourless.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Use these quick associations:
        • brown gas \(\rightarrow NO_2\)
        • greenish-yellow gas \(\rightarrow Cl_2\)
        • room temperature stable sulphur \(\rightarrow\) rhombic sulphur
      • That eliminates three options almost instantly.
    • Final result
      • The correct statement is Rhombic sulphur is stable at room temperature.
      • Answer: (c)

      • The word pyro is a strong clue for condensation of two units through oxygen.

    • Explanation: Nitrogen (II) oxide means nitric oxide, \(NO\). Copper with dilute nitric acid gives \(NO\) as one of the reduction products of nitric acid. In contrast, copper with concentrated nitric acid usually gives \(NO_2\), and zinc with nitric acid can give other nitrogen oxides depending on conditions. Therefore the correct option is (a).
    • Important reaction set
      • The image lays out the comparison nicely and helps prevent confusion between dilute and concentrated nitric acid products.
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • The phrase nitrogen (II) oxide means the oxidation state of nitrogen is \(+2\).
      • The oxide of nitrogen with nitrogen in \(+2\) oxidation state is:
        • \(NO\) (nitric oxide)
      • Now look at the reactions of metals with nitric acid.
      • Copper with dilute nitric acid:
        • \(3Cu + 8HNO_3(dil.) \rightarrow 3Cu(NO_3)_2 + 2NO + 4H_2O\)
      • This directly gives \(NO\), so this matches the question.
      • Copper with concentrated nitric acid:
        • \(Cu + 4HNO_3(conc.) \rightarrow Cu(NO_3)_2 + 2NO_2 + 2H_2O\)
      • Here the product is \(NO_2\), not \(NO\).
      • Zinc behaves differently and can give products like \(N_2O\), \(NO\), or \(NO_2\) depending on conditions, but the standard reaction highlighted here for the asked product is copper with dilute nitric acid.
      • So the correct answer is (a).
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • Nitric acid is a strong oxidizing agent.
      • Its reduction product depends on:
        • the metal used
        • the concentration of nitric acid
        • reaction conditions
      • A very standard memory trend is:
        • Cu + dilute \(HNO_3 \rightarrow NO\)
        • Cu + concentrated \(HNO_3 \rightarrow NO_2\)
      • This is one of the most commonly asked reaction patterns in p-block and general inorganic chemistry.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Only option (a) is the standard reaction that gives nitric oxide \((NO)\), which is nitrogen (II) oxide.
      • Hence option (a) is correct.
    • Why the other options are wrong
      • (b) gives \(NO_2\), where nitrogen is in \(+4\) oxidation state.
      • (c) and (d) do not match the standard asked case for \(NO\) as shown in the given comparison set.
      • The main trap is confusing \(NO\) with \(NO_2\).
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Memorize this pair exactly:
        • Cu + dilute \(HNO_3 \rightarrow NO\)
        • Cu + concentrated \(HNO_3 \rightarrow NO_2\)
      • If the question says nitrogen (II) oxide, immediately translate it to \(NO\), then the answer becomes much easier.
    • Final result
      • The reaction giving nitrogen (II) oxide is Cu + dil. \(HNO_3\).
      • Answer: (a)

    • Correct answer: (a) It is also called phosphonic acid
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: Here \(Y\) is \(H_3PO_2\), which is hypophosphorous acid, not phosphonic acid. It is monobasic, it acts as a reducing agent, and the oxidation state of phosphorus in it is +1. So the incorrect statement is option (a).
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • White phosphorus reacts with alkali and water to give phosphine and sodium hypophosphite.
      • The reaction is:
        • \(P_4 + 3NaOH + 3H_2O \rightarrow 3NaH_2PO_2 + PH_3\)
      • So \(X = NaH_2PO_2\), which is sodium hypophosphite.
      • Now on treatment with aqueous HCl:
        • \(NaH_2PO_2 + HCl \rightarrow H_3PO_2 + NaCl\)
      • Therefore, \(Y = H_3PO_2\).
      • \(H_3PO_2\) is called hypophosphorous acid.
      • Its structure is effectively \(HOPH_2\), meaning only one hydrogen is attached to oxygen and is ionisable.
      • That is why it is a monobasic acid.
      • Also, because it contains \(P-H\) bonds, it behaves as a reducing agent.
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This question is based on oxoacids of phosphorus and their properties.
      • A very important rule for phosphorus oxyacids is:
        • Only the hydrogen attached to oxygen is acidic.
        • Hydrogen directly attached to phosphorus is not ionisable.
      • So:
        • \(H_3PO_2\) has one \(O-H\) bond \(\rightarrow\) monobasic
        • \(H_3PO_3\) has two \(O-H\) bonds \(\rightarrow\) dibasic
        • \(H_3PO_4\) has three \(O-H\) bonds \(\rightarrow\) tribasic
      • Another standard fact is that oxyacids containing \(P-H\) bonds act as reducing agents.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (a) is the incorrect statement because \(H_3PO_2\) is not phosphonic acid.
      • Its correct name is hypophosphorous acid.
      • So saying “It is also called phosphonic acid” is chemically wrong.
    • Why the other options are right
      • Option (b) is correct because \(H_3PO_2\) has only one ionisable hydrogen, so it is monobasic.
      • Option (c) is correct because the presence of \(P-H\) bonds makes it a reducing agent.
      • Option (d) is correct. Oxidation state of phosphorus in \(H_3PO_2\) is:
        • Let oxidation state of P be \(x\)
        • \(3(+1) + x + 2(-2) = 0\)
        • \(3 + x - 4 = 0\)
        • \(x = +1\)
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • For phosphorus oxyacids, do not count total hydrogens to decide basicity.
      • Count only the number of \(O-H\) bonds.
      • Also remember:
        • \(P-H\) bond present \(\rightarrow\) reducing property likely present
      • This one rule solves many direct NCERT-style MCQs.
    • Final result
      • \(Y = H_3PO_2\) = hypophosphorous acid
      • The incorrect statement about \(Y\) is (a).

    • Step-by-step explanation

    • Correct answer: (b) distorted octahedral, square pyramidal
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: \(XeF_4\) reacts with \(O_2F_2\) to form \(XeF_6\) as \(X\). Then \(XeF_6\) hydrolyses with one mole of water to form \(XeOF_4\) as \(Y\). The shape of \(XeF_6\) is distorted octahedral, and the shape of \(XeOF_4\) is square pyramidal.
    • Structures used to identify the shapes of \(X\) and \(Y\)
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • First identify \(X\).
      • \(XeF_4\) gets further fluorinated by \(O_2F_2\) to form \(XeF_6\).
      • So:
        • \(X = XeF_6\)
      • Now the next reaction says:
        • \(X + H_2O \rightarrow Y + 2HF\)
      • When \(XeF_6\) reacts with one mole of water, it gives \(XeOF_4\).
      • So:
        • \(Y = XeOF_4\)
      • Now find the shapes.
      • For \(XeF_6\):
        • xenon has 8 valence electrons
        • it forms 6 bonds with fluorine
        • so one lone pair remains on xenon
        • thus steric number = 7
      • Electron-pair geometry for steric number 7 is pentagonal bipyramidal, but due to lone pair arrangement and fluxional behaviour, the observed molecular shape is described here as distorted octahedral.
      • For \(XeOF_4\):
        • Xe is bonded to one O and four F atoms = 5 bond pairs
        • one lone pair remains on xenon
        • steric number = 6
      • A species with 5 bond pairs and 1 lone pair has octahedral electron-pair arrangement and square pyramidal molecular shape.
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This is an application of noble gas chemistry and VSEPR theory.
      • Important reactions to remember:
        • \(XeF_4 \xrightarrow{fluorination} XeF_6\)
        • \(XeF_6 + H_2O \rightarrow XeOF_4 + 2HF\)
      • Important shapes:
        • \(XeF_4\) = square planar
        • \(XeF_6\) = distorted octahedral
        • \(XeOF_4\) = square pyramidal
      • This is a favourite entrance-exam memory + shape-recognition combination.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (b) correctly identifies:
        • \(X = XeF_6\) with distorted octahedral shape
        • \(Y = XeOF_4\) with square pyramidal shape
    • Why other options are wrong
      • Option (a) is wrong because \(X\) is not square planar; square planar is the shape of \(XeF_4\), not \(XeF_6\).
      • Option (c) is wrong because neither of the listed shapes matches the actual geometry of \(XeF_6\) and \(XeOF_4\).
      • Option (d) is wrong because \(X\) is not square pyramidal and \(Y\) is not tetrahedral.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Memorise this xenon-series pattern:
        • \(XeF_2\) = linear
        • \(XeF_4\) = square planar
        • \(XeF_6\) = distorted octahedral
        • \(XeOF_4\) = square pyramidal
      • If this pattern is remembered, such questions become direct one-step MCQs.
    • Final result
      • \(X = XeF_6\), shape = distorted octahedral
      • \(Y = XeOF_4\), shape = square pyramidal
      • Hence, the correct answer is (b).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic
      • Chapter: p-Block Elements
      • Topic: Noble gas compounds
      • Subtopic: Reactions and shapes of xenon fluorides and oxyfluorides
    • Correct answer: (d) \(H_2S, H_2O\)
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: Among group 16 hydrides, water has the highest boiling point because of strong intermolecular hydrogen bonding. \(H_2S\) has the lowest boiling point because it has no hydrogen bonding and the smallest molecular mass among the heavier hydrides below water.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • The important hydrides of group 16 are:
        • \(H_2O\)
        • \(H_2S\)
        • \(H_2Se\)
        • \(H_2Te\)
      • Normally, boiling point increases down the group because molecular mass increases and van der Waals forces become stronger.
      • So if hydrogen bonding were absent, the order would generally rise from \(H_2S\) to \(H_2Te\).
      • But \(H_2O\) is an exception.
      • Water molecules form strong intermolecular hydrogen bonds.
      • Because of this, much more energy is needed to separate water molecules, so \(H_2O\) has an exceptionally high boiling point.
      • Among the remaining hydrides, \(H_2S\) has the smallest molar mass, so it has the weakest intermolecular attraction and the lowest boiling point.
      • Therefore:
        • \(X = H_2S\)
        • \(Y = H_2O\)
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • This question is based on boiling point trends in hydrides.
      • Boiling point usually depends on intermolecular forces.
      • For \(H_2S\), \(H_2Se\), and \(H_2Te\), the main force is van der Waals attraction, which increases with molecular size.
      • For water, hydrogen bonding dominates and makes its boiling point abnormally high.
      • That is why water breaks the normal trend.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Option (d) correctly identifies:
        • lowest boiling point \(= H_2S\)
        • highest boiling point \(= H_2O\)
    • Why other options are wrong:
      • \(H_2Te\) does not have the lowest boiling point because its large size increases intermolecular attraction.
      • \(H_2O\) cannot be the lowest because hydrogen bonding makes it the highest.
      • \(H_2Te\) is not the highest because water’s hydrogen bonding dominates over simple mass effect.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • In hydride boiling point questions, always check for hydrogen bonding exceptions first.
      • For group 16 hydrides, remember:
        • highest = \(H_2O\)
        • lowest = \(H_2S\)
    • Final result:
      • \(X = H_2S\) and \(Y = H_2O\).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic:
      • Chapter: The p-Block Elements
      • Topic: Hydrides of group 16
      • Subtopic: Boiling point trends and hydrogen bonding

    • Explanation: Sodium nitrite reacts with hydrochloric acid to form nitrous acid first, and nitrous acid is unstable. It decomposes to give a mixture of nitrogen oxides, namely nitric oxide \((NO)\) and nitrogen dioxide \((NO_2)\), along with water.

      • It undergoes decomposition and disproportionation reactions.

      • This is based on the behavior of nitrous acid.
      • Nitrites on treatment with acids form nitrous acid.

    • Correct answer: (c) The products of \(ICl\) and water are \(HI + HOCl\) is incorrect
      p-Block Elements (Groups 15 to 18) TTR
    • Explanation: \(ICl\) does react with water, but the products are not \(HI\) and \(HOCl\). Instead, the hydrolysis gives hypoiodous acid and hydrochloric acid. So statement (c) is the incorrect one.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • Let us check the statements one by one.
      • Statement (a): \(ICl\) is more reactive than \(I_2\).
        • This is correct.
        • Interhalogen compounds are generally more reactive than the parent halogens except fluorine because the bond between two different halogen atoms is weaker and more polar.
      • Statement (b): They are diamagnetic in nature.
        • This is correct in the usual exam context.
        • Most common interhalogen compounds have paired electrons.
      • Statement (c): Products of \(ICl\) and water are \(HI + HOCl\).
        • This is incorrect.
        • The hydrolysis reaction is:
          • \(ICl + H_2O \longrightarrow HOI + HCl\)
        • So the correct products are hypoiodous acid \((HOI)\) and hydrochloric acid \((HCl)\).
      • Statement (d): They act as fluorinating agents.
        • This is taken as correct for interhalogen compounds containing fluorine, which are commonly used as fluorinating agents.
      • Therefore the incorrect statement is option (c).
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • Interhalogen compounds are compounds formed between two different halogens.
      • Examples include:
        • \(ClF\)
        • \(BrF_3\)
        • \(IF_5\)
        • \(ICl\)
      • They are usually covalent, diamagnetic and more reactive than the corresponding halogens.
      • Hydrolysis reactions of interhalogens must be remembered carefully because product combinations are frequently tested.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Option (c) is wrong because it gives the wrong hydrolysis products.
      • \(ICl\) with water forms:
        • \(HOI\)
        • \(HCl\)
      • It does not form \(HI\) and \(HOCl\).
    • Why the other statements are right:
      • \(ICl\) is more reactive than \(I_2\).
      • Interhalogen compounds are diamagnetic in the standard entrance-exam treatment.
      • Fluorine-containing interhalogens act as fluorinating agents.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • For hydrolysis of \(ICl\), match each halogen with the acid it logically forms:
        • \(I \rightarrow HOI\)
        • \(Cl \rightarrow HCl\)
      • That immediately shows why \(HI + HOCl\) is incorrect.
    • Final result:
      • The incorrect statement is option (c).
