    • Correct answer: (d) \(H_2O > H_2Te > H_2Se > H_2S\)
    • Explanation: Down Group 16, the boiling point of hydrides generally increases with molecular mass because London dispersion forces become stronger. But water is an exception because it shows strong intermolecular hydrogen bonding, which makes its boiling point much higher than expected. Therefore, the correct order is \(H_2O > H_2Te > H_2Se > H_2S\).
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • First look at the normal down-the-group trend for hydrides of sulfur family:
        • As molecular size and mass increase, van der Waals forces increase.
        • So the boiling point should increase:
          • \(H_2S < H_2Se < H_2Te\)
      • Now consider water separately.
      • \(H_2O\) forms strong intermolecular hydrogen bonds because oxygen is highly electronegative and the \(O-H\) bond is strongly polar.
      • Hydrogen bonding is much stronger than the ordinary intermolecular attractions present in \(H_2S\), \(H_2Se\), and \(H_2Te\).
      • Therefore, even though \(H_2O\) is lighter, its boiling point becomes abnormally high.
      • Hence the final order is:
        • \(H_2O > H_2Te > H_2Se > H_2S\)
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • This question combines two ideas:
        • increase in boiling point with increasing molar mass down a group
        • anomalous behavior caused by hydrogen bonding
      • Normally heavier molecules boil at higher temperatures because intermolecular attractions become stronger.
      • But compounds capable of hydrogen bonding can have unexpectedly high boiling points.
      • Water is the classic example.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Option (d) is correct because among \(H_2S\), \(H_2Se\), and \(H_2Te\), boiling point rises down the group, while \(H_2O\) stays highest due to hydrogen bonding.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable:
      • Option (a) is wrong because the three heavier hydrides do not have equal boiling points.
      • Option (b) is wrong because it puts water as the lowest, which is opposite to the real trend.
      • Option (c) is wrong because among \(H_2S\), \(H_2Se\), and \(H_2Te\), boiling point increases downward, not decreases.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • For hydrides of Groups 15, 16, and 17, usually boiling point increases down the group.
      • But always check for the first member:
        • \(NH_3\), \(H_2O\), and \(HF\) show hydrogen bonding and often have abnormally high boiling points.
      • That shortcut immediately gives water as highest here.
    • Final result:
      • The correct order of boiling points is \(H_2O > H_2Te > H_2Se > H_2S\).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic:
      • Chapter: p-Block Elements
      • Topic: Group 16 hydrides
      • Subtopic: Boiling point trend and hydrogen bonding
    • Correct answer: (d) Carnallite
    • Explanation: Fluorspar, cryolite, and fluoroapatite are well-known minerals containing fluorine. Carnallite is not a mineral of fluorine; it is a hydrated double chloride of potassium and magnesium.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • Let us identify each mineral one by one.
      • Fluorspar:
        • Fluorspar is \(CaF_2\).
        • It is a common mineral of fluorine.
      • Cryolite:
        • Cryolite is \(Na_3AlF_6\).
        • It also contains fluorine and is an important fluorine mineral.
      • Fluoroapatite:
        • Fluoroapatite is approximately \(Ca_5(PO_4)_3F\) or written in another common form as \(3Ca_3(PO_4)_2 \cdot CaF_2\).
        • So it also belongs to fluorine minerals.
      • Carnallite:
        • Carnallite is \(KCl \cdot MgCl_2 \cdot 6H_2O\).
        • It is a hydrated double chloride mineral.
        • It contains no fluorine.
      • Therefore, the one that is not a fluorine mineral is Carnallite.
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • This is a direct mineral-identification question from inorganic chemistry.
      • Such questions are memory-based, but they become easy if you recall the formulae.
      • Anything containing \(F\) in its standard mineral formula can be identified as a fluorine mineral.
      • Carnallite is usually remembered in the context of potassium and magnesium salts, not fluorine.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Option (d) is correct because carnallite is \(KCl \cdot MgCl_2 \cdot 6H_2O\), a chloride mineral, not a fluoride mineral.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable:
      • Option (a) is wrong as a choice because fluorspar is \(CaF_2\), definitely a fluorine mineral.
      • Option (b) is wrong as a choice because cryolite is \(Na_3AlF_6\), also a fluorine mineral.
      • Option (c) is wrong as a choice because fluoroapatite contains fluorine in its structure.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • Memorize these three together:
        • Fluorspar \(\rightarrow CaF_2\)
        • Cryolite \(\rightarrow Na_3AlF_6\)
        • Fluoroapatite \(\rightarrow\) fluorine-containing phosphate mineral
      • And separately remember:
        • Carnallite \(\rightarrow KCl \cdot MgCl_2 \cdot 6H_2O\)
    • Final result:
      • Carnallite is not a mineral of fluorine.
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic:
      • Chapter: p-Block Elements
      • Topic: Group 17 elements
      • Subtopic: Occurrence and minerals of fluorine
    • Correct answer: (a) He
    • Explanation: Helium has an extremely small atomic size and very low mass compared to the other noble gases listed. Because of this, it can diffuse through tiny spaces in materials like silica glass.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • The question compares noble gases:
        • \(He\), \(Ar\), \(Kr\), \(Xe\)
      • Among these, helium is the smallest atom.
      • Diffusion through very fine pores or microscopic spaces becomes easier for smaller particles.
      • Silica glass appears solid, but at a microscopic level, very tiny species can pass through under suitable conditions.
      • Because helium atoms are exceptionally small, helium is able to diffuse through silica glass.
      • The other noble gases are significantly larger and therefore do not do so as readily.
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • This is based on the very small size and high diffusibility of helium.
      • Helium often behaves differently from heavier noble gases because:
        • it has the smallest size
        • it has the lowest mass
        • it diffuses very rapidly
      • This is why helium is also difficult to store completely without slow leakage from some containers.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Option (a) is correct because helium is the smallest noble gas and can diffuse through silica glass.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable:
      • Options (b), (c), and (d) are wrong because \(Ar\), \(Kr\), and \(Xe\) are much larger atoms and do not show this behavior like helium.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • Whenever a question asks about:
        • fastest diffusion
        • smallest noble gas
        • passing through fine pores or glass
      • think of helium first.
    • Final result:
      • The element that can diffuse through silica glass is helium.

    • Correct answer: (c) 2
    • Explanation: We must count only those products which are both gaseous and paramagnetic. So this is a two-step filter question: first identify the gaseous products, then check which of them have unpaired electrons.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • (A) Decomposition of ammonium nitrate:
        • \(\mathrm{NH_4NO_3 \xrightarrow{Heat} N_2O + 2H_2O}\)
        • The gaseous product of interest is \(\mathrm{N_2O}\).
        • \(\mathrm{N_2O}\) does not have any unpaired electron, so it is diamagnetic.
        • Therefore, reaction (A) contributes 0 paramagnetic gaseous products.
      • (B) Reaction of sodium nitrite with sulphuric acid:
        • \(\mathrm{2NaNO_2 + H_2SO_4 \rightarrow 2HNO_2 + Na_2SO_4}\)
        • No gaseous product is formed in the reaction as written in the given solution logic.
        • So reaction (B) contributes 0 paramagnetic gaseous products.
      • (C) Thermal decomposition of lead nitrate:
        • \(\mathrm{2Pb(NO_3)_2 \xrightarrow{673\ K} 2PbO + 4NO_2 + O_2}\)
        • Here the gaseous products are \(\mathrm{NO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{O_2}\).
        • \(\mathrm{O_2}\) is paramagnetic because it has two unpaired electrons.
        • \(\mathrm{NO_2}\) is also paramagnetic because it has one unpaired electron.
        • So reaction (C) contributes 2 paramagnetic gaseous products.
      • Total:
        • From (A): 0
        • From (B): 0
        • From (C): 2
        • Total \(= 2\)
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • Paramagnetic substances contain one or more unpaired electrons.
      • Diamagnetic substances have all electrons paired.
      • Very important examples:
        • \(\mathrm{O_2}\) is paramagnetic
        • \(\mathrm{NO_2}\) is paramagnetic
        • \(\mathrm{N_2}\), \(\mathrm{CO_2}\), \(\mathrm{N_2O}\) are diamagnetic in ordinary exam treatment
      • This question tests both inorganic reaction memory and molecular magnetic behaviour.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Only two gaseous products among all the reactions are paramagnetic: \(\mathrm{NO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{O_2}\).
      • Hence the answer is 2.
    • Why other options are wrong:
      • (a) 0 is wrong because \(\mathrm{NO_2}\) and \(\mathrm{O_2}\) are clearly paramagnetic.
      • (b) 1 is wrong because there are two such gases, not one.
      • (d) 3 is wrong because \(\mathrm{N_2O}\) is not paramagnetic and reaction (B) does not add another gaseous paramagnetic product here.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • Whenever you see \(\mathrm{O_2}\), remember instantly: paramagnetic.
      • Whenever you see \(\mathrm{NO_2}\), also remember: odd-electron species, so paramagnetic.
      • That makes reaction (C) enough to solve the question quickly.
    • Final result:
      • Total number of paramagnetic gaseous products formed in (A), (B), and (C) is 2.
      • Hence, the correct option is (c).

    • Correct answer: (c) \(P = \mathrm{SeCl_4}\), \(Q = \mathrm{Se}\)
    • Explanation: In a disproportionation reaction, the same element present in one oxidation state gets oxidised and reduced at the same time. So we first find the oxidation state of selenium in \(\mathrm{Se_2Cl_2}\), then look for one product where selenium has a higher oxidation state and another where selenium has a lower oxidation state.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • Find oxidation state of Se in \(\mathrm{Se_2Cl_2}\):
        • Let oxidation state of each Se be \(x\).
        • Then \(2x + 2(-1) = 0\)
        • \(2x - 2 = 0\)
        • \(2x = 2\)
        • \(x = +1\)
      • So selenium is in \(+1\) oxidation state in \(\mathrm{Se_2Cl_2}\).
      • In disproportionation, one part of Se must be oxidised to a higher oxidation state and another part must be reduced to a lower oxidation state.
      • Check the products in option (c):
        • In \(\mathrm{SeCl_4}\), Se is \(+4\)
        • In elemental \(\mathrm{Se}\), oxidation state is \(0\)
      • So from \(+1\):
        • \(+1 \rightarrow +4\) means oxidation
        • \(+1 \rightarrow 0\) means reduction
      • This perfectly matches disproportionation.
      • The balanced reaction is:
        • \(\mathrm{2Se_2Cl_2 \rightarrow SeCl_4 + 3Se}\)
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • Disproportionation means the same species acts as both reducing agent and oxidising agent.
      • One atom of the same element goes to a higher oxidation state, while another atom of that same element goes to a lower oxidation state.
      • This is why oxidation state analysis is the fastest method for solving such questions.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • \(\mathrm{Se_2Cl_2}\) has selenium in \(+1\) state.
      • \(\mathrm{SeCl_4}\) has selenium in \(+4\) state, showing oxidation.
      • \(\mathrm{Se}\) has selenium in \(0\) state, showing reduction.
      • Thus, \(\mathrm{SeCl_4}\) and \(\mathrm{Se}\) are the two main disproportionation products.
    • Why other options are wrong:
      • (a) \(\mathrm{SeCl_2}\) and \(\mathrm{SeCl_3}\) do not give the standard disproportionation pair expected here, and \(\mathrm{SeCl_3}\) is not the suitable stable product in this context.
      • (b) \(\mathrm{SeCl_4}\) and \(\mathrm{SeCl_2}\) do not represent oxidation and reduction from \(+1\) in the required way.
      • (d) \(\mathrm{Se_2}\) is not the proper reduced product expected here; elemental selenium is \(\mathrm{Se}\).
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • For disproportionation questions, do not start by balancing.
      • First find the oxidation state of the central element in the reactant.
      • Then look for one product with higher oxidation state and one with lower oxidation state.
      • That usually gives the answer in seconds.
    • Final result:
      • The main products are \(\mathrm{SeCl_4}\) and \(\mathrm{Se}\).
      • Hence, the correct option is (c).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic:
      • Chapter: Redox Reactions / p-Block Elements
      • Topic: Disproportionation reaction
      • Subtopic: Oxidation state method for identifying products
    • Correct answer: (c) HClO > HBrO > HIO
    • Explanation: For oxyacids of the same type, acidity depends mainly on how strongly the central atom pulls electron density away from the O-H bond. The more electronegative the central atom, the more polar the O-H bond becomes, and the more easily \(H^+\) is released. Since electronegativity decreases as \(Cl > Br > I\), the acidity order is \(HClO > HBrO > HIO\).
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • All three given acids are of the same general form:
        • \(\mathrm{HOX}\), where \(X = Cl, Br, I\)
      • When oxyacids have the same number of oxygen atoms, their acidity is compared by the electronegativity of the central atom.
      • The central atom pulls electron density through the oxygen atom.
      • If the central atom is more electronegative, it pulls electrons more strongly, which weakens the O-H bond.
      • A weaker O-H bond means hydrogen is released more easily as \(H^+\).
      • Now compare the halogens:
        • \(Cl\) is more electronegative than \(Br\)
        • \(Br\) is more electronegative than \(I\)
      • So the acidity follows:
        • \(\mathrm{HClO > HBrO > HIO}\)
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • For oxyacids, two major comparison rules are used:
        • If the central atom is the same, more oxygen atoms usually mean stronger acid.
        • If the number of oxygen atoms is the same, higher electronegativity of the central atom means stronger acid.
      • Here, all three acids have one oxygen atom, so the second rule applies.
      • This is an inductive effect based idea. A more electronegative atom pulls electron density away and helps the acid lose proton more easily.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Option (c) correctly follows the electronegativity order:
        • \(Cl > Br > I\)
      • Hence the acid strength is:
        • \(\mathrm{HClO > HBrO > HIO}\)
    • Why other options are wrong:
      • (a), (b), and (d) place \(HIO\) or \(HBrO\) above \(HClO\), which is incorrect because iodine is the least electronegative among the three and chlorine is the most electronegative.
      • So any order that does not keep \(HClO\) as the strongest and \(HIO\) as the weakest must be rejected.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • For acids like \(HOCl\), \(HOBr\), \(HOI\), remember:
        • same oxygen count \(\rightarrow\) compare electronegativity of the attached atom
      • Among halogens:
        • \(Cl > Br > I\)
      • So acidity also goes:
        • \(HOCl > HOBr > HOI\)
    • Final result:
      • The correct acidity order is \(\mathrm{HClO > HBrO > HIO}\).
      • Hence, the correct option is (c).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic:
      • Chapter: Chemical Bonding / Hydrogen and p-Block acid trends
      • Topic: Acidity of oxyacids
      • Subtopic: Effect of electronegativity on acid strength
    • Correct answer: (d) \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\)
    • Explanation: To identify the linear molecule, we use VSEPR theory. \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\) has five electron pairs around xenon, out of which three are lone pairs and two are bond pairs. This arrangement gives a linear molecular shape. The others are bent or angular.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • Let us inspect each option using shape and lone pair logic.
      • \(\mathrm{SnCl_2}\):
        • Tin has a lone pair along with two bond pairs.
        • So the shape is bent or angular, not linear.
      • \(\mathrm{PbCl_2}\):
        • Lead also has a lone pair effect in this molecule.
        • Hence it is bent or V-shaped, not linear.
      • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\):
        • Sulphur has one lone pair in the usual VSEPR picture.
        • Therefore \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) is bent, not linear.
      • \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\):
        • Xenon has 8 valence electrons.
        • It forms two bonds with two fluorine atoms.
        • That leaves three lone pairs on xenon.
        • So the steric number is 5, corresponding to trigonal bipyramidal electron pair arrangement.
        • In this arrangement, the three lone pairs occupy equatorial positions to minimize repulsion.
        • The two bond pairs occupy opposite axial positions.
        • Therefore the molecular shape becomes linear.
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • VSEPR theory says electron pairs arrange themselves to minimize repulsion.
      • There is a difference between:
        • electron pair geometry
        • molecular shape
      • For \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\):
        • \(AX_2E_3\)
        • electron pair geometry = trigonal bipyramidal
        • molecular shape = linear
      • This is a classic exam favorite among shapes of xenon compounds.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\) alone has an arrangement where lone pairs occupy equatorial positions and bonded atoms stay opposite to each other, giving a \(180^\circ\) bond angle.
      • That makes it linear.
    • Why other options are wrong:
      • \(\mathrm{SnCl_2}\) is bent due to a lone pair on Sn.
      • \(\mathrm{PbCl_2}\) is bent or V-shaped for the same general reason.
      • \(\mathrm{SO_2}\) is bent because sulphur has lone pair-bond pair repulsion.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • Memorize a few standard xenon compound shapes:
        • \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\) linear
        • \(\mathrm{XeF_4}\) square planar
        • \(\mathrm{XeF_6}\) distorted octahedral
      • If \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\) appears in options with common bent molecules, it is usually the linear one.
    • Final result:
      • The linear molecule is \(\mathrm{XeF_2}\).
      • Hence, the correct option is (d).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic:
      • Chapter: Chemical Bonding and Molecular Structure
      • Topic: VSEPR theory and molecular shapes
      • Subtopic: Shape of xenon fluorides and bent molecules
    • Correct answer: (a) dibasic, tribasic
    • Explanation: Phosphorous acid is \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) and phosphoric acid is \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\). Basicity depends on the number of ionisable hydrogen atoms attached through \(\mathrm{O-H}\) bonds, not simply on the total number of hydrogen atoms in the formula. In \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\), only two hydrogens are attached to oxygen, so it is dibasic. In \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\), all three hydrogens are attached to oxygen, so it is tribasic.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • First identify the acids:
        • Phosphorous acid \(= \mathrm{H_3PO_3}\)
        • Phosphoric acid \(= \mathrm{H_3PO_4}\)
      • Now recall the rule for basicity of oxyacids:
        • Only those hydrogens which are attached to oxygen as \(\mathrm{O-H}\) can be released as \(H^+\).
        • Hydrogen directly attached to phosphorus as \(\mathrm{P-H}\) is not ionisable.
      • For phosphorous acid, \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\):
        • Its structure is best written as \(\mathrm{HPO(OH)_2}\).
        • It has:
          • two \(\mathrm{O-H}\) hydrogens
          • one \(\mathrm{P-H}\) hydrogen
        • So only 2 hydrogens can ionise.
        • Hence phosphorous acid is dibasic.
      • For phosphoric acid, \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\):
        • Its structure is \(\mathrm{PO(OH)_3}\).
        • All three hydrogens are present as \(\mathrm{O-H}\) hydrogens.
        • So all three can ionise.
        • Hence phosphoric acid is tribasic.
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • For oxoacids, basicity is not decided by the total number of H atoms in the molecular formula.
      • It is decided by the number of replaceable or ionisable hydrogens.
      • Only hydrogen bonded to oxygen can generally be released as proton.
      • This is why:
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) is monobasic
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) is dibasic
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) is tribasic
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Option (a) correctly states:
        • phosphorous acid is dibasic
        • phosphoric acid is tribasic
    • Why other options are wrong:
      • (b) is wrong because phosphorous acid is not tribasic.
      • (c) reverses the basicities incorrectly.
      • (d) is wrong because phosphorous acid cannot be tetrabasic.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • Memorize this highly asked trio:
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_2}\) monobasic
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_3}\) dibasic
        • \(\mathrm{H_3PO_4}\) tribasic
      • The trick is to count \(\mathrm{O-H}\) bonds, not total H atoms.
    • Final result:
      • Phosphorous acid is dibasic and phosphoric acid is tribasic.
      • Hence, the correct option is (a).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic:
      • Chapter: p-Block Elements
      • Topic: Oxyacids of phosphorus
      • Subtopic: Basicity of phosphorous and phosphoric acids
    • Correct answer: (b) \(NO\)
    • Explanation:
      • This question is based on the brown ring test for nitrate ion. In that test, nitrate is reduced by \(Fe^{2+}\) in acidic medium to form nitric oxide, \(NO\), in situ. This \(NO\) then forms the brown ring complex with iron.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • The procedure described in the question is exactly the brown ring test for nitrate ion.
      • In this test:
        • freshly prepared \(FeSO_4\) provides \(Fe^{2+}\)
        • conc. \(H_2SO_4\) creates acidic medium
        • nitrate ion gets reduced
      • The nitrogen oxide formed first is:
        • \(NO\) (nitric oxide)
      • The reduction step can be written as:
        • \(2NO_3^- + 6H^+ + 6Fe^{2+} \rightarrow 2NO + 6Fe^{3+} + 4H_2O\)
      • This \(NO\) is formed in situ, meaning it is generated within the reaction mixture itself and is not added separately.
      • Then \(NO\) combines with the iron aqua complex to form the brown ring complex:
        • \(NO + (Fe(H_2O)_6)^{2+} \rightarrow (Fe(H_2O)_5(NO))^{2+} + H_2O\)
      • This brown-colored nitrosyl complex appears as a ring at the junction of the two liquids.
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • The brown ring test is a qualitative analysis test for nitrate ion.
      • The important chemistry behind it is:
        • \(NO_3^-\) is reduced to \(NO\)
        • \(Fe^{2+}\) is oxidized to \(Fe^{3+}\)
        • the formed \(NO\) coordinates with \(Fe^{2+}\) to give a nitrosyl complex
      • The complex responsible for the brown ring is:
        • \((Fe(H_2O)_5(NO))^{2+}\)
      • This is a very common memory-based inorganic chemistry question, and the trap is that students sometimes confuse nitrate itself with \(NO_2\), but the actual gas formed here is \(NO\).
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Option (b) is correct because \(NO\) is the nitrogen oxide produced in the reaction mixture during the brown ring test.
      • That \(NO\) then participates in complex formation.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable:
      • Option (a) \(NO_2\) is not the oxide formed in this test.
      • Option (c) \(N_2O\) is unrelated to the brown ring test chemistry.
      • Option (d) \(N_2O_3\) is also not formed here.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • Whenever you see:
        • nitrate ion + \(FeSO_4\) + conc. \(H_2SO_4\)
      • immediately think:
        • brown ring test
        • \(NO\) formed in situ
        • brown ring complex \((Fe(H_2O)_5(NO))^{2+}\)
      • A quick memory trick is: Nitrate gives NO in brown ring test.
    • Final result:
      • The nitrogen oxide formed in situ is \(NO\).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic:
      • Chapter: p-Block Elements
      • Topic: Nitrogen compounds
      • Subtopic: Brown ring test for nitrate ion
    • Correct answer: (a) \(Mn^{2+}\) only
    • Explanation:
      • When \(SO_2\) is treated with aqueous \(KMnO_4\), permanganate acts as an oxidizing agent and gets reduced from manganese oxidation state \(+7\) to \(+2\). At the same time, sulphur in \(SO_2\) is oxidized from \(+4\) to \(+6\).
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • First identify oxidation states:
        • in \(KMnO_4\), manganese is \(+7\)
        • in \(SO_2\), sulphur is \(+4\)
      • \(KMnO_4\) is a strong oxidizing agent, especially in acidic medium.
      • So manganese undergoes reduction:
        • \(Mn^{7+} \rightarrow Mn^{2+}\)
      • And sulphur undergoes oxidation:
        • \(S^{+4} \rightarrow S^{+6}\)
      • That means \(SO_2\) is oxidized to sulphate or sulphuric acid form.
      • The overall reaction shown in standard form is:
        • \(2KMnO_4 + 5SO_2 + 2H_2O \rightarrow K_2SO_4 + 2MnSO_4 + 2H_2SO_4\)
      • Here, manganese ends up as \(Mn^{2+}\) in \(MnSO_4\).
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • This is a redox reaction involving a strong oxidizing agent and a reducing agent.
      • \(KMnO_4\) behaves differently in different media:
        • in acidic medium: \(MnO_4^- \rightarrow Mn^{2+}\)
        • in neutral or weakly alkaline medium: \(MnO_4^- \rightarrow MnO_2\)
        • in strongly alkaline medium: \(MnO_4^- \rightarrow MnO_4^{2-}\)
      • Since the question refers to aqueous reaction with \(SO_2\), the standard expected exam result here is reduction to \(Mn^{2+}\).
      • This is one of the classic reactions used to show the reducing nature of sulphur dioxide.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Option (a) is correct because manganese goes from \(+7\) in permanganate to \(+2\) after oxidizing \(SO_2\).
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable:
      • Option (b) \(Mn^{4+}\) corresponds to \(MnO_2\), which is not the expected product here.
      • Option (c) \(Mn^{6+}\) corresponds to manganate, which forms in alkaline conditions, not in this standard reaction.
      • Option (d) is wrong because manganese does not reduce simultaneously to both \(+4\) and \(+6\) in this case.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • Memorize the three famous reductions of permanganate:
        • acidic medium \(\Rightarrow Mn^{2+}\)
        • neutral medium \(\Rightarrow MnO_2\)
        • alkaline medium \(\Rightarrow MnO_4^{2-}\)
      • Also remember: \(SO_2\) is a reducing agent and commonly decolorizes acidified \(KMnO_4\).
    • Final result:
      • On treating \(SO_2\) with aqueous \(KMnO_4\), manganese is reduced to \(Mn^{2+}\) only.

    • Correct answer: (b) A - III, B - IV, C - I, D - II
    • Explanation:
      • This is a direct colour-identification question from the halogen family. We just need to remember the characteristic colours of \(F_2\), \(Cl_2\), \(Br_2\), and \(I_2\). Once those are known, the matching becomes straightforward.
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • Let us write the standard colours of these halogens:
        • \(F_2\) is yellow
        • \(Cl_2\) is greenish yellow
        • \(Br_2\) is red or reddish brown in common description
        • \(I_2\) is violet
      • Now match them with Column-II:
        • \(F_2 \rightarrow\) Yellow \(\rightarrow\) (III)
        • \(Cl_2 \rightarrow\) Greenish yellow \(\rightarrow\) (IV)
        • \(Br_2 \rightarrow\) Red \(\rightarrow\) (I)
        • \(I_2 \rightarrow\) Violet \(\rightarrow\) (II)
      • So the correct matching is:
        • A - III
        • B - IV
        • C - I
        • D - II
      • That corresponds to option (b).
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • Halogens absorb light in the visible region, and because they absorb different amounts of energy, they appear in different colours.
      • As we move down the group from fluorine to iodine, the colour generally becomes deeper and darker.
      • This is related to changes in electronic transitions and molecular structure across the group.
      • For exam purposes, the most important point is to memorize the visible colours in order.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Option (b) exactly matches the standard halogen colours:
        • \(F_2 \rightarrow\) yellow
        • \(Cl_2 \rightarrow\) greenish yellow
        • \(Br_2 \rightarrow\) red
        • \(I_2 \rightarrow\) violet
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable:
      • The other options mix up the standard colours of halogens.
      • A very common trap is to confuse the colours of \(F_2\) and \(Cl_2\), because both are pale shades.
      • Another trap is to forget that iodine vapours are violet, which is a classic memory point in inorganic chemistry.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • Memorize the halogen colours in this order:
        • \(F_2\): yellow
        • \(Cl_2\): greenish yellow
        • \(Br_2\): red / reddish brown
        • \(I_2\): violet
      • A quick exam memory line is: Fluorine yellow, chlorine greenish yellow, bromine red, iodine violet.
    • Final result:
      • The correct match is A - III, B - IV, C - I, D - II.

    • Correct answer: (b) \(XeF_6 > XeF_4 > XeF_2\)
    • Explanation:
      • To act as both a fluorinating agent and an oxidising agent, the xenon fluoride should be able to accept electrons strongly and also supply fluorine effectively. Both these tendencies increase as the oxidation state of xenon increases. Since xenon is in \(+2\), \(+4\), and \(+6\) states in \(XeF_2\), \(XeF_4\), and \(XeF_6\) respectively, the overall order is \(XeF_6 > XeF_4 > XeF_2\).
    • Step-by-step explanation:
      • First write the oxidation state of xenon in each compound:
        • in \(XeF_2\), xenon is \(+2\)
        • in \(XeF_4\), xenon is \(+4\)
        • in \(XeF_6\), xenon is \(+6\)
      • A compound acts as an oxidising agent when it tends to get reduced, that is, when it has a tendency to accept electrons.
      • A compound acts as a fluorinating agent when it can transfer fluorine to another substance.
      • Now think chemically:
        • higher oxidation state means the central atom is more electron-deficient
        • so it has a stronger tendency to get reduced
        • and compounds like \(XeF_6\) can also provide fluorine more readily than the lower fluorides
      • Therefore, both oxidising power and fluorinating power increase in the order:
        • \(XeF_2 < XeF_4 < XeF_6\)
      • So the decreasing order asked in the question is:
        • \(XeF_6 > XeF_4 > XeF_2\)
    • Concept used / theory behind it:
      • Xenon forms fluorides because fluorine is highly electronegative and can stabilize xenon in positive oxidation states.
      • The common xenon fluorides are:
        • \(XeF_2\)
        • \(XeF_4\)
        • \(XeF_6\)
      • As the oxidation state of xenon increases from \(+2\) to \(+6\), the compound becomes more electron-poor.
      • That generally increases its tendency to behave as an oxidising agent.
      • Also, the higher fluoride contains more fluorine and shows stronger fluorinating behaviour.
      • This is a standard conceptual trend question from noble gas chemistry.
    • Why the correct option is right:
      • Option (b) is correct because \(XeF_6\) has xenon in the highest oxidation state and therefore shows the maximum tendency to act as both an oxidising and fluorinating agent.
      • \(XeF_4\) comes next, and \(XeF_2\) is the least among the three.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable:
      • Option (a) gives the exact reverse of the actual trend.
      • Option (c) incorrectly places \(XeF_4\) above \(XeF_6\).
      • Option (d) is wrong because \(XeF_6\) and \(XeF_4\) are not equal in these tendencies.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue:
      • Whenever xenon fluorides are compared for oxidising or fluorinating nature, think:
        • higher oxidation state of Xe \(\Rightarrow\) stronger oxidising tendency
        • more fluorine attached \(\Rightarrow\) stronger fluorinating tendency
      • So the quick order is:
        • \(XeF_6 > XeF_4 > XeF_2\)
    • Final result:
      • The correct decreasing order is \(XeF_6 > XeF_4 > XeF_2\).

    • Step-by-step explanation

        • \((N_2 + O_2 \rightleftharpoons 2NO)\)

      • Therefore, ordinary temperatures are not sufficient for appreciable reaction.
      • The reaction becomes important only at very high temperatures, roughly in the range:
        • \((1600{-}2200\,K)\)
      • Among the given options, the most appropriate value is:
        • \((2000\,K)\)
      • So option (b) is correct.
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • The formation of nitric oxide from nitrogen and oxygen is an endothermic process.
      • Endothermic reactions are favoured at higher temperatures.
      • In addition, the activation energy is very high because the nitrogen molecule is exceptionally stable.
      • That is why nitrogen and oxygen can coexist in air at room temperature without reacting significantly.
      • Only under high-energy conditions, such as electric sparks, lightning, or very high temperature, does \((NO)\) formation become appreciable.
    • Why the correct option is right

    • Step-by-step explanation
      • In assertion–reason questions, test both statements separately.
      • Assertion (A): “Both rhombic and monoclinic sulphur have \(S_8\) molecules.”
      • This is correct.
      • Both of these crystalline allotropes contain sulphur as cyclic \((S_8)\) molecules.
      • The difference between them is not the formula of the molecule, but the way these \((S_8)\) units are packed in the crystal lattice.
      • So Assertion is true.
      • Reason (R): “They have planar structure.”
      • This is false.
      • The \((S_8)\) ring is not planar like benzene.
      • Instead, it has a puckered ring or crown-shaped non-planar structure.
      • Therefore, Reason is false.
      • So the correct option is (c): Assertion is true but Reason is false.

      • Sulphur shows allotropy.
      • The two common crystalline allotropes are:
        • rhombic sulphur
        • monoclinic sulphur
      • Both are made of \((S_8)\) molecules.
      • They differ in crystal form and stability range:
        • rhombic sulphur is stable below \(96^\circ C\)
        • monoclinic sulphur is stable between \(96^\circ C\) and \(119^\circ C\)
      • The \((S_8)\) ring is folded, not flat.
      • This non-planar arrangement reduces strain and gives the characteristic crown shape.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (c) correctly represents the truth values:
        • Assertion = true
        • Reason = false
      • That is exactly the chemistry of rhombic and monoclinic sulphur.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable
      • (a) is wrong because the Reason is not true.
      • (b) is wrong for the same reason; it incorrectly treats the Reason as true.
      • (d) is wrong because the Assertion is not false; both allotropes do contain \((S_8)\) molecules.

      • Remember this pair:
        • rhombic sulphur = \(S_8\)
        • monoclinic sulphur = \(S_8\)
      • But do not confuse sulphur ring with a planar aromatic ring.
      • The standard description is:
        • \(S_8\) has a puckered crown-shaped ring
    • Final result
      • Assertion is true and Reason is false.
      • So, the correct answer is (c).

    • Correct answer: (a) (A) is true, (R) is true and (R) is the correct explanation for (A)
    • Explanation: Hydrogen fluoride has a much higher boiling point than other hydrogen halides because HF molecules are strongly associated through intermolecular hydrogen bonding. This strong attraction between molecules requires extra energy to separate them, so the boiling point becomes unusually high.
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • In assertion–reason questions, first test the Assertion and then the Reason.
      • Assertion (A): “Hydrogen fluoride has higher boiling point than other hydrogen halides.”
      • This is correct.
      • Among hydrogen halides \((HF, HCl, HBr, HI)\), HF shows an abnormally high boiling point.
      • Reason (R): “Hydrogen fluoride exhibits strong hydrogen bonding.”
      • This is also correct.
      • Fluorine is very small and highly electronegative, so the \((H-F)\) bond is highly polar.
      • As a result, neighbouring HF molecules attract each other strongly by hydrogen bonding.
      • This intermolecular association raises the boiling point.
      • Therefore, the Reason correctly explains the Assertion.
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • Normally, in the series of hydrogen halides, boiling point tends to increase with molecular mass because London dispersion forces become stronger:
        • \((HCl < HBr < HI)\)
      • But HF does not follow this simple trend because it shows hydrogen bonding.
      • Hydrogen bonding forms when hydrogen is attached to a very electronegative atom like \(F\), \(O\), or \(N\).
      • In HF, the polarity is so high that molecules associate strongly, often forming chains through hydrogen bonds.
      • Because of this strong intermolecular force, more heat is needed to convert liquid HF into vapour.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (a) is correct because both statements are true.
      • Also, the Reason is not just true independently; it directly explains why HF has a higher boiling point than the other hydrogen halides.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable
      • (b) is wrong because the Reason does explain the Assertion.
      • (c) is wrong because the Reason is not false.
      • (d) is wrong because the Assertion is not false; HF really does have the highest boiling point among hydrogen halides.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Whenever you see an abnormal physical property in a small molecule like HF, \(H_2O\), or \(NH_3\), immediately think of hydrogen bonding.
      • For hydrogen halides, remember:
        • \(HF\) is the exception because of hydrogen bonding
        • \(HCl, HBr, HI\) mainly follow mass-based intermolecular force trend
    • Final result
      • Assertion is true.
      • Reason is true.
      • Reason is the correct explanation of Assertion.
      • So, the correct answer is (a).

    • Correct answer: (b) pyramidal; square pyramidal
    • Explanation: In \(XeO_3\), xenon has three bond pairs and one lone pair, so the shape is trigonal pyramidal. In \(XeOF_4\), xenon has five bond pairs and one lone pair, giving an octahedral electron-pair arrangement and a square pyramidal molecular shape.
    • Shapes of \(XeO_3\) and \(XeOF_4\)
      • The image shows \(XeO_3\) as trigonal pyramidal and \(XeOF_4\) as square pyramidal.
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • This is a VSEPR-theory question. We identify the number of bond pairs and lone pairs on xenon in each molecule.
      • For \(XeO_3\):
        • Xenon is the central atom.
        • It is bonded to three oxygen atoms.
        • There is one lone pair on xenon.
        • So the total number of electron domains is 4.
        • Electron-pair geometry is tetrahedral.
        • With one lone pair and three bonded atoms, the molecular shape becomes trigonal pyramidal.
      • For \(XeOF_4\):
        • Xenon is bonded to one oxygen and four fluorine atoms.
        • There is one lone pair on xenon.
        • So there are 6 electron domains around xenon.
        • Electron-pair geometry is octahedral.
        • With one lone pair in an octahedral arrangement, the molecular shape becomes square pyramidal.
      • Hence, the structures are:
        • \(XeO_3\) = pyramidal
        • \(XeOF_4\) = square pyramidal
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • According to VSEPR theory, the shape of a molecule depends on repulsions between electron pairs around the central atom.
      • Lone pair–bond pair and lone pair–lone pair repulsions are stronger than bond pair–bond pair repulsions.
      • That is why the actual molecular shape is obtained after considering lone pairs properly.
      • For \((AX_3E)\)-type species, the shape is trigonal pyramidal.
      • For \((AX_5E)\)-type species, the shape is square pyramidal.
      • Here:
        • \(XeO_3\) behaves like \((AX_3E)\)
        • \(XeOF_4\) behaves like \((AX_5E)\)
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (b) correctly gives:
        • pyramidal for \(XeO_3\)
        • square pyramidal for \(XeOF_4\)
      • This matches the VSEPR-based analysis exactly.
    • Why other options are wrong or less suitable
      • (a) is wrong because \(XeO_3\) is not planar and \(XeOF_4\) is not trigonal bipyramidal.
      • (c) is wrong because \(XeO_3\) is not planar.
      • (d) is wrong because \(XeOF_4\) is not square planar; one atom remains above the square plane, giving square pyramidal shape.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • For xenon compounds, count total electron domains around xenon first.
      • Then remove the lone pair mentally to get the molecular shape.
      • Useful memory anchors:
        • \(XeO_3\) → trigonal pyramidal
        • \(XeOF_4\) → square pyramidal
    • Final result
      • The structures of \(XeO_3\) and \(XeOF_4\) are pyramidal and square pyramidal, respectively.
      • So, the correct answer is (b).

      • Chapter: p-Block Elements
      • Topic: Noble gas compounds
      • Subtopic: VSEPR shapes of xenon oxides and oxyfluorides

    • Correct answer: (b) \(8/3\).
    • Explanation: When chlorine reacts with excess ammonia, ammonia gets oxidised and nitrogen gas is formed. The balanced reaction is \(3Cl_2 + 8NH_3 \rightarrow 6NH_4Cl + N_2\). From this, 3 moles of chlorine react with 8 moles of ammonia, so 1 mole of chlorine reacts with \(8/3\) moles of \(NH_3\).
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • The important condition in the question is excess ammonia.
      • Under excess \(NH_3\), chlorine does not mainly form \(NCl_3\). Instead, ammonia reduces chlorine and itself gets oxidised to nitrogen.
      • The balanced reaction is:
        • \(3Cl_2 + 8NH_3 \rightarrow 6NH_4Cl + N_2\)
      • Now compare the mole ratio:
        • \(3\) moles of \(Cl_2\) react with \(8\) moles of \(NH_3\)
      • Therefore, \(1\) mole of \(Cl_2\) reacts with:
        • \(\frac{8}{3}\) moles of \(NH_3\)
      • So \(Z = \frac{8}{3}\).
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • This is a reaction of chlorine with ammonia where the product depends on which reagent is in excess.
      • With excess ammonia, nitrogen gas is formed.
      • With excess chlorine, nitrogen trichloride \((NCl_3)\) may form, which is dangerous and explosive.
      • So the phrase “excess ammonia” is the key clue in the question.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (b) is correct because it directly comes from the balanced stoichiometric ratio:
        • \(\frac{8\ mol\ NH_3}{3\ mol\ Cl_2}\)
      • Hence 1 mole of chlorine oxidises \(\frac{8}{3}\) moles of ammonia.
    • Why other options are wrong
      • (a) \(3/8\) is the inverse ratio.
      • (c) \(2/3\) does not match the balanced equation.
      • (d) \(3/2\) is also not obtained from the stoichiometric coefficients.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • Memorize this standard reaction:
        • \(3Cl_2 + 8NH_3 \rightarrow 6NH_4Cl + N_2\)
      • Always notice whether ammonia is excess or chlorine is excess, because the product changes.
      • For stoichiometric questions, write the balanced equation first. That prevents sign or ratio mistakes.
    • Final result
      • \(Z = \frac{8}{3}\).
      • So the correct option is (b).

      • Chapter: The \(p\)-Block Elements
      • Topic: Compounds and reactions of chlorine
      • Subtopic: Reaction of chlorine with ammonia
    • Correct answer: (a) Xe > Kr > Ar > Ne > He.
    • Explanation: Noble gases are monoatomic and interact mainly through weak van der Waals’ forces. These forces increase with atomic size and molar mass. Therefore, heavier noble gases require more energy to vaporise, so their enthalpy of vaporisation is higher.
    • Step-by-step explanation
      • Noble gases do not have strong intermolecular attractions like hydrogen bonding.
      • The main force between their atoms is London dispersion force, a type of van der Waals’ force.
      • As we move down the group:
        • atomic size increases
        • number of electrons increases
        • polarizability increases
      • Hence van der Waals’ forces become stronger from \(He\) to \(Xe\).
      • Stronger intermolecular attraction means more heat is needed to convert liquid into vapour.
      • So enthalpy of vaporisation increases in the order:
        • \(He < Ne < Ar < Kr < Xe\)
      • Therefore, written in decreasing order:
        • \(Xe > Kr > Ar > Ne > He\)
    • Concept used / theory behind it
      • Enthalpy of vaporisation depends on the strength of intermolecular forces.
      • For noble gases, stronger dispersion forces come from larger electron clouds.
      • So the trend roughly follows increasing atomic mass and increasing polarizability.
    • Why the correct option is right
      • Option (a) correctly places xenon at the top because it has the strongest intermolecular attractions among the listed gases.
      • Helium comes last because it is the smallest and least polarizable noble gas.
    • Why other options are wrong
      • (b) reverses the true trend.
      • (c) and (d) mix the order incorrectly and do not follow the increase in van der Waals’ forces down the group.
    • Exam tip / shortcut / elimination clue
      • For noble gases, remember:
        • larger atom \(\rightarrow\) stronger dispersion force \(\rightarrow\) higher boiling point and higher enthalpy of vaporisation
      • So the order usually follows:
        • \(Xe > Kr > Ar > Ne > He\)
    • Final result
      • The correct order is \(Xe > Kr > Ar > Ne > He\).
      • So the correct option is (a).
    • Chapter / topic / subtopic
      • Chapter: The \(p\)-Block Elements
      • Topic: Noble gases and physical properties
      • Subtopic: Trend in enthalpy of vaporisation
